601 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cathey v. Weissburd


No. B034510


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


202 Cal. App. 3d 982; 249 Cal. Rptr. 204; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 618



July 12, 1988 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is denied. This decision is final as to this court. ( Cal. Rules of Court, rule 24(a).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, candidates for municipal court judge, brought an application for writ of mandate, requesting that the court order respondent Registrar/Recorder of the County of Los Angeles) to save, count, report, and certify the results of a scheduled primary election for two municipal court judge positions. Respondent postponed the election for these positions until the general election pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 71180(b).


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, candidates for municipal court judge, filed nomination documents and paid fees to become candidates in the primary election for two positions that were vacated when the incumbents retired. Respondent Registrar/Recorder of Los Angeles County informed petitioners that because the offices were vacated between the last day to file nomination documents and the primary election date, the election for these offices was postponed until the general election under Cal. Gov't Code § 71180(b). Petitioners were also informed that their fees would not be refunded. Petitioners sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to save, count, report, and certify the results of the primary election. The court held that § 71180, was not unconstitutional on its face because it did not violate the constitution to treat superior and municipal courts differently. The court held that § 71180, was not unconstitutional as applied because the constitution did not require election by majority vote. The court noted that Cal. Elec. Code § 54, provided that a plurality of votes in any election was sufficient to choose the officeholder unless otherwise directed by the constitution.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for a writ of mandate or other extraordinary relief brought by petitioners, candidates for municipal court judge, after respondent Registrar/Recorder of Los Angeles County postponed the election the election for the two vacated municipal court judge positions until the next general election. The statute pursuant to which respondent postponed the election was not unconstitutional on its face or as applied.

 

602 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Wightman v. Franchise Tax Bd.


No. A031798


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


202 Cal. App. 3d 966; 249 Cal. Rptr. 207; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 637



July 12, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified July 25, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 777318, Roy L. Wonder, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment, as modified in accordance with this opinion, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged a decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which entered summary judgment in favor of respondents, the franchise tax board, the state, and the state controller. Appellants' action challenged the withholding of their income tax refunds by respondents pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 12419.5.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers challenged the withholding of their state income tax refunds by respondents, franchise tax board (FTB), the state, and state controller, pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 12419.5. Appellants' refunds were offset due to student loan default, failure to make child support payments, and due to the failure of a spouse to make child support payments pursuant to a child support judgment, respectively. Appellants sought summary judgment, but the trial court found for respondents, ruling that the income tax refund intercept program adequately protected the due process rights of appellants under the California and United States Constitutions. Appellants contended that the implementation of the tax intercept program lacked procedural safeguards, such as notice and the right to be heard, among others. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, finding that the intercept program as applied to child support obligation debts did not violate appellants' due process rights. The court, however, held that for non-child support debts, due process required that taxpayers be provided with notice and the opportunity for review of his or her objection to an offset.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that denied appellant taxpayers' request for summary judgment, as modified. The court held that the intercept program as applied to child support obligation debts did not violate appellants' due process rights. The court, however, held that for nonchild support debts, due process required that taxpayers be provided with notice and opportunity to review their objection to an offset.

 

603 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



McMartin v. County of L.A.


Nos. B026091, B026238


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


202 Cal. App. 3d 848; 249 Cal. Rptr. 53; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 598



July 6, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied October 12, 1988. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C618406 and C602058, Warren H. Deering, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order as to Chad and Wendi is modified to provide they may litigate those parts of their causes of action against defendants which accrued within 100 days of the filing of their claims with defendants. In all other respects, the orders appealed from are affirmed. Chad and Wendi to recover costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their petitions for relief under the Tort Claims Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 815 et seq., for failing to file their claims within 100 days after the alleged wrongful conduct.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were prosecuted for child molestation. The criminal charges were dismissed and appellants filed claims with respondent county alleging injury arising from the prosecution. The trial court dismissed the parts of the claims that fell outside the 100 day time frame under Cal. Gov't Code § 945.4, which governed suits for money damages against respondent. Appellants challenged the judgment of the trial court. The court found that § 945.4 as amended was constitutional because the government had a legitimate interest in getting notice of potential civil claims in a timely manner. The court further found that appellants' claims were partially barred because the fact that a criminal defendant contested their prosecution did not give respondent knowledge of their claims and the related circumstances. The court found that appellants were required to substantially comply with the requirements of § 945.4, and their failure to do so without presenting competent evidence of any excuse for the failure to timely file the claim barred the action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellants' claims against respondent county, except for those parts of the claims that accrued within 100 days of appellants' filing their claims because the government had an interest in investigating potential claims against it, even if the claim could not yet be pursued and the mere contest of a criminal prosecution was not sufficient to put respondent on notice of the existence of a civil claim.

 

604 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Wilson


No. A039793


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


202 Cal. App. 3d 661; 249 Cal. Rptr. 36; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 602



June 30, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 29, 1988. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 112722, Alfred G. Chiantelli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prison officials sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, (California) which granted respondent inmate's petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Appellants alleged that the trial court erred when it found they had acted arbitrarily and capriciously in classifying respondent as a sex related crime offender.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prison officials classified respondent inmate as a sex-related crime offender within the prison population after his conviction for rape was overturned and then dismissed. Respondent successfully sought habeas corpus relief and the trial court ordered that this classification be removed. Appellants sought review of the trial court's order. . On appeal, the court reversed, finding that the trial court erred in concluding that appellants' actions were arbitrary and capricious. The court noted that the question presented was whether this internal classification was properly imposed based upon the rape charge, which were found true by a jury but was subsequently dismissed. The court concluded that because the record included more than a modicum of evidence establishing that the classification decision had some basis in fact, appellants' actions should have been sustained according to the "some evidence" standard.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order because there was more than a modicum of evidence to support appellant prison officials' decision, based upon the crime charged and found true by the jury. The trial court abused its discretion when it applied the wrong reviewing standard and found appellants' action to be arbitrary and capricious.

 

605 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. McNulty


No. E003990


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


202 Cal. App. 3d 624; 249 Cal. Rptr. 22; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 585



June 29, 1988 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II, III, V and VI.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 15, 1988, and Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 29, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. ICR 9873, James H. Herrin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in Superior Court of Riverside County (California) of three counts of pimping, Cal. Penal Code § 266h, three counts of pandering, Cal. Penal Code § 266i, one count of conspiracy to commit pimping, and one count of conspiracy to commit pandering, Cal. Penal Code § 182. The court sentenced defendant to prison for a total of eight years, the maximum allowable term, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted and sentenced to eight years in prison for three counts of pimping, Cal. Penal Code § 266h, three counts of pandering, Cal. Penal Code § 266i, one count of conspiracy to commit pimping, and one count of conspiracy to commit pandering, Cal. Penal Code § 182, and appealed his sentence as constituting constitute cruel and unusual punishment. The court of appeal affirmed. Cal. Penal Code § 1203.065(a)'s absolute ban on probation and sections 266h's and 266i's three-year minimum prison terms for pimps and panderers were not cruel or unusual. The total sentence of eight years for convictions on eight separate charges was not so disproportionate to the crimes for which it was inflicted that it shocked the conscience or offended fundamental notions of human dignity. The trial court was not required to instruct that pimping was a specific intent crime. In regard to the first basic way to violate the statute, deriving support with knowledge that the other person was a prostitute was all that was required for violating the section. No specific intent was required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence. The absolute ban on probation and three-year minimum prison terms for pimps and panderers were not cruel or unusual. The trial court was not required to instruct that pimping was a specific intent crime with regard to the first basic way to violate the pimping statute.

 

606 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Timothy R.


No. H003177


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


202 Cal. App. 3d 593; 248 Cal. Rptr. 721; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 592



June 27, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 94008, Mark E. Thomas, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The order adjudicating the minor a ward of the juvenile court and subsequent orders are reversed because he was convicted of violating an unconstitutionally vague municipal code section.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant minor was convicted of trespassing. The Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) determined appellant to be a ward, fined him, ordered four days' confinement, ordered him not to loiter at the shopping center in question, and put him on probation for 30 days. Appellant raised constitutional challenges to San Jose, Cal., Municipal Code § 10.20.140.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a minor, was convicted under San Jose, Cal., Municipal Code § 10.20.140 of trespassing after being arrested at a shopping mall. The court of appeal reversed the trial court's orders because appellant was convicted of violating an unconstitutionally vague municipal code section. It was a basic principle of due process that an enactment was void for vagueness if its prohibitions were not clearly defined. Further, even a clear and precise enactment could nevertheless be overbroad if in its reach it prohibited constitutionally protected conduct. The court could attempt to preserve an enactment's constitutionality by a reasonable narrowing construction, but if elimination of objectionable parts of a statute required a wholesale rewriting, a court's attempt to do so transgressed both the legislative intent and the judicial function. Judicial construction could not avoid the unconstitutional vagueness in the combination of San Jose, Cal., Municipal Code § 10.20.140, subsection (c) and subsection (d)(5), which exempted from the status of trespassers those who were on another's premises under claim or color of legal right.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order adjudicating appellant, a minor, a ward of the juvenile court and subsequent orders, because he was convicted of violating an unconstitutionally vague San Jose Municipal Code section.

 

607 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Smith v. Bd. of Medical Quality Assurance


No. A038881


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


202 Cal. App. 3d 316; 248 Cal. Rptr. 704; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 578



June 22, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 15, 1988, and Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 15, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 869804, Lucy Kelly McCabe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant physician challenged a judgment of the Superior Court, San Francisco County (California) that entered judgment for appellee board of medical quality assurance in appellant's suit challenging the constitutionality of appellee's reasonable cause proceedings pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2292(d).


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2292(d) (§2292(d)), appellee board of medical quality assurance prepared a petition requesting that appellant, a licensed physician, be compelled to take a professional competency examination because there was reasonable cause to believe appellant was unable to practice medicine with reasonable skill and safety to patients. Appellee board approved the petition without granting appellant's request for a hearing and ordered appellant to undergo an examination. Appellant filed suit, seeking declaratory and injunctive relief, a writ of mandate, and attorney fees. The trial court denied the requested relief and entered judgment for appellee board. On appeal, the court affirmed. Appellee board's procedure did not deny appellant equal protection because, inter alia, § 2292(d) did not adopt any classifications. Moreover, neither the federal nor state due process clause required the imposition of the full range of due process rights at the reasonable cause proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of appellee board of medical quality assurance in appellant physician's suit challenging the constitutionality of appellee's reasonable cause proceedings; appellee's procedure did not deny appellant equal protection or due process.

 

608 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



City & County of San Francisco v. Patterson


No. A039002


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


202 Cal. App. 3d 95; 248 Cal. Rptr. 290; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 557



June 15, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 1, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 874043, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ordinance proponent sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which issued a peremptory writ that mandated that the proposition supported by appellant be removed from the ballot because the restrictions within the proposition could not be imposed on respondent city and school district by the electorate.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ordinance proponent supported an initiative ordinance that limited the ability of respondent city and school district to lease and sell property. Respondents filed a petition for writ of mandate directing the registrar to remove the proposition from the ballot as being beyond the scope of the initiative power of the people. The trial court accepted respondents' argument and ordered the peremptory writ. Appellant contended that the trial court erred in conducting a pre-election review of the validity of the proposed ordinance because an initiative measure may be deleted only where there is clear invalidity. The court rejected this claim and found that pre-election review was appropriate because the electorate did not have the power to adopt the proposal. The court held that the ordinance could not be applied to respondent school district because it was preempted by the education code and was beyond the power of the people to enact. It found that any restriction on the board of supervisor's charter-based authority to sell or lease city property must be accomplished by charter amendment and not by initiative ordinance. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that issued a writ mandating that the proposition supported by appellant ordinance proponent be removed from the ballot. The court held that the proposed measure was beyond the power of the people to enact. It could not be applied to respondent school district because it was preempted and could not be applied to respondent city because restrictions on the board's charter based power must be by charter amendment.

 

609 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Amarel v. Connell


No. C000557


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


202 Cal. App. 3d 137; 248 Cal. Rptr. 276; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 543; 1988-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P68,129



June 15, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 323029, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff independent rice growers appealed an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which dismissed their complaint against defendant agricultural cooperatives. The complaint alleged restraint of trade in violation of the Cartwright Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 16700 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs alleged that defendants violated a state statute by conspiring in restraint of trade. They contended the trial court erred in sustaining defendants' demurrer. The trial court determined the causes of action asserted under state law for anticompetitive practices impermissibly intruded upon the federal domain since the alleged unlawful practices involved trade with a foreign nation. The court reversed the trial court's order of dismissal. The court held that federal law did not preempt plaintiffs' state law causes of action so long as the anticompetitive conduct in question had a direct, substantial, and reasonably foreseeable effect within the state.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order dismissing the complaint was reversed because federal law did not preempt plaintiffs' state law causes of action so long as the anticompetitive conduct alleged had a direct, substantial, and reasonably foreseeable effect within the state.

 

610 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Smith v. Regents of University of California


No. A020553.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One.


226 Cal. App. 3d 1098; 248 Cal. Rptr. 263; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 555



June 14, 1988.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted September 29, 1988 (S006588) (See 2 Cal. App. 4th 750)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. 519977-2 and 533997-6, Charles Zook F. Sutton, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff students challenged the decision of the Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One (California), which denied their constitutional challenges to defendant state university's collection and use of a mandatory student activities fee.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff students filed a lawsuit which challenged defendant state university's collection and use of a mandatory student activities fee to fund certain activities of a student body organization. The trial court granted a partial summary adjudication in favor of defendant state university, finding that the mandatory student activities fee was constitutional. Furthermore, the trial court determined that the use of the fees was not invalid. Plaintiffs appealed. On review, the court found that defendant state university was a constitutional entity whose powers derived from the state constitution and not from the legislature. Thus, it had the authority to assess a mandatory student activities fee as a condition of enrollment. Furthermore, plaintiffs could be compelled to provide financial support to the student organization because plaintiffs were not obliged to associate with anyone; they were merely required to pay the activities fees. Therefore, the court found that the compelled financial support of the student organization and its funded activities was constitutionally permissible. The trial court's judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision that denied plaintiff students' constitutional challenges to defendant state university's collection and use of a mandatory student activities fee was affirmed. The court held that defendant state university obtained its power from the state constitution and thus had the authority to institute the fees.

 

611 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Patterson v. Bd. of Supervisors


Nos. A038352, A039856


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


202 Cal. App. 3d 22; 248 Cal. Rptr. 253; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 553



June 14, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of All Appellants for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 14, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 873695, 880904, Lucy Kelly McCabe and Daniel M. Hanlon, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgments, and each of them, are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two identical cases, appellant citizens sought review of judgments from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), granting peremptory writs that mandated deletion of certain offending material from voter's pamphlets in actions brought by respondent city attorney, acting on behalf of the registrar of voters, to strike the offending material as false, misleading, or inconsistent with legal requirements.


OVERVIEW: Appellant citizens sought to have certain material included in voter's pamphlets, but the lower court ordered deletion of the material. On appeal, appellants claimed that the statutes that provided the authority for the deletion of the material, Cal. Elec. Code §§ 3795, 5025, violated U.S. Const. amend. I. The challenged statutes, which were referenced in the city charter, authorized review and amendment or deletion of material for voter's pamphlets that was false, misleading, or inconsistent with the requirements of the statutes. The reviewing court found that the challenged restrictions were constitutional, reasoning that the restrictions were content-neutral and carefully tailored to promote the purpose of providing the voters information concerning the measures on the ballot. Moreover, the challenged provisions neither limited nor curtailed the ability of individuals or groups to assemble, speak, distribute literature, or lobby public opinion. Nor did the city's charter that authorized the pamphlets require inclusion of all arguments in the pamphlets. The court concluded that the restrictions had been applied properly and affirmed the judgments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the lower court that granted peremptory writs that mandated the deletion of certain offending material from voter's pamphlets. The court found that the statutory restrictions limiting the content permitted in voter's pamphlets were constitutional and that the lower court had properly applied the restrictions in ordering certain material deleted.

 

612 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Jordan v. Long Beach Community Hospital


No. B014458, No. B016989.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four.


201 Cal. App. 3d 1402; 248 Cal. Rptr. 651; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 549



June 10, 1988 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellants' petitions for review DENIED September 15, 1988 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SOC 71241, Robert C. Nye, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant patient and wife sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in a medical malpractice action against respondent hospital and doctors and appellant doctor. The trial court reduced appellant patient and wife's total collective award of noneconomic damages to $ 250,000. Appellant doctor sought review of the trial court's apportionment order. The appeals were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: Appellant patient's medical malpractice action arose from the removal of appellant patient's healthy left kidney rather than his cancerous right kidney. Appellant wife joined her loss of consortium cause of action with her husband's medical malpractice cause of action. Appellant patient and wife argued that the trial court committed reversible error in excluding expert testimony that appellant doctor's conduct constituted a conscious disregard of appellant patient's safety and that Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2 permitted medical malpractice plaintiffs who join their independent causes of action in a single suit to recover up to $ 250,000 each in noneconomic damages. The court held that the trial court abused its discretion in refusing to allow appellant patient's medical experts to testify to the ultimate fact whether appellant doctor acted in conscious disregard. The court also held that appellant patient and wife should receive the same recovery as if they had brought separate actions rather than joining in one action. The court ordered a new trial on the issue of punitive damages and reversed the trial court's order reducing appellant patient and wife's noneconomic damage award.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the matter to the trial court. The trial court was directed to modify the judgment to award appellant patient $ 250,000 in noneconomic damages, to award appellant wife $ 250,000 in noneconomic damages, and to conduct a new trial on whether appellant patient and wife were entitled to punitive damages. The court held that appellant patient and wife should receive the same recovery as if they had brought separate actions.

 

613 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Frank O.


No. B025913.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven.


201 Cal. App. 3d 1041; 247 Cal. Rptr. 655; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 517



June 3, 1988 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Respondent's petition for review DENIED September 1, 1988 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. J262195, Victor T. Barrera, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his motion to suppress evidence of possession of burglar tools as the fruit of an illegal arrest in his trial for possession of burglar tools and violation of the local curfew ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a juvenile whom officers saw standing at an intersection at 2:30 a.m. Immediately upon seeing defendant, the officers asked defendant to approach the car because he was young and on the street unattended past the curfew time. An officer testified at trial that he did not know why defendant was out at that time so he took him into custody per the local curfew law. The officer in no way indicated that he suspected that defendant had committed a crime. Defendant appealed the court's failure to suppress evidence collected from him as a result of his illegal arrest. The court found that the curfew ordinance was subject to multiple interpretations; therefore, it was void for vagueness because it failed to give the persons subject to it fair notice of what conduct was forbidden and it allowed arbitrary and erratic arrests and convictions. Since defendant was illegally arrested without probable cause, the trial court should have granted defendant's motion to suppress burglary tools found in his possession because they were the fruit of an illegal arrest. The court struck down the ordinance and defendant's arrest; therefore, the judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court because the curfew ordinance under which defendant was arrested was void for vagueness and the arresting officers had no probable cause for arresting defendant; therefore, the evidence of burglar tools should have been suppressed as the fruit of an illegal arrest.

 

614 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Sinclair & Valentine Co. v. County of L.A.


No. B021961


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


201 Cal. App. 3d 1021; 247 Cal. Rptr. 568; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 518



June 3, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 43551, Ralph A. Biggerstaff, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it awards attorney's fees to plaintiff; in all other respects it is affirmed. The County is entitled to costs on appeal. Plaintiff Sinclair & Valentine and defendants Landau and Alexander shall bear their own costs and shall contribute equally to those incurred by the County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county challenged that portion of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, (California), which required it to pay plaintiff company's attorneys' fees in the amount of $ 30,000 pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. Plaintiff asserted the trial court abused its discretion by halving its attorney's fee award and disallowing its costs.


OVERVIEW: Defendant county did not contest the propriety of the trial court's judgment insofar as it restored plaintiff's title to real property, but rather challenged that portion which required appellant to pay plaintiff's attorneys' fees of $ 30,000, pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. On the other hand, plaintiff asserted the trial court abused its discretion by arbitrarily halving its requested attorneys' fee award and disallowing its out-of-pocket costs. The court reversed the trial court's judgment insofar as it awarded attorneys' fees to plaintiff. The court affirmed the judgment in all other respects and awarded costs on appeal to defendant. The court found that plaintiff had not demonstrated that it was entitled to attorneys' fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. The court noted that plaintiff had neither alleged in its complaint nor demonstrated at the hearing on its summary judgment motion that defendant deprived it of rights protected by the constitution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment insofar as it awarded attorneys' fees to plaintiff company. The court affirmed the judgment in all other respects and awarded costs on appeal to defendant county.

 

615 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (elder)


No. F009407


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


201 Cal. App. 3d 1061; 247 Cal. Rptr. 647; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 516



June 3, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Real Parties in Interest for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 15, 1988. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.



DISPOSITION:  Accordingly, we hold the relevant portion of section 597b refers to presence as a spectator for the purpose of watching the animal fighting and is constitutional as construed. The petition for a peremptory writ is granted. Respondent court is directed to vacate its order of September 16, 1987, granting the real parties' petition for writ of mandate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which granted a writ of mandate and held that the word "spectator" as used in Cal. Penal Code § 597(b), was unconstitutionally vague and did not give spectators who were present at a cockfight notice as to the criminality of their conduct.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state convicted parties who were present at a cockfight pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 597(b). The parties filed a petition for writ of review on the grounds that the statute unconstitutionally criminalized their acts of merely being present at the scene of a crime. The lower court granted the petition and held that the word "spectator" as used in Cal. Penal Code § 597(b) was unconstitutionally vague and violated due process because it did not give the alleged violators notice as to what conduct was criminal. Thus, such vague terms would have subjected a mere passerby to criminal prosecution. Appellant sought review of the order. On review the court disagreed and ordered the lower court to vacate the order granting the writ of mandate. The court held that Cal. Penal Code § 597(b), criminalized the knowing presence of a "spectator" who was present for the purpose of watching the animal fight and not those parties who merely caught a glimpse. Further, the statute criminalized the act of being a knowing spectator of the illegal sport of cockfighting because such activity could not have taken place without spectators. Thus, the statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court directed the lower court to vacate its order granting a writ of mandate addressing the unconstitutionality of the word "spectator" as used in statute that prohibited cockfighting. The court held that the trial court erred in determining that the statute was unconstitutionally vague because the statute applied only to those spectators who were knowingly present at the cockfight.

 

616 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Supple v. City of L.A.


No. B029166


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


201 Cal. App. 3d 1004; 247 Cal. Rptr. 554; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 511



June 2, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 394532, Daniel Leibl, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The purported appeal is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment on a final arbitration award, as well as a prior interlocutory order sustaining a demurrer to two of his five causes of action without leave to amend, in the suit plaintiff filed against defendant city under the federal Civil Rights Acts, 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 1983, 1985, and for negligence and negligent employment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff filed suit against defendant city for an incident in which he was allegedly beaten by police officers. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer to plaintiff's causes of action under the federal Civil Rights Acts, 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 1983 and 1985, and dismissed them without leave to amend. The parties went to arbitration on plaintiff's claims of negligence and negligent employment, and the trial court entered the arbitration award, which became final. No party filed a request for de novo trial. Plaintiff filed a notice of appeal, alleging that the trial court's dismissal of his civil rights claims was error. The court dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, holding that the interlocutory order of which plaintiff complained had been superseded by the final arbitration award. The court held that because plaintiff did not request either an extraordinary writ or a de novo trial, the arbitration award was not appealable, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1141.23 and Cal. R. Ct. 1615(c).


OUTCOME: The court dismissed plaintiff's appeal for lack of jurisdiction, because the final arbitration award superseded the prior interlocutory order dismissing two of plaintiff's causes of action under federal Civil Rights Acts, the final arbitration award was not appealable, and plaintiff did not request a de novo trial.

 

617 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Bovard v. Am. Horse Enters.


No. C000134


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


201 Cal. App. 3d 832; 247 Cal. Rptr. 340; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 492



May 27, 1988 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part V.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 283030 and 28031, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment dismissing the supplemental complaint, the order striking the memorandum of costs and taxing costs, and the judgment dismissing the cross-complaint are affirmed. The parties are to bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant seller challenged the order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that dismissed his supplemental complaint against appellees, a corporation and individual buyer, in an action by appellant to recover on promissory notes executed in connection with the purchase of appellee corporation. Appellee individual cross-appealed from the trial court's postjudgment order striking his memorandum of costs and taxing costs.


OVERVIEW: Appellant seller brought an action against appellees, a corporation and individual buyer, to recover on promissory notes executed by appellees in connection with the purchase of appellee corporation. The trial court dismissed appellant's supplemental complaint, holding that the contract upon which his action was founded was illegal and void as contrary to public policy. The court found that the corporation predominantly produced drug paraphernalia used to smoke marijuana and was not engaged significantly in jewelry production. The trial court also struck appellee individual's memorandum of costs and taxing costs. Appellant challenged the judgment and appellee individual cross-appealed. After review, this court held that the trial court correctly declared the contract contrary to the policy of express law and was therefore, illegal and void. Consistent with this decision, the appellee individual was not entitled to attorney fees. The trial court's judgment dismissing the supplemental complaint, the order striking the memorandum of costs and taxing costs, and the judgment dismissing the cross-complaint were affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that dismissed appellant seller's complaint and the order striking appellee individual buyer's memorandum of costs and taxing costs were affirmed because the contract was illegal and void.

California Trout, Inc. v. State Water Resources Control Bd.

619 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hawkins


No. A035669, No. A036752


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One.


201 Cal. App. 3d 502; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 484



May 20, 1988 


NOTICE:   [*1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Granted June 17, 1988. Opinion filed March 23, 1989 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 31269, Norman Spellberg, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants' husband and wife sought to reverse the judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Coasta County (California), which convicted them of dependent adult abuse under Cal. Penal Code § 368(a). The two appeals were consolidated for purposes of hearing and review.


OVERVIEW: Appellants' husband and wife challenged their convictions for dependent adult abuse under Cal. Penal Code § 368(a). At trial, it was shown that appellants lived with appellant husband's mother, that appellant took care of his mother's finances, and that she died due to extreme neglect. Appellants claimed § 368(a) was void for vagueness. Because a statute must give "fair warning" and "minimal guidelines" as to what individuals and what conduct was covered by the statute, the court held the statute was unconstitutionally vague. It was not clear whether it applied only to caregivers, or included bystanders who learned of the abuse. Because the statute required "criminal negligence," this implied a duty of care which § 368(a)(1) did not expressly create. The court affirmed appellant husband's conviction because the statute clearly applied to him. The court held that simply living in the same household was not enough to establish that appellant wife had a duty of care to the victim or the requisite criminal intent. Because appellant wife might have been convicted under the portion of the statute that had been declared void and not as a caregiver, the court reversed her conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of appellant husband but reversed appellant wife's conviction. The dependent adult abuse statute was void for vagueness as to appellant wife because it was not clear that under the circumstances the statute applied to her, or her conduct, or that she had the requisite criminal intent. The statute was not void as to appellant husband because the statute clearly applied to him as the caregiver of the victim.

 

620 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City of Sacramento v. State of California


No. C002265.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


214 Cal. App. 3d 1184; 247 Cal. Rptr. 109; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 454



May 18, 1988 


NOTICE:  [**1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for review GRANTED August 18, 1988 (S006188) (See 51 Cal. 3d 51)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 331607, Darrel W. Lewis, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of May 18, 1988, previously reported at 201 Cal. App. 3d 409.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), that denied its petition for mandate to require appellee state to reimburse appellant for costs incurred in implementing 1978 Cal. Stat. 2, which required public employees to be covered under unemployment insurance.


OVERVIEW: In prior litigation between appellant city and appellee state, the court held that appellant was entitled to reimbursement under Cal. Const. art. XIII B, § 6 for its costs incurred in implementing 1978 Cal. Stat. 2, which required public employees to be covered by unemployment insurance. When reimbursement was not made, appellant filed a petition for mandate. While the petition was pending, the supreme court ruled that the proper analysis for reimbursement under § 6 was not whether the law was state-mandated, which was the focus in the prior litigation. Rather, the analysis was whether the law imposed a new program or higher level of service. The trial court denied appellant's petition. The court reversed, holding that collateral estoppel precluded appellee from relitigating the issue of reimbursement. In so ruling, the court held that the issue of reimbursement was decided even though appellee had not argued that 1978 Cal. Stat. 2 did not represent a new program or a higher level of service. The court held that reimbursement was likewise required under the new analysis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order denying appellant city's petition for mandate requiring appellee state to reimburse appellant for costs incurred in implementing a state-mandated law. A prior judgment collaterally estopped appellee from re-litigating the issue of reimbursement, despite the fact that after the prior judgment, the supreme court issued a new analysis for determining entitlement to reimbursement.

 

621 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Salazar v. Honig


No. B026629


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six.


200 Cal. App. 3d 1576; 246 Cal. Rptr. 837; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 424



May 10, 1988 


NOTICE:   [**1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for review DENIED September 1, 1988 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).
Arguelles, J., is of the opinion the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 87506, Joe D. Hadden, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which denied appellants' motion for summary judgment in their action against respondents, the California Department of Education and associated institutions, seeking injunctive relief and writ of mandamus.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers brought an action seeking injunctive relief and writ of mandamus against defendants, the California Department of Education and associated institutions. The action alleged that defendants unlawfully implemented Cal. Educ. Code § 39807.5 by charging students fees for the use of a school bus, which appellants claimed violated the free school guaranty under Cal. Const. art. IX, § 5 and the equal protection guaranty under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a). Appellants sought summary judgment. Respondents claimed that appellants had no remedy because neither the California Constitution nor § 39807.5 revealed any specific powers or duties authorizing defendants to prohibit local school districts from assessing bus fees. The trial court denied appellants' motion. The court reversed and vacated the trial court's judgment. The court held that the imposition of student transportation fees violated the free school guaranty of the Constitution. The court further held that § 39807.5 violated the equal protection guaranty of the Constitution, and remanded the case to the trial court with directions to issue an injunction and a writ of mandate consistent with the court's decision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and vacated the trial court's denial of summary judgment to appellant taxpayers because the imposition of student transportation fees violated the free school guaranty of the California Constitution, and because the applicable statute violated the equal protection guaranty of the Constitution. The court remanded the case to the trial court with directions to issue an injunction and a writ of mandate.

 

622 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People ex rel. Van De Kamp v. Cappuccio


No. F009724


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


204 Cal. App. 3d 750; 251 Cal. Rptr. 657; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 961



May 10, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 77347, Edmund J. Leach, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondent to have costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which imposed penalties against defendants under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17200 for failure to report the correct weight of the squid that defendants purchased.


OVERVIEW: Defendants purchased squid from certain fishing boats and were charged under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17200 with unfair competition based upon their failure to correctly weigh and report the amount of the squid. Defendants claimed that their method of weighing the squid conformed to industry practice. The trial court ruled against defendants and imposed penalties upon them. On appeal, the court agreed with the trial court's ruling. The court held that defendants' intent was irrelevant because their conduct was harmful to the public, and that industry custom was no defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

623 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Youngblood v. Gates


No. B002438


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


200 Cal. App. 3d 1302; 246 Cal. Rptr. 775; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 403



May 4, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Defendants and Appellants for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied August 18, 1988. Arguelles, J., did not Participate therein. Lucas, C. J., and Panelli, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 202343 and C 344693, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, chief of police and city, challenged an order of the Los Angeles County Superior Court (California), which found the county's policies of arraignment within 48 hours of arrest under Cal. Penal Code § 825 and denial of certain privileges to be impermissible.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, an indigent arrestee and individual taxpayers, filed a class action suit, alleging unnecessary delay in bringing detainees before the magistrate and improper jail conditions against appellants, chief of police and city. Appellants challenged an adverse judgment. The appellate court affirmed judgment against appellants and held that it was unnecessary delay if detainees were not brought before a magistrate within two days, not 48 hours, of their arrest, that arrestee's' right to prompt arraignment outweighed the burden placed on appellants, and that appellants' inadequate financial resources was not an excuse for depriving detainees of their constitutional rights. Further, the court found that the trial court's order was proper in directing that arrestee's were entitled to sanitary conditions, family visits, reading and recreation materials, and medical and psychological treatment during detention.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment against appellants, chief of police and city, because arraignment was required to be within two days, not 48 hours, and, while there were no statutory requirements as to jail conditions, they should be consistent with those applicable to prisons.

 

624 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Copren v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. C002272


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


200 Cal. App. 3d 828; 246 Cal. Rptr. 361; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 373



April 26, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 20, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sierra County, No. 3954, Stanley C. Young, Jr., Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant organization sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Sierra County (California), which found Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 214.12 unconstitutional in a case to determine the tax-exempt status of real property.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the lower court judgment because it found that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §214.12 was unconstitutional. The court found that §214.12 conflicted with Cal. Const. amend XIII, §6, because it attempted to provide retroactive relief for those seeking a welfare exemption for property taxes. Cal. Const. amend. XIII, § 6 provided that the failure in any year to claim an exemption or classification that would reduce property tax would be deemed a waiver of the exemption or classification for that year. The court found that appellant organization had failed to claim an exemption and failed to record its interest in the property with the assessor as required under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 261(a). Appellant's failure to record their interest in the property and the failure to claim an exemption resulted in a waiver of the welfare exemption for the years prior to the recordation. The court concluded that the lower court correctly concluded that § 214.12 conflicted with Cal. Const. amend. XIII, § 6, and that appellant was not entitled to a welfare exemption for the years that they had failed to properly record their interest in the property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court judgment because it found that that the statute that attempted to provide retroactive relief to appellant organization was unconstitutional. The court held that a statute could not supersede the state constitutional provision that stated that a claimant waived a welfare exemption if it failed to claim an exemption for a specific year.

 

625 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Reed v. Dick


No. A029828.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One.


200 Cal. App. 3d 469; 246 Cal. Rptr. 131; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 337



April 18, 1988 


NOTICE:   [**1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for review DENIED July 21, 1988 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opinion in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).
Mosk, J., is of the opinion the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 102473, Peter A. Smith, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Marin County, (California) granting judgment in plaintiffs' favor in plaintiffs' action founded on California's Invasion of Privacy Act, Cal. Penal Code § 632, purporting to proscribe eavesdropping, interception, and recording by electronic devices of confidential telephone communications, and fixing civil and criminal penalties therefor.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were found civilly and criminally liable under California's Invasion of Privacy Act (Act), Cal. Penal Code § 632, for recording telephone communications. The court of appeals reversed. Defendants were parties to the conversations they recorded. No right of privacy existed as against a party to a telephone communication. The purpose of the Act as applied to defendants failed because no right of privacy existed and there was no "eavesdropping." The Act was applicable to persons not a party to a communication or to those acting without the consent of such a party.


OUTCOME: The order granting plaintiffs judgment was reversed because the state statute proscribing eavesdropping, interception, and recording by electronic devices of confidential telephone communications did not apply to a party to the conversation.

 

626 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (clements)


No. A039899


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


200 Cal. App. 3d 491; 246 Cal. Rptr. 122; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 364



April 18, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 17, 1988. Poche, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted. The Petition of Real Parties in Interest Piet, Cascio, Clements and Calish for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied July 28, 1988. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue requiring respondent court to set aside its orders denying petitioner's petitions in that court and to enter new orders in conformity with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state filed a petition for writ of mandate challenging respondent court's release of assets subject to forfeiture pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11470 et seq. to enable defendants to secure private counsel.


OVERVIEW: Respondent court released assets subject to forfeiture pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11470 et seq. to enable defendants to secure private counsel. The court of appeals granted the state's petition for writ of mandate challenging such orders. The definition of property subject to forfeiture made no mention of attorney's fees, nor did the provision for third-party claims of exemption. Additionally, the forfeiture of money necessary for attorney's fees did not unconstitutionally deny defendants their right to counsel.


OUTCOME: The writ of mandate was granted and respondent court's orders releasing assets subject to forfeiture to enable defendants to secure private counsel were quashed because the forfeiture statute authorized forfeiture of even that property necessary for the retention of private counsel and such retention did not violate defendants' right to counsel.

 

627 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Lexington Hills Ass'n v. Cal.


Nos. H000420, H000962, H001172


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


200 Cal. App. 3d 415; 246 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 332



April 15, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 9, 1988, and Appellants' Petition in No. H000420 for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied June 29, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Nos. 535438, 575614 and 572429, David W. Leahy, Charles Gordon, and John A. Flaherty, Judges.



DISPOSITION: In Santa Clara County Superior Court action 572429, Lexington Hills Association et al. v. Soho et al. (our proceeding H001172), the appeal is dismissed as moot. The trial court is directed to dissolve any existing injunction against logging activities which were the subject of timber harvesting plan 5-83-48 SCL and of this action. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeals were taken in two environmental cases concerning timber harvesting from orders of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that denied relief to opposers of the logging plan in one action and vacated the issuance of an encroachment permit to the loggers in another action.


OVERVIEW: Two lawsuits were filed regarding approval of a timber harvesting plan. Objections were made to encroachment permits issued by the California Department of Transportation (Caltrans). In one suit, the court affirmed the judgment entered in favor of the viability of the logging. The court held that the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA), Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 4511, established adequate notice and hearing requirements, providing procedural due process. In the other suit, the court reversed the order vacating the permits and requiring Caltrans to hold a hearing in compliance with CEQA. The court held CEQA was not applicable. The court held that the issuance of encroachment permits to the loggers did not constitute a project under CEQA and did not constitute approval by Caltrans of the activities. The court also held that Caltrans was not obligated to prepare an environmental impact report or a negative declaration.


OUTCOME: In one action, the court affirmed the judgment in favor of the timber harvesting plan because the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA) contained adequate notice and hearing requirements. In the other action, the court reversed the order vacating the issuance of the encroachment permits because it was not necessary to comply with CEQA, as it was inapplicable to the issuance of the permits.

 

628 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Laupheimer v. Cal.


No. H000445


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


200 Cal. App. 3d 440; 246 Cal. Rptr. 82; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 329



April 15, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 9, 1988, and the Petition of Plaintiffs and Appellants for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied June 29, 1988. Panelli, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 89254, Roland K. Hall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Insofar as it denies relief with respect to Timber Harvesting Plan #5-84-5 SCR the judgment is affirmed. With respect to Timber Harvesting Plan #5-84-28 SCR the judgment is reversed with directions to enter a new judgment which grants a peremptory writ of mandate ordering the California Department of Forestry to set aside its approval of said Plan #5-84-28 SCR and to disapprove the said plan. Upon duly noticed motion of any of appellants Ronald Laupheimer, Ronald Highsmith, and Lompico Creek Homeowners Association made within 30 days after issuance of the remittitur herein, the trial court shall determine whether and (if so) in what amount the State of California should be required to pay attorney fees to these appellants, or any of them, under Code of Civil Procedure section 1021.5, and for that purpose shall take evidence if necessary, hear argument, and render [***2] a written decision. Subject to the trial court's determination with respect to attorney fees under section 1021.5, each party shall bear his or its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant area homeowners sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California) denying them relief and ruling in favor of appellee California Department of Forestry in a writ of mandate action for injunctive relief to prevent logging.


OVERVIEW: Appellant area homeowners filed a petition for a writ of mandate and injunctive relief to prevent appellee California Department of Forestry from approving a landowner's plans #5-84-28 and #5-84-5 to cut and remove trees from the owner's property in Santa Cruz. The trial court denied appellants relief. On review, appellants joined by intervenors Santa Cruz County contended that approval of the plan was invalid because the Z'berg-Nejedly Forest Practice Act of 1973, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 4511 et seq. and Cal. Code Regs. Tit. 14, § 911 et seq. violated the California Environmental Quality Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq. and the federal and California constitutions. The court held appellee's approval of the plans for the property was a prejudicial abuse of discretion. However, the act and rules established procedures that were adequate, as a matter of procedural due process and did not deny equal protection to the classes of which appellants were members. The record compelled a conclusion that appellee did not proceed as required by law. The trial court should have concluded that appellee's approval of plan #5-84-28 was a prejudicial abuse of its discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment insofar as it denied relief with respect to plan #5-84-5 SCR. It ruled in favor of appellant homeowners by reversing with directions to enter a new judgment granting a peremptory writ of mandate ordering appellee California Department of Forestry to set aside its approval of said Plan #5-84-28 SCR and to disapprove the said plan. The record compelled a conclusion that appellee did not proceed as required by law.

 

629 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. V C. Van Pool Bail Bonds


No. B020150


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


200 Cal. App. 3d 303; 246 Cal. Rptr. 79; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 342



April 14, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A032219, Sheila F. Pokras, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bail bondsman sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), setting aside a forfeiture of bail upon the payment of costs associated with appellant's client's failure to appear for trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bail bondsman furnished a cash bond to secure the appearance at trial of its client, a criminal defendant. When the client did not appear, the trial court revoked bail. Within 180 days, appellant produced its client and moved to set aside the forfeiture under Cal. Penal Code § 1305(a). The trial court agreed to set aside the forfeiture, but required appellant to pay costs associated with its client's failure to appear. The court affirmed the trial court's order. In so ruling, the court held that it was proper for the trial court to impose such costs. Moreover, the court rejected appellant's argument that the costs were twice what appellant was permitted by law to charge in fees. The court observed that appellant was permitted to charge all reasonable costs and expenses incurred by a breach by an arrestee and may recoup these costs from the collateral posted by the arrestee. The court also chose not to reach appellant's argument that the imposed costs violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, finding no evidence that courts imposed costs on bail bondsmen more often than on individuals who posted their own bail.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order setting aside a forfeiture of bail because the trial court did not abuse its discretion in conditioning relief on payment of costs to the public proximately caused by appellant's client's failure to appear for trial.

 

630 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Rodriguez v. Superior Court


No. F008873


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


199 Cal. App. 3d 1453; 245 Cal. Rptr. 617; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 309



April 5, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 3, 1988.



DISPOSITION: The petition for a writ of mandate or prohibition is denied and the order staying further proceedings is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought a writ of prohibition or mandate restraining respondent Superior Court of Tulare County (California) from taking any further action in the pending criminal case against them. Petitioners also sought to vacate respondent's order denying their motion to suppress evidence pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538.


OVERVIEW: After respondent superior court denied petitioners' motion to suppress evidence seized in connection with drug-related charges, petitioners argued that the time limitation of Cal. Penal Code § 1510 did not bar pretrial review and that the evidence should be suppressed because petitioners had a legitimate expectation of privacy in the premises searched, because the warrant lacked probable cause, and because the nighttime execution of the warrant violated Cal. Penal Code § 1533. The court determined that, although the motion to suppress had not been adjudicated within 60 days, § 1510 did not bar pretrial review because the motion had been made within that time. Real party in interest, the state, failed to raise the issue of whether petitioners lacked a reasonable expectation of privacy at the hearing and were precluded from raising the issue on appeal. Although the affidavit supporting the search warrant lacked probable cause, the evidence was admissible under the good faith exception. Finally, although the nighttime search violated Cal. Penal Code § 1533, the evidence was still admissible because the search was otherwise reasonable under U.S. Const. amend. IV.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for writ of prohibition or mandate, holding that the evidence could be admitted because the warrant to search the premises lacked probable cause but fell within the good faith exception and because the nighttime search was not unconstitutional.

 

631 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hutchins


No. D004485


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


199 Cal. App. 3d 1219; 245 Cal. Rptr. 541; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 283



March 29, 1988 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976.1 and 976(b), this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts I, II, III, V, VI and VII.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR64766, David M. Gill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction affirmed; case remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), entered upon a jury verdict, convicting him of burglary in the first degree and other offenses, following the trial court's comment during jury instructions that the location of the alleged offenses was in an inhabited apartment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with first-degree burglary, robbery, assault with a deadly weapon, forcible oral copulation, and forcible rape of a woman who was attacked in her apartment. While instructing the jury on first-degree burglary, the trial court stated that burglary of an inhabited dwelling house, such as that occupied by the victim in the case, would be burglary in the first degree. Defendant was subsequently convicted of all charges and he appealed. In affirming, the court ruled that the comments of the trial court did not amount to a directed verdict of first-degree burglary, as opposed to second-degree burglary, because defendant never disputed the location of the offenses or the fact that the victim had been attacked and the jury was properly instructed otherwise. If there was any error it was harmless because there was no evidence to support a finding that the apartment was uninhabited, so as to reduce the offense to second-degree burglary. Moreover, the taking of the issue of degree from the jury was not reversible error because it did not involve an element of the underlying crime of burglary. The remaining convictions were affirmed by an unpublished opinion.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed because, inter alia, the trial court's comment did not constitute a directed verdict as to first-degree burglary since defendant never disputed the location, the jury was otherwise properly instructed, the issue of degree was not an element of the underlying offense, and there was no evidence to support a finding that the burglarized premises were uninhabited, so as to reduce the offense to second-degree burglary.

 

632 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cal. State Employees' Ass'n v. Cal.


No. C000698


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


199 Cal. App. 3d 840; 245 Cal. Rptr. 232; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 245



March 22, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 336815, Fred W. Marler, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying the petition for writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state employees' association sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied its petition for writ of mandate seeking to have respondent state ordered to refrain from entering into personal service contracts pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 19130.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state employees' association sought review of the trial court's judgment, which refused to enjoin respondent state from entering into personal service contracts under Cal. Gov't Code § 19130 on the basis that it was unconstitutional on its face. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court because § 19130(a) did not facially conflict with Cal. Const. art. VII or its purposes. The court distinguished the legislature's authority under the state constitution from that of the U.S. constitution and further noted that the restriction on "contracting out" of state activities to the private sector did not arise from the express language of art. VII. While conflict might arise in the application of § 19130(a), appellant had the burden of demonstrating it inevitably posed a present total and fatal conflict with constitutional prohibitions. After in-depth review of § 19130, the court held that it was carefully crafted to permit personal service contracts to achieve cost savings only when they would have had no detrimental effect on the integrity of the civil service and, therefore, did not displace or destroy the constitutional scheme.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment denying appellant state employees' association a writ of mandamus against respondent state because the statute that endorsed personal service contracts was not unconstitutional on its face.
