634 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Menefee & Son v. Dep't of Food & Agric.


No. C000765


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


199 Cal. App. 3d 774; 245 Cal. Rptr. 166; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 227



March 18, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 341855, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter judgment in favor of plaintiffs in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lettuce growers sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied plaintiffs' petition for a writ of mandate and injunctive relief against defendant Department of Food and Agriculture for seizure of plaintiffs' crops under Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 12648. Plaintiffs contended that the crop seizure violated plaintiffs' due process rights and hence was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff lettuce growers filed an action against defendant Department of Food and Agriculture for an injunction and writ of mandate concerning defendant's seizure of plaintiffs' crops pursuant to Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 12648. The trial court denied both the injunction and the writ of mandate. On appeal, plaintiffs contended that Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 12648 was unconstitutional. The court agreed, finding that plaintiffs had an interest protected by procedural due process under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7 and that the statute did not meet the requirements of due process for several reasons. First, the statute allowed the seizure of property without any notice or opportunity to be heard and without any requirement that authorization be obtained from a neutral judicial officer. Second, the statute failed to provide for a hearing on the merits of the taking, either before or after the taking. Third, the statute required plaintiffs to file an action instead of defendant. Therefore, defendant never had to carry the burden of proving facts upon which it relied to seize plaintiffs' crops. Accordingly, the court reversed and remanded with directions to enter judgment for plaintiffs.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that denied plaintiff lettuce growers' petition for a writ of mandate and declaratory relief against defendant Department of Food and Agriculture for the seizure of plaintiffs' crops. The court found that the statutory authority under which defendant seized plaintiffs' crops was unconstitutional because the statute deprived plaintiffs of due process.

 

635 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Mahon v. Safeco Title Ins. Co.


No. C000313


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


199 Cal. App. 3d 616; 245 Cal. Rptr. 103; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 209



March 11, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 300771, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee challenged a ruling of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted appellee employer summary judgment on appellant's wrongful termination claim.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee was fired after she refused to take a transfer as requested by appellee employer. Appellant applied for unemployment compensation benefits but was deemed ineligible when the Department of Employment Development found appellant left work voluntarily without good cause. An administrative law judge agreed with that determination and deemed appellant ineligible to receive benefits. Appellant brought a wrongful discharge action against appellee and appellee was granted summary judgment by the trial court, which held that the administrative decision should be given collateral estoppel effect on the issue of whether appellant quit work without good cause. Appellant challenged that ruling, and while the appeal was pending the legislature passed Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1960, which indicated that such administrative adjudications should not be given collateral estoppel effect. On this basis, the court reversed. The court found that the text and context of § 1960 implied that it would be applied to all pending cases. The statutory section, which the court found to be a rule of procedure, was therefore applied to the current action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court ruling, which granted appellee employer summary judgment on appellant employee's wrongful termination claim. The court held that a recently passed statute, which prevented the application of collateral estoppel to certain administrative decisions, applied to all pending cases including the current action and, therefore, summary judgment in favor of appellee should not have been granted.

 

636 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Conservatorship of Delay


No. D005376


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


199 Cal. App. 3d 1031; 245 Cal. Rptr. 216; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 257



March 11, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. MH57447, Kenneth A. Johns, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant conservatee challenged the facial constitutionality of the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 5361, 5365.1 (1979), and sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), that granted respondent county's petition to reestablish the conservatorship of appellant and reappoint the conservator pursuant to § 5361.


OVERVIEW: After the automatic one-year expiration of the conservatorship of appellant conservatee for being gravely disabled as the result of a mental disorder under the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5361 (1979), the trial court granted respondent county's petition to reestablish the conservatorship of appellant and reappoint the conservator. Appellant sought review and argued that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 5361 and 5365.1 (1979) violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The appellate court found that the general reference to physicians in § 5361 did not rise to the level of a constitutional deficiency in the context of the requirements to present the petition for the reappointment. Further, there was nothing in § 5365.1 that compelled appellant to waive the presence of the physicians at the reappointment hearing. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment because the statute on its face did not violate appellant's due process rights and issues as to the qualifications of the physicians and whether they personally examined appellant were matters for the trier of fact when evaluating whether the requisite showing of grave disability had been made.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the trial court to reestablish the conservatorship of appellant conservatee because the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act did not violate appellant's due process rights and the qualifications of the evaluating physicians and whether they personally examined appellant were matters for the trier of fact when it determined whether the requisite showing of grave disability had been made.

 

637 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Jones


No. B026530


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


199 Cal. App. 3d 543; 245 Cal. Rptr. 85; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 207



March 10, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A034674, Norman W. Gordon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment convicting defendant of arson of an inhabited structure (Pen. Code, § 451, subd. (b)) is modified so as to convict him of arson of a structure (Pen. Code, § 451, subd. (c)) and is affirmed as so modified. The cause is remanded to the trial court for resentencing under Penal Code section 451, subdivision (c).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of setting fire to an inhabited structure in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 451(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of setting fire to an inhabited structure, the most serious form of arson, in violation of Cal. Penal Code, § 451(b). The house he set fire to was the one he and others had been evicted from the day before. Defendant challenged his conviction, contending that, at the time of the fire, the house was not inhabited as defined in Cal. Penal Code § 450(d). On appeal, the court ruled that inhabitation was an element of the crime that the prosecution had to prove beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that there was insufficient evidence to prove that the house was actually being used for dwelling purposes at the time of the fire. Thus, the court modified the judgment so as to convict defendant of the reduced charge of arson of a structure in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 451(c).


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's judgment and convicted defendant of arson of a structure because the fact that defendant and others were seen leaving the house in the morning was insufficient to support an inference that any of them spent the night there; and even if defendant did spend the night in the house, setting fire to the house contravened an intent to use it for dwelling purposes. The cause was remanded for resentencing.

 

638 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Finnie v. Town of Tiburon


No. A038052


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


199 Cal. App. 3d 1; 244 Cal. Rptr. 581; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 159



February 29, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied June 2, 1988. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, Nos. A132452, A038052, E. Warren McGuire, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The appeal from the denial of the preliminary injunction is dismissed as moot. Imposition of the $ 2,500 sanction by the trial court is affirmed. Appellants' counsel is to pay respondent an additional penalty of $ 14,000 and the court $ 2,324 for pursuing a frivolous appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant voters sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Marin County (California) which denied there request for a preliminary injunction against appellee town and imposed sanctions upon appellants and their attorney for initiating a frivolous action.


OVERVIEW: Appellant voters filed an action against appellee town to enjoin an election regarding a ballot measure from taking place. The trial court denied the injunction and ordered sanctions against appellants and their attorney for filing a frivolous action. Upon review, the court affirmed and ordered appellants' counsel to pay an additional penalty for pursuing a frivolous appeal. The court held that the appeal from the denial of the injunction was moot because there was no justiciable controversy where the election appellants sought to enjoin already had transpired. The court reasoned that once the election took place the remedies of appellants were removed from the field of injunctive relief and were relegated to remedies subsequent to the election. The court held the sanctions ordered against appellants for filing a frivolous action were proper where appellants lacked legal grounds, were guilty of latches, and completely lacked evidence to substantiate the allegations contained in their pleadings. The court found that sanctions on appeal were also appropriate because the appeal was completely devoid of merit and that no reasonable attorney would have pursued the appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that issued sanctions against appellant voters and their attorney for filing a frivolous action. The court found that an appeal of the trial court's order denying an injunction was moot where the election appellants sought to enjoin took place. The court held that sanctions were proper where appellants lacked both legal grounds and evidence and were guilty of laches. The court also ordered sanctions on appeal.

 

639 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ricks


No. B017709.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN


198 Cal. App. 3d 1266; 244 Cal. Rptr. 696; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 149



February 25, 1988 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. B017709, Robert L. Roberson, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that convicted him of first-degree murder and robbery pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 211.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder and robbery under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 211. The trial court denied defendant's motion that the prosecution be required to make an election between murder with malice aforethought and felony murder as to the murder charge. Defendant admitted robbing and shooting the victim but claimed diminished capacity. Defendant requested instructions defining malice aforethought, second degree murder, manslaughter, and the absence of premeditation, deliberation, malice, or intent to kill due to diminished capacity. The trial court refused to give the instructions and defendant was found guilty as charged. On appeal, the court found the trial court committed error in failing to give these instructions. Their omission practically guaranteed defendant's convictions of robbery and first degree felony murder as the defendant had admitted robbing and killing the victim, but offered the sole defense of diminished capacity with respect to the mens rea elements of robbery and murder. Failure to give the instructions required reversal of defendant's conviction of murder; the court remanded for further proceedings. The conviction for robbery was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the defendant's conviction of first-degree murder because the trial court erred in failing to give certain instructions to the jury that contained the defense of diminished capacity, and remanded for further proceedings; the robbery conviction was affirmed.

 

640 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Camillo


No. C001670


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


198 Cal. App. 3d 981; 244 Cal. Rptr. 286; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 167



February 22, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Butte County, No. 90998, Roger G. Gilbert, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed her conviction in the Superior Court of Butte County (California) for welfare fraud under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 10980, claiming the charge should have been for a series of misdemeanors rather than a single felony, and the classification of the crime as a felony violated her due process and equal protection rights. Defendant was also convicted of perjury, Cal. Penal Code § 118.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed her conviction for felony welfare fraud, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 10980, for obtaining over $ 2,900 in excess welfare benefits by failing to report all her income, even though she never received more than $ 400 in one month. Defendant did not appeal her conviction for perjury, Cal. Penal Code § 118. Affirming the conviction, the court decided that the test whether a series of acts constituted one felony or a series of misdemeanors depended upon whether the evidence disclosed one general intent or separate and distinct intents. The primary concern was whether the acts were pursuant to one intention, one general impulse, and one plan. The court rejected defendant's claim that the legislature did not intend to provide so much discretion in the application of a penal statute and that the discretion violated equal protection rights. The court also rejected her claim that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 10980(c)(2) was void for vagueness, explaining her conduct fell clearly within its bounds.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's felony conviction for welfare fraud for obtaining over $ 2,900 in excess benefits even though she never obtained more than $ 400 in one month. The court found one general intent which supported combining the serial crimes into one felony. The court rejected defendant's claim that the statute was void for vagueness and the discretion provided violated equal protection rights.

 

641 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Zumwalt v. Superior Court


No. D006479.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One.


211 Cal. App. 3d 821; 244 Cal. Rptr. 273; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 117



February 18, 1988 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for review GRANTED May 19, 1988 (S001618) (See 49 Cal. 3d 167)


PRIOR HISTORY: This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of February 18, 1988, previously reported at 198 Cal. App. 3d 833.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county clerk sought review of the action of respondent Superior Court, San Diego County (California), adopting a rule pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 69898 that transferred employment and supervision of 121 employees of petitioner to real party in interest executive officer.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner county clerk sought to compel respondent Superior Court of San Diego County (California) to rescind a rule, adopted pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 69898, that transferred 121 employees from supervision of petitioner to real party in interest executive officer. The court denied the petition. The court found that Cal. Gov't Code § 69898(c)-(d), which gave respondent the flexibility to allocate duties and responsibilities between petitioner and real party in interest, was consistent with Cal. Const. art. VI, § 4, and gave effect to its general intent to provide the necessary superior court staffing for the effective administration of justice. The court found that, under art. VI, § 4, delegation from the legislature to the superior courts of the function of providing for the duties of their executive officers was constitutionally permitted and reasonable. Even if the constitution did not specifically permit the legislature to delegate the function to the superior courts, such delegation was not necessarily unconstitutional because the doctrine of separation of powers did not prohibit one branch from exercising in some degree the functions of another branch.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner county clerk's action to compel respondent Superior Court of San Diego County (California) to rescind its rule transferring employees from supervision of petitioner to real party in interest executive officer, because rule was adopted under proper delegation of authority.

 

642 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People ex rel. Smith v. Parkmerced Co.


No. A035487


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


198 Cal. App. 3d 683; 244 Cal. Rptr. 22; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 100



February 17, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 10, 1988, and Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 19, 1988. Kaufman, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 778868, Claude D. Perasso, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlords challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of San Francisco County (California), which enjoined appellants from charging extra lease fees to respondents, tenants and former tenants, and which ordered reimbursement of said fees paid by respondents.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, former tenants and tenants, filed suit against appellant landlords, alleging that appellants had unlawfully retained security fees in violation of Cal. Civ. Code § 1950.5. The trial court found that appellants' retention of the fees constituted an unlawful business practice and enjoined them from charging the fees. It also ordered appellants to reimburse respondents. Appellants challenged the trial court's order, contending that § 1950.5 did not prohibit landlords from receiving nonrefundable security deposits, that it was unconstitutional, that the trial court abused its discretion in assessing civil penalties, and that the order to refund the fees withheld was erroneous. The court affirmed, holding that § 1950.5 permitted collection of limited security deposits, but restricted the use thereof to specifically defined purposes. The court held that the fees withheld by appellants were not applied for any of the purposes permitted by § 1950.5, and thus, they were required to be refunded. The court further found that § 1950.5 was not unconstitutional, nor did the trial court abuse its discretion in assessing civil penalties against appellants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, holding that landlords were permitted to collect and use security deposits, but their use was restricted to specifically defined purposes. The fees withheld by appellant landlords were not applied for any of the purposes statutorily permitted purposes and thus, they were required to be refunded.

People v. Romo


No. A037010


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


198 Cal. App. 3d 581; 243 Cal. Rptr. 801; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 109



February 11, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied April 27, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 8699-C, James W. Luther, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), after he was convicted of cultivating marijuana, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11358, possessing marijuana for sale, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359, and possessing cocaine, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, on the ground that a helicopter overflight of his backyard violated his U.S. Const. amend. IV rights.


OVERVIEW: The police conducted a helicopter overflight of defendant's fenced-in backyard and observed marijuana, without the use of electronic aids. Later, the police went to defendant's home with a search warrant; they were greeted by defendant's sister, and they all went into the house together. The police recovered cocaine and marijuana. After his motion to suppress the evidence, under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, was denied, defendant was convicted of cultivating marijuana, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11358, possessing marijuana for sale, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359, and possessing cocaine, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350. Appellant argued that the overflight violated his U.S. Const. amend. IV rights and that the police violated the "knock-notice" provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 1531. The court affirmed the judgment, holding that defendant's U.S. Const. amend. IV rights were not violated, as the plants were visible without the use of electronic aids and the flight was conducted in a "physically nonintrusive manner," and that the superior court properly found that § 1531 was not applicable, as the door was open and the officers were not told they could not enter.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that defendant's Fourth Amendment rights were not violated by a helicopter overflight, as the marijuana plants were visible without the use of electronic aids and the flight was conducted in a "physically nonintrusive manner," and that the superior court properly found that "knock-notice" statute was not applicable, as the door to the home was open and the officers were not told they could not enter.

 

644 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. McGirr


No. F008432


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


198 Cal. App. 3d 629; 243 Cal. Rptr. 793; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 88



February 11, 1988 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II and III.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 216519, Frank S. Pierson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), which found him guilty of child concealment in violation of Cal. Penal. Code § 277.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found appellant guilty of child concealment in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 277. On review, appellant challenged the statute as being unconstitutionally vague in that § 277 failed to provide any guidance as to what constituted "good cause" for child concealment. The court found that "good cause" had acquired reasonable certainty by established usage, interpretation, and a settled commonsense meaning. Moreover, the court found that the term "good cause" was further narrowed or circumscribed in the statute by the requirement that the taking, detaining, concealment, or enticing of the child be done with malice. Moreover, the court concluded that appellant's conduct clearly fell within the prohibition of § 277 that the phrase "without good cause," as applied to him, was constitutionally valid. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed because applicable statute was not unconstitutionally vague. Moreover, the court concluded that appellant's conduct fell clearly within the prohibition against child concealment, where there was no credible evidence of good cause for such concealment.

People v. Dasher


No. D004523


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


198 Cal. App. 3d 28; 243 Cal. Rptr. 486; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 53



January 29, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 25, 1988, and Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 19, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR29265, Raul Rosado, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order of recommitment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sex offender challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that found that he was still a mentally disordered sex offender and recommitted him to the state mental hospital for an additional two years.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sex offender pleaded guilty to one count of forcible oral copulation of a 13-year-old girl and one count of kidnapping. The superior court found that appellant was a mentally disordered sex offender (MDSO). Pursuant to former Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6316, appellant was committed to the state department of mental health for confinement in the state hospital. On four previous occasions, appellant's commitment was extended pursuant to § 6316.2. On this occasion, appellant's commitment was extended for an additional two years after a trial in which the jury found that appellant was still a MDSO. Appellant challenged this latest recommitment as a MDSO on constitutional grounds and on the basis of instructional error. After consideration, the court concluded that it was not error for the trial court not to instruct the jury on a definition of sexual offense within the meaning of § 6316.2. The court also found that it was not error to allow certain documents into evidence, the jury was not inaccurately instructed on the definition of conservatorship, and the recommitment scheme was constitutional. The trial court's order of recommitment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court that recommitted appellant sex offender to the state mental hospital for an additional two years because the recommitment scheme was constitutional, and the trial court did not err in its jury instructions or by allowing certain documents into evidence.
