Leeb v. Delong


No. G002587


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


198 Cal. App. 3d 47; 243 Cal. Rptr. 494; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 253



January 29, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 430889, Philip Edgar Schwab, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a student newspaper editor, sought review of the order from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that granted appellee school district's motion for summary judgment in an action claiming that the school district violated the free press provision of the Cal. Const. art. I, § 2.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a student newspaper editor, submitted a proposed edition to appellee high school principal for approval. The edition was an April Fool's Day spoof. One of the articles contained a photograph of five female students that gave the impression that they were waiting in line to have their photographs taken in the nude. The principal, believing that the article would damage the reputations of the girls, the school, and the school district, prohibited distribution of the newspaper. Appellee found that the disclaimer was too inconspicuous and difficult to see. Appellant sought immediate review by the associate superintendent and the superintendent, who both supported the principal. Claiming that Cal. Educ. Code § 48907 and the school district's administrative regulation violated the free press provision of the Cal. Const. art. I, § 2, appellant unsuccessfully sought a temporary restraining order and preliminary injunction. Summary judgment was granted in the school district's favor. The court concluded that Cal. Educ. Code § 48907 was constitutional and that the school district could censor material reasonably believed to contain an actionable defamation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court that granted summary judgment in favor of appellee school district because appellee school district could censor material from inclusion in a school newspaper that was reasonably believed to contain an actionable defamation.

 

647 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Perumal v. Saddleback Valley Unified Sch. Dist.


No. G003181


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


198 Cal. App. 3d 64; 243 Cal. Rptr. 545; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 254



January 29, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On February 19, 1988 and February 29, 1988, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied April 27, 1988. Broussard, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 457895, Philip Edgar Schwab, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant students challenged a judgment of the Superior Court, Orange County (California), which denied appellants' action seeking a writ of mandate to compel appellee school district to allow distribution of promotional materials related to appellants' religious group.


OVERVIEW: Appellant students sought a writ of mandate compelling appellee school district to permit distribution of flyers and acceptance of an advertisement for their religious group. Appellants contended a school board policy prohibiting off-campus groups or clubs from functioning on campus was overbroad. However, the court disagreed, holding that appellants' group fell squarely within the meaning of the terms "club" or group." The court also held that the fact that school principals allowed the religious group to meet informally on campus did not change the group's "off-campus" or private status. Additionally, the court held that the school yearbook's rejection of an advertisement for the group was allowable because appellee school district had validly adopted a closed-forum policy, in which only school-sponsored clubs were authorized to function or advertise in school publications. The court then held allowance of such promotional activities by appellants' group did not violate appellants' right to freedom of religious expression.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying appellant students' action that sought a writ of mandate compelling appellee school district to allow distribution of promotional materials related to appellants' religious group because a policy prohibiting distribution of such materials was constitutionally valid.

 

648 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Searle v. City of Berkeley Rent Stabilization Bd.


No. A035905


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


197 Cal. App. 3d 1251; 243 Cal. Rptr. 449; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 49



January 26, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 17, 1988, and appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 27, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 606233-6, Henry Ramsey, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlords sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied appellants' petition for a writ of mandamus to vacate respondent city rent stabilization board's decision that they were ineligible for annual general rent adjustments under a city rent stabilization ordinance that the prior owners had lost.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landlords purchased an apartment building that had been previously unregistered the city's rent stabilization ordinance, and notified tenants of rent increases incorporating annual general rent adjustments (AGAs) for the previous three years. The tenants filed a petition with respondent city rent stabilization board, which determined that appellants were ineligible for AGAs that the prior owners had lost pursuant to the provisions of the ordinance. The trial court denied appellants' petition for a writ of mandamus. Upon review, the court affirmed, finding that in the plain meaning of the ordinance, appellants did not have rights conferred upon them the prior owner did not own, thus they were on notice be the terms of the ordinance that they could not recapture the lost AGAs. The court stated that if the ordinance had a confiscatory result on appellants, the proper remedy was to file an individual rent adjustment petition showing that they were not receiving a fair return on their investment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant landlords' writ of mandamus. The court found that appellant landlords were on notice that the terms of respondent city's rent stabilization ordinance would prevent them from recapturing annual general rent adjustments that the prior owners lost.

 

649 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City of Compton v. Bunner


No. B021102.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four.


197 Cal. App. 3d 662; 243 Cal. Rptr. 100; 1988 Cal. App. LEXIS 8



January 6, 1988 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review denied July 21, 1988 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. IV, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SCC 12731, Marvin D. Rowen and Kenneth W. Gale, Judges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, city, organization, and individuals, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which granted defendants, commissioner, state, and insurance company's, demurer and dismissed plaintiffs' action challenging the constitutionality of territorial calculation of automobile insurance rates.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, city, organization, and individuals, filed suit against defendants, commissioner, state, and insurance company, seeking to declare unconstitutional Cal. Ins. Code § 11628 and redlining practices. The trial court granted defendants' demurrers and dismissed plaintiffs' action. Plaintiffs appealed. The court held that with the exception of the claims raised by plaintiff city on its own behalf, the action against defendant insurance company was barred by the exhaustion of administrative remedies doctrine. Administrative remedies were available under Cal. Ins. Code § 1858, and no exceptions to the exhaustion rule applied. Plaintiff individuals did not have standing to sue because they failed to state facts sufficient to constitute a cause of action for taxpayer relief. Plaintiff city lacked standing to sue, individually and as a representative, because it was not a member of the class. Plaintiff organization had standing. The redlining practice was not a state action. The authority under § 11628 to differentiate in rates between geographical areas did not allow invidious discrimination. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment granting defendants, commissioner, state and insurance company's, demurrer and dismissing plaintiffs, city, organization, and individuals', action challenging the constitutionality of territorial calculation of automobile insurance rates. Furthermore, the redlining practice was not a state action, and the statute allowing differential rates was constitutional.

 

650 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Coleman v. Department of Personnel Administration


No. C000398.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


223 Cal. App. 3d 1016; 242 Cal. Rptr. 839; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2506



December 30, 1987.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review GRANTED April 7, 1988 (S004129) (See 52 Cal.3d 1102)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 332442, Joseph A. DeCristoforo, Jr., Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of December 30, 1987, previously reported at 197 Cal. App. 3d 390 and Reprinted at 210 Cal. App. 3d 986.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied plaintiff's petition for a writ of administrative mandate and adopted the decision of respondent department of personnel administration to not reinstate plaintiff to employment with real party in interest state employer pursuant to the automatic resignation statute, Cal. Gov't Code § 19996.2.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee was terminated pursuant to the automatic resignation statute, Cal. Gov't Code § 19996.2, from his employment with real party in interest state employer because plaintiff was absent without leave for five consecutive working days. Respondent department of personnel administration denied plaintiff's administrative appeal and plaintiff filed a petition for a writ of administrative mandate in the superior court, which found that Cal. Gov't Code § 19996.2 was constitutional on its face and as applied to plaintiff and that the administrative decision denying plaintiff's reinstatement was supported by the weight of the evidence. On appeal, plaintiff contended that the automatic resignation statute was so arbitrary, unreasonable, and unduly harsh that it ran afoul of substantive due process and equal protection of the laws. The court affirmed that the plain language of Cal. Gov't Code § 19996.2 clearly stated that absence without leave for five consecutive working days was an automatic resignation; thus, plaintiff's intent was irrelevant. The court also held that the procedure contemplated by Cal. Gov't Code § 19996.2 satisfied procedural due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of plaintiff employee's petition for a writ of mandate after a decision not to reinstate plaintiff to employment with real party in interest state employer. The procedure of the automatic resignation statute satisfied procedural due process and was constitutionally applied to plaintiff.

 

651 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



McNair v. Worldwide Church of God


No. B010791


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


197 Cal. App. 3d 363; 242 Cal. Rptr. 823; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2478



December 30, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On January 27, 1988, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 17, 1988. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NEC 27381, No. B010791, Robert M. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded for a new trial consistent with the requirements of this opinion. Each to bear own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants two ministers and church challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded respondent former church member compensatory and punitive damages for libel, slander, and intentional infliction of emotional distress.


OVERVIEW: Respondent former church member brought an action for libel, slander, intentional infliction of emotional distress, invasion of privacy, and conspiracy against appellants two ministers and church. Respondent based her action on stories in church publications and statements at church meetings concerning her divorce from one of appellants. The jury was not instructed that it was required to find constitutional malice in order to impose liability on appellants. The jury found in respondent's favor and she was awarded compensatory and punitive damages. The court reversed and remanded for a new trial consistent with its opinion because in order for respondent to recover damages for defamatory remarks made during the course of a doctrinal explanation by a duly authorized minister, she was required to show, by clear and convincing evidence, that the defamatory statements were made with the knowledge that they were false or with reckless disregard of whether they were false or not.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that awarded respondent former church member damages for libel, slander, and intentional infliction of emotional distress because in order to recover damages, respondent had to prove that the appellants two ministers and church had made the defamatory statements with constitutional malice.

 

652 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Wilson v. Cook


No. F007836


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


197 Cal. App. 3d 344; 242 Cal. Rptr. 806; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2476



December 29, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 348960-6, James L. Quaschnick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the preliminary injunction is reversed insofar as it restrains the appellants from regulating respondents' barbecue and demonstration activities at the lake. The order is affirmed insofar as it prohibits appellants from interfering with respondents' teaching activities provided no solicitation or advertising of such lessons or the sale or rental of the boards occurs at the lake. Each side to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Superintendent of Parks and Recreation and others, challenged the order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), that granted a preliminary injunction to respondent business owners enjoining appellants from prohibiting respondent's teaching and demonstrating sailboarding in a state park.


OVERVIEW: The State Department of Parks and Recreation (Department) solicited bids for a concession for the rental and sale of sailboards and sailboard instruction within the Millerton Lake State Recreation Area. The concessionaire paid a percentage of all business receipts to the Department. Respondent business owners sold sailboards for use at the lake and taught sailboarding at the lake, but the lessons were arranged for and prepaid for outside the park area and the sailboards were sold outside the park area. Respondents also gave free lessons in connection with a weekly barbecue. After awarding the concession for sailboarding, appellant superintendent notified respondents that, pursuant to Cal. Code Regs. tit. 14 § 4331, they were prohibited from giving sailboard lessons or demonstration barbecues at the lake. The court held that the trial court erred in granting a preliminary injunction enjoining appellants from prohibiting respondents from holding the promotional weekly barbecue. The court held that respondents were permitted to continue to teach sailboarding at the lake because the commercial and promotional aspects of the lessons occurred outside the park area.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order granting a preliminary injunction insofar as it restrained appellants, Superintendent of Parks and Recreation and others, from regulating respondent business owners' barbecue and demonstration activities at the lake. The court affirmed the trial court's order insofar as it prohibited appellants from interfering with respondents' teaching activities.

 

653 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



County of L.A. v. Classified Ins. Corp.


No. B024506


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


197 Cal. App. 3d 60; 242 Cal. Rptr. 673; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2450



December 18, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 5, 1988, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 2, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 613704, No. C 123456, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We therefore affirm.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bail bond surety challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded respondent county summary judgment and imposed postjudgment interest on appellant's bail bond forfeiture. Appellant contended that the judgment was entered in excess of the trial court's jurisdiction because Cal. Penal Code § 1306(a) prohibited penalty assessments.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bail bond surety posted bail for a criminal defendant who failed to appear in the trial court. The bail was declared forfeited by the trial court, and appellant failed to bring an action within the time limit for relief from forfeiture. The trial court therefore granted summary judgment in favor of respondent county pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1306. The judgment against appellant included the amount of the bond, costs, and postjudgment interest. Appellant challenged the ruling and contended that the trial court entered the ruling on postjudgment interest in excess of its jurisdiction. Appellant claimed that the general provisions of the statute concerning enforcement of money judgments controlled, and that the statute prohibited a penalty assessment. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that the legislature intended to impose postjudgment interest on bail bond forfeitures to compensate local governments for the loss of use of money while a judgment remained unpaid. The court further held that the prohibition of a penalty assessment applied to a criminal defendant's posting of bond, and did not apply after forfeiture of the bond.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent county and imposed postjudgment interest on appellant bail bond surety's bond forfeiture because the legislature intended to impose postjudgment interest on bail bond forfeitures and a penalty assessment prohibition only applied to bail bonds before forfeiture.

 

654 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Boags v. Mun. Court


No. B030243


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


197 Cal. App. 3d 65; 242 Cal. Rptr. 681; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2451



December 18, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 13, 1988, and the petition of real party in interest for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 17, 1988.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue commanding the respondent municipal court to vacate that part of its order of May 28, 1987, overruling petitioner's demurrer to count II of the complaint in case number 87M00368, and thereafter to enter a new a different order sustaining such demurrer. In all other respects the petition is denied. The stay order issued herein October 15, 1987, shall continue in effect until (1) all acts directed above have been performed, (2) this decision is final in all California courts, or (3) the Supreme Court grants review of this decision, whichever may first occur.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner judge sought review of an order from respondent Municipal Court for Beverly Hills Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which denied petitioner's motions to dismiss and for an evidentiary hearing in connection with his motion to dismiss, and overruled his demurrer to both counts. Petitioner had been criminally charged for failure to disqualify himself from presiding over a matter.


OVERVIEW: In this case of first impression, the court was called upon to decide whether petitioner judge could be charged with a crime for failing to disqualify himself from deciding proceedings, when such disqualification was made mandatory by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code 170.1. The court vacated the trial court's order which denied petitioner's motions to dismiss and for an evidentiary hearing. The court concluded that such prosecution constituted an improper invasion by the executive of the province of the judiciary, and was therefore violative of the separation of powers set forth in Cal. Const. art. III, § 3. The doctrine of separation of powers demanded that the branches of government were to be coequal. The judicial power therefore had to be independent, and that power was compromised when an individual judge wished to perform his functions but had to first consider the possibility that the prosecutor could disapprove and charge him criminally.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's order, which denied petitioner judge's motion to dismiss and for an evidentiary hearing in connection with his motion to dismiss, and overruled his demurrer to both counts. The court concluded that prosecution of petitioner for failure to disqualify himself constituted an improper invasion by the executive of the province of the judiciary, and was therefore violative of the separation of powers.

 

655 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Riese v. St. Mary's Hospital & Medical Center


No. A034048


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


209 Cal. App. 3d 1303; 271 Cal. Rptr. 199; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2430



December 16, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review granted March 3, 1988. Review dismissed and opinion ordered published June 22, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 841488, Raymond D. Williamson, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the case remanded for proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant institutionalized patient, on behalf of a class of similarly situated patients, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied her petition seeking a determination that appellant's informed consent was required before antipsychotic drugs could be administered by respondent hospital.


OVERVIEW: Appellant institutionalized patient was involuntarily committed to respondent hospital, who administered antipsychotic drugs without appellant's consent. Appellant brought a class action on behalf of institutionalized patients similarly situated to seek a determination that the patients' informed consent was required before antipsychotic drugs could be administered. The trial court dismissed the action. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000 et seq., did not permit involuntarily committed patients to be administered antipsychotic drugs in nonemergency situations without first obtaining their informed consent. The court held that a determination of whether appellant was competent to give informed consent had to be made by a court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of appellant institutionalized patient's action against respondent hospital, holding that absent a judicial determination of incompetence, appellants could not be given antipsychotic drugs in nonemergency situations without their informed consent.

 

656 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Title Ins. and Trust Co. v. County of Riverside


No. E002223.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two.


196 Cal. App. 3d 1353; 242 Cal. Rptr. 515; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2427



December 15, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellant's petition for review GRANTED March 17, 1988 (See 48 Cal. 3d 84)
KAUFMAN, J., did not participate.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 144292, Gerald F. Schulte, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, counties and state board of equalization, challenged a holding of the Riverside Superior Court (California), that determined that reassessment of real property was not required after a corporate consolidation, under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 64(c), and which denied respondent subsidiary's motion for refunds. Appellants contended that change of ownership of an acquired corporation's subsidiary was a taxable event.


OVERVIEW: Following a corporate consolidation by stock transfer, appellants, counties and state board of equalization, issued assessors' letters, to respondent subsidiary, indicating that the acquisition of a corporation through stock transfer resulted in a change of ownership which required reappraisal of any real property owned by the parent and the subsidiaries under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 64(c). The trial court held that § 64(c) was inapplicable to respondent, as a subsidiary of an acquired corporation, and that appellants should refund excessive taxes paid by respondent. Appellants sought review. Finding that the lower court's ruling that § 64(c) applied to property owned by respondent, as a subsidiary, was contrary to the language to the tax code, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. Moreover, the court held that there was no change of ownership in the real property of the acquired corporation because it survived the merger. The court denied refunds for respondent's overpayments to appellants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent subsidiary, because there was no change of ownership, following a corporate consolidation, within the meaning of the state tax code that triggered value reassessment by appellants, counties and state board of equalization, for real estate tax purposes. The court affirmed the denial of refunds for overpayments by respondent to appellants.

 

657 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



E. Gottschalk & Co. v. County of Merced


No. F007762


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


196 Cal. App. 3d 1378; 242 Cal. Rptr. 526; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2429



December 15, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Merced County, No. 81805, George C. Barrett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Costs are awarded to appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Merced County (California), which held that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 61 was unconstitutional as being in conflict with the provisions of the Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, § 2, in respondent landowner's challenge to reappraisal of his land. Appellant contended that it was not unconstitutional, under its power to levy taxes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county reappraised respondent landowner's property, after respondent entered into a 30-year lease, with two successive 10-year renewal options, thereby significantly raising respondent's tax responsibility. Respondent challenged the reapprasial, contending that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 61 was unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, § 2. The trial court found in favor of respondent, and appellant challenged. On appeal, the court held that the statute was to be construed to have its common sense meaning in order to avoid an unreasonable or absurd result. Because the Legislature had the authority to levy taxes, and enact statutes toward that purpose, the provisions of the statute defining a change in ownership as a leasehold period that approximated the valuation of the lease fee interest for revaluation purposes were not unconstitutional. The court reversed the trial court's decision in favor of respondent landowner and awarded costs to appellant county.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of respondent landowner, holding that because a lease to use a structure had, inherent in it, the right to occupy the land, appellant county's reappraisal was justified under a 35-year lease as a change in ownership, and that it was an unreasonable construction of the controlling statute to conclude that lease renewal options should not be included in the lease term for revaluation purposes.

 

658 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Tri County Apt. Ass'n v. City of Mt. View


No. H002346


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


196 Cal. App. 3d 1283; 242 Cal. Rptr. 438; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2419



December 10, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 17, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 593445, Conrad Lee Rushing, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a city and its city attorney, appealed an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), in which Mountain View, Cal., Ordinance No. 27.85 was found invalid because it impermissibly conflicted with Cal. Civ. Code § 827.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, two landlords and an apartment association, sought declaratory and injunctive relief from the enforcement of Mountain View, Cal., Ordinance No. 27.85. Plaintiffs asserted that the ordinance was invalid because it conflicted with Cal. Civ. Code § 827, which dealt with notification for increasing rent. The trial court found the ordinance invalid and an appeal ensued. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. According to the court, a city could make and enforce within its limits all ordinances and regulations not in conflict with general laws. The court stated that a determination of whether local legislation was in conflict turned on whether the state's preemption of the field was complete. The court stated that the task was to determine whether the state had occupied a relevant field that included the subject of the local legislation, and was sufficiently related so that a court could detect a patterned approach to the subject. The court concluded that the ordinance dealt with notification, not rent control, and that § 827 specified not only a time for notice to tenants, but also prohibited the parties from agreeing to a shorter notice than another specified period.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment finding that a local ordinance extending the notice requirement for a rent increase was invalid because the state legislature had specifically addressed the very issue of notice. Therefore, the ordinance impermissibly conflicted with a state statute.

 

659 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Jarrell


No. A036203


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


196 Cal. App. 3d 604; 242 Cal. Rptr. 219; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2355



November 24, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Parts II through V of this opinion are not certified for publication. (See Cal. Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1.)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 31547, David A. Dolgin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) for first-degree burglary under Cal. Penal Code §§ 459, 460(1), contending multiple errors, including that the trial court erred in instructing the jury as to degrees of conviction, his involuntary confession and identification testimony of a key witness should have been suppressed, and ineffective assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and accomplices were observed by a neighbor while removing items from complainant's residence. At trial for first-degree burglary, the trial judge instructed the jury that if they found defendant guilty, as a matter of law, the offense was first degree burglary. Defendant was convicted and challenged, alleging as the primary error, that the trial judge's instruction denied him a trial by jury on the essential element of whether the residence was a dwelling, thereby making the offense first-degree burglary. On appeal, the court held that because defendant had not submitted evidence that the residence was not a dwelling, the jury instruction did not improperly relieve the prosecution of proving each element beyond a reasonable doubt. Further, the court held that where the error complained of was harmless, the per se rule of reversal did not apply and the court affirmed defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first-degree burglary, holding that the evidence before the trial court established degree as a matter of law, and showed that contrary evidence was not worthy of consideration. Therefore, the trial court's jury instruction did not deprive defendant of a jury trial on every essential element of the crime.

 

660 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Zendejas


No. H002027


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


196 Cal. App. 3d 367; 241 Cal. Rptr. 715; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2334



November 19, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 25, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 101451, Read Ambler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara (California), which entered a conviction against him for the offense of threatening a public employee, a felony under Cal. Penal Code § 71. Defendant argued that his conduct did not violate § 71 because his threats were not "directly communicated" to a public employee.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of threatening a public employee, a felony under Cal. Penal Code § 71. Defendant argued on appeal that his conduct did not violate § 71 because his threats were not "directly communicated" to the service manager of his former employer where he left a message on an answering machine, and in another instance he conversed with a co-employee. The court found that § 71 expressly provided that the phrase "directly communicated" included, but was not limited to, communication to the recipient of the threat by telephone, telegraph, or letter and that each of these methods of communication may involve an intervening agency. Therefore, the court held that the word "directly" required that the threat must reach the public officer personally to be "directly communicated" to the officer within the meaning of § 71. Furthermore, the court held that § 71 was not unconstitutional because it was not vague or ambiguous. As such, the court affirmed defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for the offense of threatening a public employee, holding that defendant's threats were "directly communicated" to a public employee where he left a message on an answering machine and conversed with a co-employee because pursuant to statute the word "directly" only required the threat to reach the public officer personally, regardless of whether there was an intervening agency.

 

661 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Kegler


No. B018744


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


197 Cal. App. 3d 72; 242 Cal. Rptr. 897; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2452



November 19, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 3, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A759269, Ernest M. Hiroshige, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting him of murder and robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17) and finding appellant used a revolver in the commission of the offenses pursuant to §§ 120.225 and 1203.06(a)(1) and inflicted great bodily injury pursuant to § 1203.075. Defendant contended the trial court erred in refusing two jury instructions and in excluding evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the trial court convicting him of murder and robbery in violation of § 190.2(a)(17) and finding that in the commission of the offenses defendant used a revolver within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code §§ 120.225 and 1203.06(a)(1) and inflicted great bodily injury pursuant to § 1203.075. The court affirmed the conviction and judgment. Trial court did not err by refusing defendant's request for a modified version of jury instructions to pinpoint his defense of third party culpability because defendant did not present sufficient evidence of third party culpability linking any particular third person to actual perpetration of the crime. The trial court also did not err in applying Cal. Evid. Code § 351.1 to bar evidence of polygraph results because the exclusion of the evidence did not deprive defendant of his constitutional right to compulsory process. The court held that concerns for reliability and fairness outweighed the relatively weak exculpatory significance of the proffered evidence, and thus, application of Cal. Evid. Code § 351.1 did not deny defendant due process or violate his state and federal right to compulsory process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and judgment and held that the trial court did not err in refusing certain instructions or in excluding evidence. The instructions regarding third party culpability were not supported because defendant did not link any third person to the actual perpetration of the crime. In addition, the polygraph was properly excluded because concerns of reliability and fairness outweighed the significance of the evidence.

 

662 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Hopkins & Axene


No. G004031


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


199 Cal. App. 3d 288; 245 Cal. Rptr. 433; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2505



November 17, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified December 16, 1987. Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 3, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. D-234089, Theodore E. Millard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Wife is entitled to costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-husband challenged a determination of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that he should not receive reimbursement for his separate property contribution to a condominium bought during his marriage to respondent ex-wife, whereby title was held in both names, arguing that Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2 entitled him to such reimbursement.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ex-husband sought review of a lower court determination that he was not entitled to reimbursement for his separate property contribution to a condominium bought during his marriage to respondent ex-wife, which condominium was taken in both parties' names, contending that Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2 governed the proceeding because the divorce was filed after the effective date of the statute even though the property was purchased before the statute's effective date. On appeal, the lower court determination was affirmed. In support of its ruling, the court held that if a property right vested before the enactment of a statute, such a right may not be altered except in those rare instances when it was necessary to promote a significantly important state interest. With respect to the instant case, the court found that no state interest was promoted by divesting respondent of her interest in the property in question when she had acquired her community property interest at a time when the only method of defeating or diminishing that right was by proof of an agreement to the contrary.


OUTCOME: The lower court determination was affirmed as the court found that the statute in question was not applicable to the instant case as the property was acquired prior to the effective date of the statute, even though the divorce proceedings were filed after the effective date of the statute.

 

663 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Scigliano


No. G004799


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


196 Cal. App. 3d 26; 241 Cal. Rptr. 546; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2307



November 9, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-61470, James J. Alfano, Sr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State of California challenged dismissal of the charges of possession for sale of methamphetamine, possession of a syringe, and giving false information to a police officer against respondent, from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), arguing that the granting of respondent's motion to suppress evidence found in a closed container during an automobile inventory search was error.


OVERVIEW: Appellant State of California sought review of dismissal of the charges of possession for sale of methamphetamine, possession of a syringe, and giving false information to a police officer against respondent, contending that the lower court erred in granting respondent's motion to suppress narcotics found in a closed container during an automobile inventory search. On appeal, the charges were reinstated. In support of its ruling, the court rejected respondent's contention that the inventory conducted by the police was unlawful because the officer had no right to impound his automobile even though such vehicle, and the property it contained, could not be secured from theft if left alone. The court further held that the police had authority to impound the vehicle to protect the property inside irrespective of his statutory authority to impound vehicles on highways. The court also found that no U.S. Const. amend. IV violation was found when police opened either a closed glove compartment or closed container during an inventory search. Accordingly, the lower court order was set aside.


OUTCOME: The charges against respondent were reinstated, as the court did not find an unconstitutional search and seizure where narcotics were found in a closed container during an inventory search of respondent's automobile pursuant to an arrest.

 

664 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Tacy


No. H002369


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


195 Cal. App. 3d 1402; 241 Cal. Rptr. 400; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2294



November 3, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 28, 1987, and Respondents' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied January 28, 1988. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 106161, Jeremy D. Fogel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly the order of the superior court denying the People's motion to reinstate the complaint is reversed. 8



8 In a case filed September 30, 1987, the First Appellate District, Division One, reached a conclusion identical to ours based upon similar facts. ( People v. White (1987) 195 Cal.App.3d 144, 154 [240 Cal.Rptr. 527].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: People sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) denying their motion to reinstate a criminal complaint pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1238(a)(9). The trial court held that the officer failed to strictly comply with the knock-notice requirement of Cal. Penal Code § 1531 in executing a search warrant because he entered the premises prior to being refused admittance.


OVERVIEW: People sought review of an order from the trial court denying their motion to reinstate a criminal complaint pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1238(a)(9). The court reversed and held that police officers sufficiently complied with the "knock-notice" requirements of Cal. Penal Code § 1531 in executing a search warrant and the execution was reasonable within the meaning of the U.S. Const. amend. IV. The court held that the officers substantially complied with § 1531 even though they entered the premises prior to refused admittance because the underlying purposes of the statute were served by the conduct of the officers. Whatever invasion of privacy occurred was minimal. The officers were visible to the occupant through the screen door. In addition, the uncontradicted evidence demonstrated that the officer made eye contact with defendant, announced his authority by identifying himself as a peace officer, and announced his purpose by saying he had a warrant to search the premises. Because the officers also had a search warrant, defendant had no right to refuse entry. Finally, the possibility of a violent confrontation was negated because defendant could see the officers were armed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and held that the officers substantially complied with the knock-notice requirement in executing a search warrant and the execution was reasonable within the meaning of the federal constitution. Although the officer entered the premises prior to being refused admittance, the officer substantially complied with the purpose of the statute. There was a minimal invasion of privacy and no threat of violent confrontation.

 

665 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Farner


No. A035634.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four.


195 Cal. App. 3d 1139; 241 Cal. Rptr. 655; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2266



October 30, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellant's petition for review DENIED January 21, 1988 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 71026, Dwight C. Ely, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Solano County (California) that awarded appellee wife a share of his military retirement benefits.


OVERVIEW: Appellee wife filed for divorce in 1978. The marriage was dissolved in June 1981. In October 1981 the parties stipulated to a disposition of property. In January 1982, appellee's attorney forwarded a proposed judgment to appellant's attorney. Appellant signed the proposed judgment on September 10, 1982 and returned it to appellee's attorney on January 27, 1983. The stipulation was signed by the judge on February 15, 1983. Over a year later, the lower court granted appellee's request for modification and gave her a share of appellant's pension. Appellant sought review. The court found that the trial court did not have power to modify the judgment because appellee did not request the modification until after the usual time limits for modification had expired. The trial court could use its nunc pro tunc power to consider the judgment as if it was entered on an earlier date because appellant agreed to the judgment on an earlier date and prevented entry of the judgment until after appellee's right to share appellant's pension benefits under Cal. Civ. Code § 5124 had expired. The court affirmed because it was within the trial court's power to enter relief and allow appellee benefits.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the lower court that found appellee wife was entitled to part of appellant husband's pension was affirmed because appellant had agreed to a judgment while appellee was entitled to benefits.

 

666 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (skoblov)


No. H003424


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


195 Cal. App. 3d 1209; 241 Cal. Rptr. 322; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2273



October 30, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Real Party in Interest for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 18, 1988.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of mandate issue as prayed, directing the respondeat superior court to vacate its order issuing a writ of mandate and instead to make a different order denying defendant's motion for diversion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecutor sought a writ of mandate to direct respondent court to revoke an order that directed a municipal court to begin pretrial diversion procedures for a misdemeanor defendant.


OVERVIEW: The prosecutor charged a minor with misdemeanor petty theft, a violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 484 and 488. The minor filed a motion to compel pretrial diversion. After denial of diversion in the municipal court, respondent superior court granted minor's motion by a writ of mandate. The prosecutor sought a writ of mandate. The county did not have a diversion program. Cal. Penal Code § 1001.50 delegated authority to the county board of supervisors to implement a diversion program, subject to the prosecutor's approval. There was no constitutional mandate that required diversion to be uniformly available throughout the state and there was no fundamental right to a diversion program. The court found the statute did not violate equal protection because there was a reasonable basis for the statute. The state legislature had authority to delegate the ability to create a diversion program. The prosecutor's right to approve the program was nothing more than what the prosecutor already had the ability to do. The court granted the writ of mandate because respondent did not have jurisdiction to order creation of a diversion program. The court ordered the proceeding be reinstated.


OUTCOME: The court granted a writ of mandate because an accused did not have a fundamental right to a diversion program and the lower court did not have jurisdiction to order creation of a diversion program.

 

667 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Bouvia v. County of L.A.


Nos. B024452, B022775


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


195 Cal. App. 3d 1075; 241 Cal. Rptr. 239; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2260



October 29, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C583828 and C896997, Jack M. Newman and Warren H. Deering, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The order denying fees in case number C 583828 is reversed and the matter is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein. Plaintiff to recover costs and fees on appeal. The order denying fees in case number C 896997 is affirmed. Plaintiff and defendants to bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient appealed the denial by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of her claims for attorney fees under the private attorney general statute, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5, and under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. The claims were based on injunctive relief she was granted against defendant county's hospital.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff patient sought a writ of mandate in the court after the trial court denied her request for injunctive relief to order removal of a nasogastric feeding tube inserted by defendant county's hospital. The court granted the petition and directed the superior court to order removal of the feeding tube and prohibit its replacement without plaintiff's consent. The trial court denied a request for fees under the private attorney general statute, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code. § 1021.5, and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. When defendant's hospital later sought to reduce plaintiff's morphine dosage, she secured a temporary injunction. Her fee request was again denied. The court on appeal reversed the denial of attorney fees requested under § 1021.5, based on the removal of the feeding tube, because plaintiff secured the enforcement of an important right affecting the public interest by helping to clarify the rights of all persons who received medical treatment. The court affirmed the denial of fees under § 1988 because the record failed to show that plaintiff's alleged injuries were proximately caused by the execution of a governmental policy or custom.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying plaintiff patient fees under the private attorney general statute because plaintiff had secured the enforcement of an important right affecting the public interest. The court affirmed the order denying fees under the federal Civil Rights Attorney's Fees Award Act of 1976 because plaintiff failed to show that the execution of a governmental policy or custom caused her alleged injuries.

 

668 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Loudermilk


No. A034103


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


195 Cal. App. 3d 996; 241 Cal. Rptr. 208; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2256



October 28, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 4, 1988. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 13000-C, John J. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California) decision, which denied defendant's motion to suppress, filed pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, and entered judgment against defendant after defendant pleaded no contest to assault with a firearm and inflicting great bodily harm, in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 245(a)(2), § 12022.7.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the trial court's denial of his motion to suppress the search and seizure of his wallet and a subsequent confession as the unlawful fruit of that seizure at defendant's trial. Defendant was on trial after pleading no contest to assault with a firearm, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245(a)(2), and to inflicting great bodily harm with intent to inflict such injury, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7. The court affirmed the judgment because the seizure of the wallet was reasonable and proper. The seizure of the wallet was used to determine defendant's identity after he denied having identification upon his self. Accordingly, the papers observed were lawfully seized and defendant's spontaneous confession was not the fruit of an illegal search. The search was also proper as a search incident to a lawful arrest since the officer had probable cause to arrest given the similarity in descriptions, the location of defendant when stopped, and the information hidden from the officer. The court also affirmed the sentence because the finding that the victim was particularly vulnerable was supported by the facts and supported the imposition of an upper term.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence and the denial his motion to suppress. The court held that the seizure of defendant's wallet was reasonable under the circumstances, which precluded a spontaneous confession from being the fruit of an illegal search and seizure. Additionally, a search of defendant was valid as a search incident to a lawful arrest, as the officer had probable cause to arrest defendant on suspicion of attempted murder.

 

669 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Anza Parking Corp. v. City of Burlingame


No. A035898


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


195 Cal. App. 3d 855; 241 Cal. Rptr. 175; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2242



October 26, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 24, 1987, and Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied January 20, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 306755, Thomas McGinn Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff parking corporation appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which denied plaintiff a writ of mandate requiring defendant city to enforce a conditional use permit and denied plaintiff damages against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Landowner leased land to plaintiff parking corporation for a parking facility. Defendant city granted a 10-year, "nontransferable" conditional use permit to plaintiff. Plaintiff either subleased to or entered into a management contract with real party in interest parking operator to manage the facility. Landowner sold lots to new owners, who failed to negotiate a new lease with plaintiff. New owners entered into leases with real party in interest. Plaintiff sought a writ of mandate to compel defendant to enforce the nontransferable condition of the conditional use permit. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that denied plaintiff a writ of mandate. The court held that defendant's municipal zoning authority did not have power to condition plaintiff's conditional use permit upon its nontransferrability by plaintiff. The court determined that defendant and real party in interest were not estopped from raising the invalidity of the nontransferable nature of the conditional use permit. The court further found no abuse of discretion in the award of attorney fees to real party in interest due to contractual provisions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that denied plaintiff parking corporation a writ of mandate against defendant city to compel defendant to enforce a nontransferrability provision of a conditional use permit. The court explained that defendant did not have the power to condition the conditional use permit upon its nontransferrability by plaintiff, and that defendant was not estopped from raising this fact.

 

670 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Ixta v. Rinaldi


No. C002805.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


195 Cal. App. 3d 886; 241 Cal. Rptr. 144; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2246; 13 OSHC (BNA) 1449; 1987 OSHD (CCH) P28,072



October 26, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Respondent's petition for review GRANTED January 21, 1988 (S003045); Dismissed March 23, 1989 as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner workers sought a writ of mandamus challenging the line item veto by respondent governor of an item of appropriation to the Department of Industrial Relations (DIR) and the subsequent decision by respondent director of DIR to terminate enforcement of Cal/OSHA in the private sector for lack of funds.


OVERVIEW: After the legislature presented the annual budget to respondent governor, he reduced, under his line item veto power, the aggregate funds appropriated to the Department of Industrial Relations (DIR) in a stated effort to terminate the state's enforcement of Cal/OSHA in the private sector. Thereafter, respondent director of DIR terminated enforcement of Cal/OSHA in the private sector. Petitioner workers sought a writ of mandamus challenging respondent governor's action as in excess of his constitutional authority and respondent director of DIR's decision to terminate private sector enforcement although some funds were appropriated. The court granted the petition and ordered respondent director of DIR to allocate funds from the items of appropriation to enforce Cal/OSHA. The court held that respondent governor's reduction of the amount of funds to be appropriated was simply that - a reduction of funds - and did not actually affect the laws to be enforced, even though that was the stated purpose. Thus, the action was constitutional. Because there were funds available to respondent director of DIR, he was required to allocate the funds for administration and enforcement of Cal/OSHA.


OUTCOME: Petitioner workers' request for a writ of mandamus challenging respondent governor's reduction of funds appropriated to the Department of Industrial Relations (DIR) and respondent director of DIR's decision to terminate private sector enforcement of Cal/OSHA was granted. Respondent governor did not exceed his constitutional authority by reducing appropriations to DIR. However, respondent director of DIR had to allocate the funds available.

 

671 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Knapp Dev. & Design v. Pal-Mal Properties


No. B022351


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


195 Cal. App. 3d 786; 240 Cal. Rptr. 920; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2235



October 23, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC074886, David M. Rothman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contractor challenged the ruling of the Superior Court for Los Angeles County (California) which entered summary judgment against it in an action to foreclose on a mechanic's lien.


OVERVIEW: Appellant contractor sought relief from a summary judgment entered against it in a foreclosure action. Appellant worked as a general contractor on a particular project and some years after the project started, respondent limited partnership was formed to obtain additional funding for the project. Respondent failed to make payments to a particular company on loan for the project. Appellant also demanded payment for the work it had performed on the property. When no payment was made, appellant filed a mechanic's lien and brought an action to foreclose on the mechanic's lien. Trustee intervenors for the particular company respondent secured a loan from moved for summary judgment on the ground that appellant had incorporated but had not obtained a separate contractor's license for respondent. The trial court granted the motion and ordered summary judgment against appellant. The court held that although the purchaser may have had actual knowledge of the pendency of the action, as the sale took place after recordation of the order expunging the lis pendens, the purchaser was statutorily without such notice and took title free of the mechanic's lien.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment, for it was shown that the sale of a particular piece of property took place after the recordation of the order expunging a lis pendens. The purchaser was statutorily without notice and therefore took the property free of the mechanic's lien.

 

672 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Roberts


No. A021591


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


195 Cal. App. 3d 479; 240 Cal. Rptr. 658; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2206



October 9, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 10999-C, John J. Gallagher and Rex H. Sater, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California) that convicted and sentenced him for cultivation of marijuana. On appeal, he contended that the police helicopter overflight above his property constituted an unconstitutional invasion of his expectation of privacy.


OVERVIEW: A police helicopter overflight above appellant's property revealed marijuana growing outside, and within a partially covered greenhouse. Appellant was thereafter charged with cultivation of marijuana and possession of marijuana for sale. He pled guilty, and was sentenced to two years in prison for cultivation. On appeal, he contended that the helicopter overflight was an invasion of his expectation of privacy. The court evaluated whether appellant had an expectation of privacy in the area under surveillance, and noted that he possessed a great expectation of privacy in the curtilage around his home. To determine whether the area under surveillance constituted protected curtilage, the court looked upon: the proximity of the area to appellant's home; whether the area was included within an enclosure surrounding the home; the nature of the uses to which the area was put; and the steps taken by appellant to protect the area from observation. The court in turn concluded that area under surveillance was within the protected residential curtilage, and that the surveillance of appellant's property was unconstitutional. The judgment of the trial court was therefore reversed.


OUTCOME: The judgement of the trial court was reversed. Police helicopter overflight above appellant's property infringed upon the reasonable expectation of privacy that he possessed relative the area under surveillance.

 

673 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Kun


No. A034798


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


195 Cal. App. 3d 370; 240 Cal. Rptr. 564; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2195



October 6, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Marin County, No. 9794, Richard H. Breiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant felon sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which found that it was not cruel and/or unusual punishment for appellant, a person convicted of felony cultivation of marijuana, to register as a drug offender as required by Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11590.


OVERVIEW: Appellant felon pled guilty to cultivation of marijuana, a felony, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11358. Appellant was ordered to register as a drug offender pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11590. Appellant sought review of the mandatory registration, alleging that as applied to him, it violated the constitutional prohibitions against cruel and/or unusual punishment, found in U.S. Const. amend. VIII and Cal. Const. art. I, § 17. The court affirmed the mandatory registration and held that in examining the nature of the offense and the offender, in comparing penalties for more serious crimes, and in comparing penalties for marijuana offenses in other jurisdictions, the mandatory registration was not cruel and unusual punishment. The court held that the punishment was not disproportionate to the offense. The court found that the legislature determined that cultivation of marijuana was a serious crime and found that appellant's involvement in the offense was substantial; therefore, the punishment was not disproportionate to appellant's individual culpability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and held that it was not cruel and/or unusual punishment for appellant felon, a person convicted of felony cultivation of marijuana, to be required to register as a drug offender. The court held that the punishment was not disproportionate to the offense. The legislature determined that cultivation of marijuana was a serious crime and appellant's involvement in the offense was substantial.

 

674 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Gill


No. F007653


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


195 Cal. App. 3d 410; 240 Cal. Rptr. 644; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2200



October 6, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II and III.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 4, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 216014, Frank S. Pierson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his sentence for committing a lewd and lascivious act with a child under age 14 in the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), contending that the trial court erred when it relied upon the provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 1170.71 in selecting an upper eight-year prison term.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant of committing a lewd and lascivious act with a child under age 14. Defendant appealed his sentence, alleging that the trial court erred in selecting the upper eight-year prison term by relying on the provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 1170.71, which made using obscene matter to induce a minor to engage in a lewd act a factor to be considered in aggravation of a sentence. Specifically, defendant argued that the time interval of approximately a year between the act of molestation and the prior exhibition of harmful or obscene material to the minor was too long to sustain an inference that the material was used to induce the minor to engage in the proscribed conduct. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence, holding that the time interval was not determinative. Rather, the focus of the statute was the purpose of the use. There was substantial evidence before the trial court that defendant showed the minor the sexual materials to influence her to engage in lewd and lascivious acts, and no other reason for doing so was suggested by defendant.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence for committing a lewd and lascivious act with a child under age 14 was affirmed because the applicable statute used to enhance defendant's sentence was properly applied. The court explained that there was sufficient evidence to make the requisite showing that defendant showed the child the sexual materials to influence her to engage in the proscribed acts.
