Rubinstein v. Barnes


No. B023759


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


195 Cal. App. 3d 276; 240 Cal. Rptr. 535; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2186



October 1, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 16, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. SM45874, Richard Albert St. John, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Each party is to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant client challenged the order of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California) which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent attorney because appellant's malpractice cause of action was barred by the statute of limitations, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.6.


OVERVIEW: Respondent attorney represented appellant client in the dissolution of appellant's marriage. After the dissolution, appellant determined that the pension benefits portion of her marital property settlement was improperly calculated. The superior court found that appellant's malpractice claim was time-barred by the statute of limitations enacted after the dissolution of appellant's marriage, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.6. The court affirmed, finding that application of § 340.6 was not unconstitutionally retroactive, considering that appellant was allowed four years from the date of enactment of the statute in which to bring the claim. The amount of time was a reasonable time in which to sue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing appellant's malpractice claim against respondent attorney because it was time-barred.

 

676 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. White


No. A035645, No. A034304.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One.


195 Cal. App. 3d 144; 240 Cal. Rptr. 527; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2174



September 30, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for review of respondents DENIED January 21, 1988.
Mosk, J. is of the opinion the petition should be granted.
Appellant's petition for review DENIED January 21, 1988 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 8755-C, James W. Luther, Judge.

Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 8420-C, Arthur B. Broaddus, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, the state challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), which granted the motion of defendants, husband and wife, to suppress evidence under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, and dismissed the action against them. Defendant husband appealed his conviction, claiming execution of a search warrant unconstitutionally resulted in seizure of property not described therein.


OVERVIEW: Two separate appeals were consolidated for review. The state appealed the lower court's grant of the motion of defendants, husband and wife, to suppress evidence under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, claiming entry into defendants' residence by an officer with a warrant, after the officer identified himself, said he had a search warrant, and entered through an unlocked screen door did not fail to give defendants reasonable time to refuse admittance. The court held that refusal of admittance may not be necessary in situations not involving closed or locked doors, and reversed the order dismissing the action because the officer yelled through an open door, and that was considered as having warned defendants of his presence and purpose. Defendant husband appealed his conviction for receiving stolen property, claiming evidence of stolen car parts should have been suppressed because they were not the subject of the search warrant. The court affirmed because the parts were in plain sight, and were observed and examined without being moved and before the officer had knowledge they were stolen. The court held that the parts were subject to seizure and could be introduced into evidence.


OUTCOME: The lower court's dismissal of the action against defendants, husband and wife, was reversed because suppressed evidence should have been admitted where entry into defendants' residence by an officer with a search warrant through an unlocked door, after he identified himself and his purpose, was sufficient warning. Defendant husband's conviction was affirmed because evidence not the subject of a search warrant, but in plain sight, was admissible.

 

677 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Hardman v. Feinstein


No. A031244


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


195 Cal. App. 3d 157; 240 Cal. Rptr. 483; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2175



September 30, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 16, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 827127, Ralph J. Flageollet, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which issued an order sustaining respondent trustees' demurrer to appellants' first amended complaint without leave to amend, in appellants' action to enjoin alleged mismanagement of a trust.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers filed an action against respondent trustees, trustees of the fine arts museums, a charitable trust, for breach and mismanagement of the trust. Respondents demurred to the complaint on the ground, among others, that appellants lacked standing to sue to enforce the provisions of the charitable trust. The trial court sustained respondents' demurrer. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, holding that appellants had no standing to bring an action against respondents because appellants had no special interest in the trust other than as taxpayers and members of the public. The court found that although the fine arts museums was allegedly administered by city officials, the trust assets did not constitute public assets but rather the res of a charitable trust. The court determined that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526(a) did not give rise to a right of action by appellants because respondents, in operating the museums, were fulfilling their fiduciary obligations as trustees, and the alleged misfeasance was not illegal governmental activity or the misappropriation of public property, but the mismanagement of a charitable trust.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court sustaining respondent trustees' demurrer, holding that appellant taxpayers had no standing to bring an action against respondents because appellants had no special interest in the charitable trust managed by respondents other than as taxpayers and members of the public.

 

678 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cox Cable of San Diego v. Bookspan


No. D005176


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


195 Cal. App. 3d 22; 240 Cal. Rptr. 407; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2160



September 25, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 566204, Milton Milkes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant cable company challenged an order from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied a preliminary injunction to allow appellant to reconnect subscribers in an apartment complex, pending resolution of a dispute between appellant and respondents, apartment owner and satellite system provider.


OVERVIEW: Appellant cable company sought a preliminary injunction that would allow it to reconnect subscribers in an apartment complex owned by respondent apartment owner. Respondent apartment owner had disconnected appellant's system in favor of respondent provider's satellite system. The trial court denied the preliminary injunction and appellant sought review. The court noted that delivery of cable television involved a permanent and physical invasion of a property owner's building and, as a result, differed significantly from the delivery of print medium. The court also noted that, while cable television was subject to local regulation, cable television regulations did not include the right to "take" private property for the private use of a cable company. Accordingly, the court found that appellant did not have a constitutional right of access to the tenants of respondent apartment owner's complex. The court found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it refused to grant a temporary injunction, and affirmed the order of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court denying appellant cable company a preliminary injunction was affirmed because appellant did not have a constitutionally compelled right of access to the tenants of respondent apartment owner's complex, and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying the preliminary injunction.

 

679 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Colvin v. S. Cal. Edison Co.


No. B017139


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


194 Cal. App. 3d 1306; 240 Cal. Rptr. 142; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2132



September 18, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rule 976.1 of the California Rules of Court this opinion is certified for partial publication. The portions of this opinion to be published follow.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of plaintiff and appellant for review by the Supreme Court was denied December 2, 1987. Lucas, C. J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 79724, Allan L. Steele, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting a new trial is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant electric utility sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which granted the motion of respondent minor for a new trial, contending there was sufficient evidence to justify the jury's finding that the accident occurred on premises in which appellant had an interest under Cal. Civ. Code § 846 making it appropriate to instruct the jury on immunity under that statute.


OVERVIEW: Respondent minor brought an action for personal injuries sustained when he touched a wire dangling from an electric pole that belonged to appellant electric utility. In a special verdict, the jury found facts that established appellant's immunity from liability under Cal. Civ. Code § 846 and judgment was entered for appellant. The trial court then granted respondent's motion for a new trial, finding there was insufficient evidence to justify the finding that the accident had occurred on premises in which defendant had an interest within the meaning of Cal. Civ. Code § 846 and it was error to instruct the jury on immunity. Appellant sought review and the court reversed the order, finding that appellant had a nonpossessory interest in the land on which its poles were located and had a duty to keep the premises safe for entry. The court concluded that while the utility poles were not recreational in nature, the remainder of the land surrounding the poles was suited for such use and that such use was foreseeable. The court found that the use of appellant's land did not fall within the exception to Cal. Civ. Code § 846 as an area unsuitable for recreation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that granted respondent minor a new trial, finding that appellant electric utility had a nonpossessory interest in the land where its poles were located, therefore coming within the provisions of the recreational use immunity statute. The court concluded it was appropriate to instruct the jury on immunity and their finding of immunity should be reinstated.

 

680 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Watkins


No. F006931


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


195 Cal. App. 3d 258; 240 Cal. Rptr. 626; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2184



September 15, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II through IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified October 1, 1987. Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 23, 1987. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 28922, James G. Bowles, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court, Kern County (California), which based upon a jury verdict, convicted appellant for first-degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 189.


OVERVIEW: Based upon a description received from several witnesses and one victim, appellant was arrested and the state charged him with murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 664 and 187, in addition to various other charges. Following a jury trial, appellant was convicted of first-degree murder and the jury also found he personally used a deadly and dangerous weapon and that appellant was engaged in the commission of a robbery at the time of the murder. Appellant challenged, arguing that he could not be convicted of felony murder, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 189, when the information charged only murder with malice, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187. The court affirmed, holding that because the offense charged in this case included both degrees of murder, appellant could be legally convicted of either degree warranted by the evidence. While identification of the statute violated was advisable, it was not required. Furthermore, under the facts at bar, appellant was not prejudiced by the prosecution's failure to specifically charge appellant with violation of Cal. Penal Code § 189.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Appellant was properly convicted for first degree murder, because despite the fact that the information charged appellant of murder with malice, whether murder was committed with malice or in the context of felony murder, the crime committed was still murder, and while identification of the statute violated was advisable, it was not required.

 

681 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Reese v. Kizer


Nos. A031937, A035545, A036276


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two.


194 Cal. App. 3d 885; 240 Cal. Rptr. 151; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2104



September 11, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellant's petition for review GRANTED December 17, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. H104037-3, Michael E. Ballachey, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Health Services challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which in respondent individuals' class action suit entered a summary judgment, later incorporated into a final judgment, ordering appellant to retroactively award respondents with certain Medicaid benefits as calculated under by Cal. Lab. Code § 14005.16(a).


OVERVIEW: When the legislature infused community property concepts into Medicaid benefit calculations by enacting Cal. Welfare & Institutions Code § 14005.16, it provided that any provision conflicting with federal statutes or regulations should be severed. After federal authorities determined Cal. Welfare & Institutions Code § 14005.16 indeed conflicted with federal law and denied conflict waivers, appellant Department of Health Services withheld implementation of the statute. Respondent individuals, potential beneficiaries under the statute, then filed a class action suit seeking injunctive relief and retroactive application of the statute, which would increase their Medicaid benefits. Intervening legislative action mooted final injunctive relief. The trial court ordered appellant to pay retroactive benefits to respondents and all the class members. The court affirmed. Appellant was barred from refusing to enforce a statute based on a conflict with federal law under Cal. Const. art. III, § 3.5, unless an appellate court had found such conflict.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Appellant Department of Health Services was properly ordered to retroactively pay respondent individuals and all class members Medicaid benefits in an amount reflecting a statutory formula. Although the state law creating the formula stated it was inapplicable if it conflicted with federal law, the state constitution forbade appellant from recognizing any conflict not previously found by an appellate court.

 

682 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hodgkin


No. F007169


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


194 Cal. App. 3d 795; 239 Cal. Rptr. 831; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2094



September 9, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kings County, No. 8109, Timothy S. Buckley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in the instant case is affirmed. The matter is remanded to the trial court for reconsideration of the conditions of probation in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was charged with a violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23153(a), the offense of felony drunk driving that caused injury or death to another person, pled nolo contendere to the charge, pursuant to a plea bargain that she would receive local time rather than a prison commitment. The Superior Court of Kings County (California) sentenced defendant to probation subject to conditions that she contended were unreasonable.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who pled nolo contendere to the charged offense of felony drunk driving that caused injury or death to another person, was sentenced to probation subject to conditions that included a requirement that she pay restitution to the victims of the accident in an amount to be determined by the probation officer up to and including $ 60,000, a condition that defendant contended was unreasonable. The court held that based on the circumstances of the case, the order of restitution was not a reasonable condition within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 1203.1 because it was made without regard for defendant's ability to pay, and nothing suggested the trial court considered alternative means to serve the rehabilitative purpose of probation. Defendant also contended a condition that required her to seek and maintain gainful employment was unconstitutional pursuant U. S. Const. amend. XIII. The court held that a particularized inquiry was needed to establish a record by which the reasonableness of the condition could be determined. The trial court's judgment was affirmed. The court remanded the matter for reconsideration of the conditions of probation.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed. The matter was remanded to the trial court for reconsideration of the conditions imposed on defendant's probation in accordance with the court's opinion. The court held that in imposing conditions of restitution and employment, the trial court failed to consider defendant's particular circumstances as required to establish that the conditions were reasonable.

 

683 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Ketchens v. Reiner


No. B023312


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


194 Cal. App. 3d 470; 239 Cal. Rptr. 549; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2057



August 26, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified August 27, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. EAC57366, Peter S. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed. Plaintiffs to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, parent and activist taxpayer, appealed from the denial of their motion for a preliminary injunction by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California). Plaintiffs sought to bar enforcement of Cal. Educ. Code §§ 44811 and 4812 by defendants, school and law enforcement officials. Plaintiffs argued that the statutes, which outlawed verbal abuse of a teacher in the presence of pupils or school personnel, were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, parent and activist taxpayer, appealed the trial court's order that denied their motion for a preliminary injunction. Plaintiffs sought to bar defendants, school and law enforcement officials, from proceeding against plaintiff parent under Cal. Educ. Code § 44811 for insulting a teacher in the presence of a pupil and for abuse of a teacher in the presence of other school personnel or pupils under Cal. Educ. Code § 4812. Plaintiff parent was charged with a misdemeanor in connection with an altercation she had with her daughter's teacher on school grounds. She filed her action when defendants refused to cease prosecution. The court ruled that the trial court abused its discretion when it denied plaintiffs' motion for a preliminary injunction. The court ruled that both statutes were so broadly drawn that they prohibited not only unprotected speech, but also speech protected by U.S. Const. amend. I. The court held that plaintiffs had satisfied both elements necessary for the issuance of a preliminary injunction because the statutes were unconstitutional and because the denial of basic constitutional freedoms even for only a brief period constituted irreparable harm.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that denied the motion of plaintiffs, parent and activist taxpayer, for a preliminary injunction. The court ruled that because the statutes at issue violated rights guaranteed by the First Amendment, plaintiffs met the requirements for the grant of the injunctive relief.

 

684 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Queen


No. E002835


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


194 Cal. App. 3d 501; 239 Cal. Rptr. 555; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2062



August 26, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 0CR 11070, Art W. McKinster, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The matter is remanded to the trial court for resentencing in accord with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the sentence imposed by the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) for his conviction for petty theft with a prior conviction of petty theft after his request to be brought to trial under Cal. Penal Code § 1381 was not granted.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged in a county with an offense while he was confined in another county. Defendant filed a request to be brought to trial on the charge, in accordance with Cal. Penal Code § 1381. The authorities did not bring him to trial, but ultimately refiled the charge and obtained a conviction and sentence in the trial court. Defendant sought review of the sentence imposed, contending that he was entitled to presentence custody credits on the basis of his § 1381 demand for trial. The court agreed and remanded the matter to the trial court for resentencing. The court reasoned that § 1381 was beneficial to prisoners because the advantage of having a trial on pending charges while serving an unrelated sentence provided the potential of a shorter sentence. The court ruled that the prosecutor's refusal to comply with § 1381 requests in some instances but not in others due to any of a myriad of reasons created unconstitutional classifications in violation of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I because those afforded the benefits of § 1381 were all part of one class without distinctions and required similar treatment.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the matter to the trial court for resentencing because prosecutor's dissimilar treatment of prisoners who requested the benefits of a statute that afforded trial on offenses unrelated to the convictions on which the prisoners were serving sentences created unconstitutional classifications that violated the equal protection of the laws.

Walker v. Meehan


No. A033077


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


194 Cal. App. 3d 1290; 240 Cal. Rptr. 171; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2131



August 24, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified September 18, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 599198-7, Winton McKibben, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the preliminary injunction is reversed. Appellant is entitled to costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant district attorney sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted respondents', card room owners, request for a preliminary injunction which prevented any interference with the playing of the game of Pai Gow in licensed card rooms on the basis that the manner in which the game was being played was not a banking or percentage game.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, card room owners, received provisional approval to offer the game of Pai Gow after learning of a decision of a superior court in Los Angeles county which determined that the game, when played in a specific manner, was not a banking or percentage game. Appellant district attorney determined that the game as it was being played in respondents' card rooms was an illegal banking and percentage game. The trial court granted respondents' preliminary injunction enjoining appellant from preventing the playing of the game and specifically finding that the game, when played according to the trial court's order, was not a banking or percentage game. The order stated that a rental fee could be collected from a player's winnings, the amount wagered, or for the time that each participant played. The court reversed, agreeing with another appellate court's construction of Cal. Penal Code § 330 that a percentage game was any game in which the house collected money calculated as a portion of the wagers made or sums won in play and did not need to involve house participation in the game, and, thus, the game of Pai Gow as played in respondents' card rooms was illegal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court. The court agreed with another appellate court's construction of the penal statute prohibiting percentage gaming and found that a percentage game was a game in which the house deducted a percentage from the wagers and did not need to involve house participation in the game and, therefore, respondent, card room owners, offer of the game and charge of a rental fee was illegal percentage gaming.

Yates v. Pollock


No. B014640


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


194 Cal. App. 3d 195; 239 Cal. Rptr. 383; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2033



August 19, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, the portion of the opinion certified for publication follows.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 8, 1987, and the Petition of Plaintiffs and Appellants for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied November 12, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SEC 41755, Robert J. Higa, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to provide that plaintiffs are awarded damages in the aggregate amount of $ 353,560. As so modified, it is affirmed. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant surgeon appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered a judgment on a jury verdict in favor of plaintiffs, the spouse and adult children of a decedent, in plaintiffs' wrongful death malpractice action against defendant. Plaintiffs cross-appealed from the order denying defendant's motion for a new trial, which was conditioned upon plaintiffs' acceptance of a reduction of the judgment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, the spouse and adult children of the decedent, filed a wrongful death malpractice action against defendant surgeon arising out of his surgery on the decedent, and plaintiffs obtained a judgment against him. Defendant appealed, contending that the award of noneconomic damages against him was erroneous, and plaintiffs cross-appealed the denial of defendant's motion for a new trial, which was conditioned on plaintiffs' acceptance of a reduction of their judgment. The court agreed with defendant and modified the judgment to reduce plaintiffs' judgment to an amount equal to the decedent's actual economic damages plus $ 250,000, as this was the maximum amount of noneconomic damages available in an action against a health care provider for professional negligence, pursuant to the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act of 1975, Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's judgment to reduce the award of noneconomic damages to plaintiffs, the spouse and adult children of a decedent, in their action against defendant surgeon, holding that the law limited the amount of noneconomic damages that could be awarded in a professional negligence action against a health care provider.

Coursey v. Superior Court


No. C001815


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


194 Cal. App. 3d 147; 239 Cal. Rptr. 365; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2029



August 18, 1987 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgment of contempt is annulled. The attorney's fee award is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother sought review by certiorari of an order of the Superior Court of Sutter County (California) holding her in contempt for willfully violating the terms of a stipulated visitation order. Petitioner argued the contempt adjudication had to be annulled because there was no substantial evidence she had the ability to comply with the order, by forcing the teen to visit her father, or that she willfully disobeyed its commands.


OVERVIEW: In a marital dissolution proceeding, the trial court entered a stipulated visitation order granting father, real party in interest, regular visitation with the parties' 14-year-old daughter. After the teen refused to visit father one day as scheduled, father obtained an order to show cause, and the trial court adjudged petitioner mother in contempt for willfully violating the terms of the order. Petitioner sought review by certiorari, arguing that there was no substantial evidence she had the ability to comply with the order or that she willfully disobeyed its commands. The appeals court annulled the judgment. The court ruled that the burden was on father to prove contempt beyond a reasonable doubt. The court stated that the trial court could not merely assume that petitioner could reasonably compel the teen to visit against the teen's strong wishes because there was no evidence to that effect. Similarly, the court ruled there was no showing that petitioner willfully violated the order. The court ruled that petitioner's mere joining with the teen in a telephone call to petitioner's attorney to inform him that the teen refused visitation was insufficient proof of willful violation.


OUTCOME: The appeals court annulled the judgment holding petitioner mother in contempt for willfully violating the terms of a stipulated visitation order. The court ruled that the trial court could not merely assume that petitioner could reasonably compel the teen to visit father against the teen's strong wishes, without any evidence to that effect.

British Motor Car Distribs. v. New Motor Vehicle Bd.


No. A031921


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


194 Cal. App. 3d 81; 239 Cal. Rptr. 280; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2022



August 17, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On September 10, 1987, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied November 10, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 828884, Roy L. Wonder, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court is reversed with directions to enter judgment in favor of the Board and real party in interest British Motors.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, franchisee and New Motor Vehicle Board, challenged an order from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted respondent franchisor's petition for a writ of mandamus and overturned a decision of appellant board, which had held that respondent had terminated appellant franchisee's dealership without just cause under Cal. Veh. Code § 3060 and had ordered the franchise reinstated .


OVERVIEW: Respondent franchisor terminated appellant franchisee's new car franchise, and appellant challenged the action before appellant New Motor Vehicle Board. Appellant board found that respondent had terminated the franchise without just cause under Cal. Veh. Code § 3060. Respondent petitioned for a writ of mandamus, and the trial court overturned appellant board's decision. On appeal, the court reversed and rendered judgment for appellants, finding that because the dealer members of appellant board took no part in the decision, appellant board's decision was not unconstitutional. The court also found that the trial court incorrectly applied the independent judgment standard and that its determination that respondent had "good cause" to terminate the franchise was erroneous, regardless of the standard applied. Since appellant board's finding that the franchise was "terminated" was supported by substantial evidence, the trial court's finding to the contrary exceeded the proper scope of its review. Because respondent failed to send out appropriate notice, the court said that it failed to trigger the running of the limitations period for appellant franchisee's protest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, finding that the trial court erred in determining that the decision of appellant New Motor Vehicle Board was unconstitutional on the grounds that several of the members were new car dealers. The court also found that the trial court incorrectly applied the independent judgment standard and that it exceeded its scope of review in reweighing the evidence related to the franchise termination.

State of Washington v. Cobb


No. E002886


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


194 Cal. App. 3d 773; 239 Cal. Rptr. 726; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2092



August 14, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 170358, Carver E. Honn, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The statute is constitutional; the judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state of Washington appealed judgment from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California) for the establishment of child support wherein the trial court held the Agnos Child Support Standards Act (Act) of 1984, Cal. Civ. Code § 4727, to be unconstitutional and made an award of child support lower than the minimum provided for in the Act.


OVERVIEW: At a hearing wherein a child support determination was made, the trial court held that the Agnos Child Support Standards Act of 1984 (Act), Cal. Civ. Code § 4727, was violative of equal protection standards, and set the amount of child support below the award dictated by the Act. The Act differentiated between families receiving Aid to Families with Dependent Children (AFDC) grants and those who were not in determining the amount of child support awards. Plaintiff state of Washington, through which the child was drawing AFDC benefits, appealed the judgment. The reviewing court reasoned that in ordinary equal protection cases not involving suspect classification the classification was upheld if it bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state purpose. The maximization of benefits for needy families, minimization of public support burden, and maintenance of stable households for financially impoverished children was a legitimate state interest. The statute was constitutional and judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: Judgment declaring a child support statute unconstitutional and setting a child support award beneath the minimum mandated by the statute was reversed. A statutory classification that differentiated between families receiving government benefits and those who were not bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state purpose of maximizing benefits, minimizing public support, and assisting financially impoverished families who had children.

Rosales v. Huntington-By-The-Sea Mobilehome Park, etc. et al


No. G003345.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three.


193 Cal. App. 3d 1388; 240 Cal. Rptr. 22; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1983



July 31, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review den. October 23, 1987 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 42-81-51, John J. Ryan, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, mobilehome owners, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which determined, in appellants' declaratory judgment action against respondent mobilehome park, that adults-only restrictions in mobilehome parks, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 798.76, did not violate the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 51 et seq., or the state and federal constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Respondent mobilehome park, which had an adults-only rule, directed appellants, mobilehome owners, to move out after appellants had their first child. Appellants brought a declaratory judgment action to determine the rule void. The trial court entered judgment for respondent, and appellants sought review. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court found that in enacting Cal. Civ. Code § 798.76, which authorized mobilehome park managements to restrict residency to adults, the legislature had created an exception to the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 51 et seq., so that § 798.76 could not be in violation of that statute, and that there was no support for the contention that the "adults only" language in § 798.76 was limited to senior citizen housing. The court held that even under a strict scrutiny standard of review, § 798.76 did not violate constitutional principles of due process and equal protection, because the legislature had a sound basis to support its conclusion that a compelling state interest was involved in the sui generis treatment of mobilehome parks.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of respondent mobilehome park, in a declaratory judgment action by appellants, mobilehome owners, to determine that respondent's adults-only rule violated civil rights legislation or the state and federal constitutions, because the legislature had created an exception to the civil rights statute for mobilehome parks and a compelling state interest supported its decision to do so.

Miller v. Board of Medical Quality Assurance


No. C000267


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


193 Cal. App. 3d 1371; 238 Cal. Rptr. 915; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1981



July 31, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 320491, Fred W. Marler, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant board of medical quality assurance challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted plaintiff doctor's writ of mandate, finding that defendant had unlawfully revoked plaintiff's medical license by first ordering his to take a mental examination without giving him a hearing in violation of his due process rights.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff doctor's license to practice medicine was revoked by defendant board of medical quality assurance. A year after his revocation plaintiff filed a petition to reinstate his license. Plaintiff was examined by a psychiatrist who found him able to practice medicine safely but recommended psychotherapy to prevent a recurrence of past problems. Defendant reinstated his license, subject to a five-year probationary period and required him to undergo psychiatric treatment. Plaintiff filed a writ of mandate. The trial court held that defendant had unlawfully revoked plaintiff's medical license by first ordering him to take a mental examination without giving him a hearing in violation of his due process rights. The court reversed and held that plaintiff could not attack the underlying revocation order because his petition for writ of mandate required him to file within 30 days of the revocation of his license under Cal. Gov't Code § 11523. His failure to file within the 30 days was untimely and was barred.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision holding that plaintiff doctor's petition for writ of mandate filed two years after revocation of his medical license was not timely filed within 30 days of the revocation of his license and was barred.

H-Chh Assocs. v. Citizens for Representative Gov't


No. B019051


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


193 Cal. App. 3d 1193; 238 Cal. Rptr. 841; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1968



July 28, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 26, 1987. Hanson (Thaxton), J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted. The Petition of the Plaintiffs and Appellants for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied October 29, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C580408, Jack T. Ryburn and Warren H. Deering, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The order granting a preliminary injunction is reversed; the order granting a temporary restraining order is affirmed. Defendants to have costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant citizen's organization challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted a temporary restraining order and a preliminary injunction to plaintiff property management corporations. Plaintiffs challenged the portion of the order invalidating plaintiffs' rules and regulations proscribing the solicitation of funds from or approaching of plaintiffs' patrons in a downtown shopping center.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted a temporary restraining order and a preliminary injunction to plaintiff property management corporations. Defendant citizen's organization challenged the grant of the orders, and plaintiffs challenged the portion of the order invalidating plaintiffs' rules and regulations proscribing the solicitation of funds from plaintiffs' patrons in plaintiffs' downtown shopping center. The court reversed the grant of the preliminary injunction, holding that a significant part of plaintiffs' application process and by far the majority of the written and unwritten rules were constitutionally defective. The court held that much of plaintiffs' application procedure was overbroad and therefore constitutionally invalid. The court stated that the issue of the temporary restraining order was moot, but the court reached the merits of the argument, holding that the issue was likely to recur and yet evade review. The court held that the temporary restraining order, to the extent it enforced the Christmas holiday ban on petitioning activity, was justified. The court held that the increase in congestion and pedestrian traffic around the Christmas season justified the ban.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of the preliminary injunction to plaintiff property management corporations, against defendant citizen's organization, because plaintiffs' application process was overbroad and unconstitutional. The court affirmed the temporary injunction, to the extent it enforced the Christmas holiday ban on petitioning activity, because the injunction was justified by the increased traffic and congestion of the season.

 

694 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Bennett v. Livermore Unified Sch. Dist.


No. A029496


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


193 Cal. App. 3d 1012; 238 Cal. Rptr. 819; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1918



July 24, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 21, 1987, and Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied October 22, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. H-91312-6, Raymond L. Marsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant school district sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which declared that the inclusion of a religious invocation in its graduation ceremonies was unconstitutional, under both state and federal constitutions pursuant to an action brought by plaintiffs, who were a student and a taxpayer.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, who were a student and a taxpayer, obtained judgment from the superior court declaring unconstitutional, under both state and federal constitutions, the inclusion of a religious invocation in defendant school district's high school graduation ceremonies. The graduation ceremony was a school-sponsored event, held upon school property purchased with tax funds. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment, holding impermissible the inclusion of an invocation at a public school graduation ceremony, finding that it violated both the state and federal constitutions. The court noted that the Supreme Court had long interpreted U.S. Const. amend. I as commanding an absolute separation of church and state. The appellate court used the three-part Lemon test to determine whether defendant's action was a violation: the state action must have a secular purpose, its principal or primary effect must be one that neither advances nor inhibits religion, and it must not foster an excessive entanglement with religion. The also court noted that California had adopted the test. The court concluded that the state action violated the Lemon test.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, declaring that the inclusion of a religious invocation in defendant school district's graduation ceremonies unconstitutional under the state and federal constitutions, in favor of plaintiffs, who were a student and a taxpayer. The court held that the state action violated all three parts of the Lemon test.

County of San Mateo v. Jensen


No. A032125.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One.


193 Cal. App. 3d 932; 238 Cal. Rptr. 701; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1952



July 23, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted October 29, 1987 (S002243)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 269050, James L. Browning, Jr., * Judge.


* Pursuant to Cal. Const., art. VI, § 21.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, parents of minor ward of the court, challenged the judgment by the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which awarded respondent county reimbursement of the costs of care the ward pursuant to Welf. & Inst. Code § 11350, which mandated compulsory reimbursements from appellants of aid to family with dependent children funds that were used to support the ward.


OVERVIEW: Respondent county filed an action against appellants, parents of a minor declared a ward of court who was committed to a group home, for reimbursement of aid to family dependant children (AFDC) funds that were used to support the ward. The trial court awarded respondent reimbursement for the costs of care of the ward pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11350. Appellants challenged the judgment requiring reimbursement of the AFDC funds, alleging it violated equal protection. On appeal the court reversed the judgment, finding that the reimbursement of the AFDC funds by appellants was precluded as it violated the guarantees of the equal protection clause. The commitment of the ward to the group home was for the protection of society, protection of the public, rehabilitation of the ward, and protection of the family under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602. To charge the cost of operation of a state function conducted for public benefit to appellants as one class of society was arbitrary and violated the basic constitutional guarantee of equal protection of law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that awarded respondent county reimbursement of aid to family with dependant children funds that appellants, parents of a ward of the court who was confined to a group home, received for the care of the ward. The reimbursement award violated equal protection as the ward's confinement was to benefit and protect society.

Williams v. Superior Court


No. B016942.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven.


193 Cal. App. 3d 726; 238 Cal. Rptr. 488; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1931



July 14, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Real Party in Interest's petition for review GRANTED October 1, 1987 (See 49 Cal. 3d 736).


PRIOR HISTORY: Williams v. Superior Court, 186 Cal. App. 3d 1373, 223 Cal. Rptr. 700, 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2662 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., Mar. 4, 1986)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner accused murderer challenged the ruling of the lower court (California), that denied his motion to quash the jury venire or for mistrial on the basis that the panels did not represent a fair cross-section of the community. Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition to prevent the lower court from taking further action in his case or issue a writ of mandate directing the lower court to vacate its denial of the motion to quash the venire.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner accused murderer was charged with first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal. Code. § 187. At trial, he challenged each of two jury panels on the basis that neither panel represented a fair cross-section of the community. Petitioner moved to quash the venire or for mistrial, asserting that the Black population which was jury-eligible for that judicial district was unconstitutionally underrepresentative of the total jury-eligible Black population of Los Angeles County. The trial court denied appellant's motions finding that petitioner had failed to make a prima facie case of showing of a significant underrepresentation of a cognizable group. The court found that to make a prima facie showing of a violation of the fair cross-section requirement in the jury venire, a criminal defendant was required to show that the distinctive group in the venire from which jurors were selected was not fair in relation to the percentage of that group living within a 20-mile radius of that court. The court affirmed the trial court's ruling but noted that it was without prejudice to petitioner to refile his motion based on the court's definition of community.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner accused murderer's petition for a writ challenging the trial court's denial of his challenge to the jury venire but allowed petitioner the opportunity to refile his motion, based on the court's definition of community, as petitioner had filed his motion without the benefit of the court's definition of community.

Long Beach Police Officers Ass'n v. City of Long Beach


No. B024791.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four.


193 Cal. App. 3d 457; 238 Cal. Rptr. 333; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1910



July 8, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted October 23, 1987 (S002096)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C613710, Warren H. Deering, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, police officer and the police union, sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellants' petition for writ of mandate to force appellee city to allow all police officers to engage in outside collateral employment under a narrow reading of Cal. Gov't Code § 1126.


OVERVIEW: Appellant police officer was granted a permit by the police department to serve civil process for a local hospital and that permit was subsequently revoked as being in conflict with his police duties under the restrictions in Cal. Gov't Code § 1126. Appellants, police officer and police officer's union, filed a grievance against appellee city and claimed that it imposed additional restrictions which were not included in § 1126. On petition for writ of mandate to the trial court, appellants were denied their requested relief because the trial court found that appellee was allowed to impose any restriction under § 1126 which did not conflict with the California constitution. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the list contained in § 1126 restricted the ability of appellee to impose additional restraints on outside collateral employment by appellants under a plain meaning review of the statute. The court found that § 1126 was not unconstitutional because off duty work by city police was a matter of statewide concern and the fact that § 1126 impinged somewhat on local control was acceptable under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment which denied the petition for writ of mandate sought by appellants, police officer and police union, because the language of the statute imposing limitations on outside collateral employment should have been narrowly interpreted. The court remanded to the trial court to grant appellants' petition for writ of mandate.

 

698 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cal. Ins. Guar. Ass'n v. Liemsakul


No. B020873


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


193 Cal. App. 3d 433; 238 Cal. Rptr. 346; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1908



July 8, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C536605, Carlos E. Velarde, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The appeal is dismissed. Each party to bear respective costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state guarantee association sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declared that it was limited to a credit for the amount of the actual settlement of a claim under Cal. Ins. Code § 1063.2(c)(1) in appellee insured's personal injury action.


OVERVIEW: Appellee insured was involved in a collision with a driver insured by an insolvent insurer. Appellee settled under his own uninsured motorist policy for less than the policy limit. After appellee brought a personal injury action against the driver, appellant state guarantee association sought a declaratory judgment that it was entitled to a credit in the amount of appellee's uninsured motorist policy limit, not the actual settlement value. The trial court declared that appellant was entitled to a credit in the actual settlement amount and, on appeal, the court disagreed, but the appeal was dismissed as moot. The court held that the term "recoverable" as used in Cal. Ins. Code § 1063.2(c)(1), meant the limits of the policy which could have been recovered by appellee, not the amount which he actually recovered. The court found that appellee could not settle with his own insurer for a nominal sum and then recover more from appellant, because that constituted subsidizing appellee's solvent insurer and was contrary to the legislative intent for the statute. The court found that the credit would also apply to the driver and his employer.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed appellant state guarantee association's appeal of a declaratory judgment in favor of appellee insured because the parties settled the underlying personal injury action and the matter was moot.

 

699 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Colvin v. Southern Cal. Edison Co.


No. B017139


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six.


193 Cal. App. 3d 345; 238 Cal. Rptr. 322; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1899



July 3, 1987 


NOTICE:   [**1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing granted July 28, 1987 (See 194 Cal. App. 3d 1306)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 79724, Allan L. Steele, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant electric company sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which granted a new trial to appellee minor after it found that the jury was erroneously instructed on immunity in appellee's action based upon personal injuries received when he was electrocuted.


OVERVIEW: Appellee minor was electrocuted when he grabbed a guy wire attached to a pole owned by appellant electric company while taking a short cut across a creek to a friend's house. Appellant brought suit against appellant for the resulting personal injuries. The jury found that appellant had immunity under Cal. Civ. Code § 846, but the trial court granted a new trial to appellee because it found it had erroneously instructed the jury on the issue of immunity, because there was insufficient evidence to find that the accident occurred on "premises" under § 846. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the "nonpossessory" interest under § 846 included appellant's easement for placing its poles on the owner's property and that, therefore, appellee was on appellant's "premises" as required by § 846. The court further held that the area on which appellant had an easement was suitable for recreational use because it was open and accessible to anyone hiking or biking and was, therefore, subject to the immunity provisions of § 846. The court found that the immunity instruction to the jury by the trial court was proper.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of a new trial to appellee minor on his personal injury action because the trial court's immunity instruction to the jury with regard to appellant electric company was proper under the language of the statute.

 

700 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Gilbert v. Superior Court


No. F008197


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


193 Cal. App. 3d 161; 238 Cal. Rptr. 220; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1880



June 30, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' Application for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied August 19, 1987.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of mandate is denied, and the stay order is dissolved. Costs to real parties in interest.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner community care givers sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent court to require two centers to obey subpoenas duces tecum to produce for inspection and copying by petitioners' attorney certain confidential patient records protected by Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 5328, 4514.


OVERVIEW: Department of Social Services accused petitioner community caregivers of mistreatment of certain patients and sought revocation of their license. Petitioners obtained several subpoenas duces tecum directed to regional centers that were custodians of confidential records of patients who petitioners claim were former patients in their facility. The centers advised that these records were confidential and would not be produced. The lower court ruled against petitioners' writ. Petitioners sought further relief. The court denied the petition for a writ of mandate. The court held that under the facts, the trial court properly determined that the records were not accessible to petitioners. The court found that under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5328, the records were intended to be absolutely confidential except for the specifically listed cases not applicable to petitioners. The court also found that the exception under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 4514(f) authorized that the records be made available to the courts, not to an administrative body through the courts.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for writ of mandate and dissolved the stay order, because petitioner community care givers' request for the records did not fall under any statutory exception to the confidentiality standard of these records. The court awarded costs to the Department of Social Services.

San Marcos Mobilehome Park Owners' Ass'n v. City of San Marcos


No. D003736


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


192 Cal. App. 3d 1492; 238 Cal. Rptr. 290; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1872



June 29, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III, IV and V.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modification of Opinion July 9, 1987. Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 23, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N18966, Fiorenzo V. Lopardo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the decisions of the Commission are reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city and city council, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which reversed a rent review commission's decisions concerning respondent mobilehome park owners's request for rent increases. The trial court also deleted portions of appellants' rent control ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, city and city council, adopted a rent control ordinance for mobilehome parks. Respondents mobilehome park owners requested rent increases from the rent review commission; the commission denied one increase and granted another. Respondents, owners' association and residents, challenged the facial constitutional validity of the rent control ordinance and respondent owners challenged the decisions of the commission. In a writ of mandate proceeding under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5, the trial court found that the commission's decisions were not supported by substantial evidence and also ordered certain portions of the ordinance deleted. Appellants sought review of the reversal of the commission's determinations. On appeal, the court and reinstated the commission's decisions. The court found that the rent increase did not involve an administrative decision that substantially affected fundamental rights and therefore the substantial evidence standard applied. Upon review the court held that the commission's findings were supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and reinstated the rent review commission's decisions because, although the trial court correctly applied the substantial evidence test which was applicable because the commission's decisions did not involve or substantially affect a fundamental vested right, the trial court erred in concluding that the commission's findings were not supported by substantial evidence.

Anderson v. San Francisco Rent Stabilization & Arbitration Bd.


No. A031219


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


192 Cal. App. 3d 1336; 237 Cal. Rptr. 894; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1859



June 25, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 24, 1987. Kline, P. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 29, 1987. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 829116, Roy L. Wonder, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment granting peremptory writ of mandate is reversed. Appellants shall recover costs on appeal ( Cal. Rules of Court, rule 26).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city rent board and landlords, sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which held that appellant rent board abused its discretion and acted contrary to law by calculating the rent increase allowed under the Rehabilitation Assistance Program to include increased monthly loan payments unrelated to the rehabilitation of the property.


OVERVIEW: The city's residential rehabilitation loan program provided property owners in designated areas with low-interest loans to rehabilitate residential property. Appellant landlords were allowed to pass through to tenants, under a prescribed rent formula, increased monthly loan payments and payments unrelated to rehabilitation of the property. Respondent tenants objected to a notice of rent increase. Rent board denied their appeal. Respondents petitioned to superior court for a writ of mandate, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5(b). The trial court granted a peremptory writ. The court found that the rent board had always allowed pass-throughs of preexisting debt service, not just rehabilitation-related loan payments, and that borrowers like appellant landlords had obviously relied on being able to pass through their mortgage debt. The court stated that its decision was based on compelling practical considerations. The success of the program depended on owners using the loan. Unless they would be able to pass through their costs, They would refrain from using the program. The judgment of the trial court in granting the writ was reversed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court granting peremptory writ of mandate was reversed because the rent board was not acting contrary to law in allowing the pass-through of costs and there was no abuse of discretion by the agency.

Simon v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Bd.


No. A033825


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


193 Cal. App. 3d 1076; 238 Cal. Rptr. 589; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1925



June 25, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 27, 1987, and Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied September 16, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 274160, David A. Dolgin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and the order awarding attorney fees, and each, are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which granted respondent claimant's writ of mandate and ordered appellant to pay disability pay benefits to respondent.


OVERVIEW: Respondent claimant was injured after she quit her job to open her own business. Respondent filed for unemployment pay benefits based on payments made by her previous job. Appellant, California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board, denied her appeal of the denial of her benefits by an administrative law judge because she was self-employed for profit. The trial court granted respondent's writ of mandate and ordered appellant to pay her disability pay benefits and attorney fees. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and held that because respondent was self-employed she earned profits and not wages as required by Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code §§ 2601, 140.5. The court held that under Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 708.5, respondent could have elected to be covered by the system if she had made her own contributions. The court held that because respondent chose not to contribute, earned profits, and had freely withdrawn from the workforce, she was not covered at the time of her disability.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the wit of mandate that ordered Appellant, Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board, to pay unemployment pay benefits to respondent claimant. The court held that respondent was not covered because she was self-employed for profit at the time of her injury.

Flores v. Natividad Medical Ctr.


Nos. A020434, A022374, A024871


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


192 Cal. App. 3d 1106; 238 Cal. Rptr. 24; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1841



June 22, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rules 976 and 976.1, California Rules of Court, the portion of the opinion certified for publication follows.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 76426, Ralph M. Drummond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it applies the limitations of MICRA to the State of California and insofar as it provides for periodic payments. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed. The cause is remanded to the superior court for modification of the judgment consistent with the views expressed in this opinion. Plaintiff is awarded his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate and appellees, the state, a medical center, and doctors, brought cross appeals challenging the order of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which applied the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2(b), to strike a portion of the general damages awarded to appellant, and granted appellees' motion to provide for periodic payments under § 3333.2(b), and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 667.7.


OVERVIEW: Appellant inmate filed an action for damages against appellees, the state, a county, a medical center, and doctors, for injuries he sustained while incarcerated. After verdict was rendered for appellant, the trial court applied the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act (MICRA), Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2(b), and struck part of appellant's general damages award and granted appellees' motion to provide for periodic payments pursuant to MICRA and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 667.7. Both parties appealed; appellant asserted that the MICRA did not apply to appellee state, that he was denied equal protection by its application, and periodic payments should not have been allowed. Appellees asserted that the trial court erred when it refused to instruct the jury on superseding cause and prison medical standards. The court reversed as to the application of MICRA to the state and the order allowing periodic payments by appellees, affirmed the rest, and remanded for modification of the judgment. The court held that MICRA was constitutional, did not apply to appellant's cause of action for failure to summon medical aid against appellee state, and was not invited or waived as error by appellant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed portion of trial court's judgment that applied the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act (MICRA) to limit the award against appellee state and allowed for periodic payments from appellees, the state, a county, a medical center, and doctors, because MICRA was constitutional, did not apply to appellant inmate's cause of action against appellee state, and was not invited or waived as error by appellant.

 

705 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Markarian


No. B020558.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven.


204 Cal. App. 3d 52; 237 Cal. Rptr. 707; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1817



June 15, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Respondent's petition for review GRANTED October 1, 1987.
Submission of additional briefing, otherwise required by rule 29.3, California Rules of Court, is deferred pending further order of the court.
Cause dismissed September 15, 1988 and remanded to Ct. App., 2d Dist., Div. 7 ( rule 29.4(c), Cal. Rules of Ct.)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A702235, David D. Perez, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of October 1, 1987, previously reported at 192 Cal. App. 3d 827.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of first degree murder of his father-in-law and the attempted murder of his mother-in-law. The trial court also convicted defendant of assault with a deadly weapon on his wife.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with first degree murder, attempted murder, and assault with a deadly weapon. A jury instruction provided that the jury must assume that defendant was of sound mind at the time of his alleged conduct. The jury found defendant guilty of all the charges and defendant was convicted of the charges. Defendant challenged the murder and attempted murder convictions. Upon appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the jury instruction was improper where the jury could have interpreted the instruction to mean defendant was required to prove his claim of diminished capacity. The court reasoned that it was the state's burden to show that defendant possessed the required state of mind when he committed the overt acts and that requiring defendant to prove diminished capacity was an unconstitutional shifting of the burden of proof of intent. The court held that the error was prejudicial where the instruction required the jury to assume defendant was of sound mind unless he proved contrary and the presumption directly affected the only element of the offenses that was in doubt and a reasonable juror could have entertained reasonable doubt as to intent.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions of first degree murder and attempted murder. The court held that a jury instruction was improper where the jury could have interpreted the instruction to require defendant to prove his claim of diminished mental capacity and that interpretation would have unconstitutionally removed the burden from the state to prove the required element of intent.

City v. Eller Outdoor Adver.


Nos. A028919, A029026


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


192 Cal. App. 3d 643; 237 Cal. Rptr. 815; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1802



June 11, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 10, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 722735 and 722737, William E. Mullins and Roy L. Wonder, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to grant the City's application for injunctive relief in a manner consistent with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought injunctive relief against respondent, billboard companies, in two consolidated actions in the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which dismissed its action to compel removal of advertising signs by respondents for failure to bring the causes to trial within the mandatory period under former Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 583(b).


OVERVIEW: Appellant city sought to compel respondent, billboard companies, to remove its general advertising signs located within a specific district in violation of San Francisco Planning Code § 601 et seq. The trial court dismissed the action for failure to bring the cause to trial within the mandatory period of former Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 583(b). The court reversed the judgment and remanded the cause to the trial court for injunctive relief against respondents. The court addressed respondents argument that the ordinance violated the U.S. Const. amend. I's freedom of expression and held that the trial court erred in failing to use the proper balancing test, that when impairment of this right appears, a court must weigh the extent of the impairment against both the importance of the governmental interest and the substantiality of the threat which the forbidden speech or related activity poses to that interest. The court found that the sign ordinance was a legitimate exercise of police power controlling time, place, and manner of expression in a limited, constitutionally permissible fashion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision against appellant city which had refused injunctive relief against respondent billboard companies. The court found the ordinances referring to placement of signs was a legitimate exercise of police power that controlled the time, place, and manner of expression in a constitutional fashion.

Altadena Library Dist. v. Bloodgood


No. B010487


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


192 Cal. App. 3d 585; 237 Cal. Rptr. 649; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1797



June 10, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C520919, Norman L. Epstein, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying a writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff library district sought writ of mandate against defendant county auditor in its challenge of the constitutionality of Proposition 13's, Cal. Const. art. XIII(a), §4, requirement that at least two-thirds of the voters in a district approve new taxes levied.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner library district sought a writ of mandate against respondent county auditor when it challenged the constitutionality of Proposition 13's, Cal. Const. art. XIII (a), §4, requirement that a supermajority approve new taxes levied. Proposition 13 substantially reduced the property tax revenues available to governments in the State of California. Petitioner claimed an exemption from Proposition 13's supermajority requirement on the grounds that the library district was not a special district within the meaning of the proposition and petitioner was not government by the supermajority requirement. Petitioner also alleged that the supermajority requirement should have been scrutinized because it interfered with the district's fundamental right to education guaranteed by the California Constitution. The court denied the writ of mandate and rejected both contentions. The court held that the library district was a special district which sought to impose a special tax on property within the meaning of Proposition 13 and the supermajority requirement of Proposition 13 was constitutionally imposed on a library district which supplied educational services.


OUTCOME: The judgment which denied a writ of mandate to petitioner library district was affirmed because a proposition that required supermajority requirement was applicable to petitioner because petitioner was a special district which sought to impose a special tax. The proposition was held to be constitutional as applied to petitioner, who supplied educational services.
