Healy v. Onstott


No. H001639


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


192 Cal. App. 3d 612; 237 Cal. Rptr. 540; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2084



June 10, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 30, 1987, and respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 26, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 91817, William M. Kelsay, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed. Costs on appeal to appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant property owner challenged an order from the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which denied defendant a trial and instead employed the confirmation procedure of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1285 et seq. The trial court heard argument on the merits of plaintiff contributing co-owners' suit to recover maintenance costs for a private right-of- way, confirmed the award of an arbitrator, and rendered a judgment against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff contributing co-owners applied with the trial court to obtain an impartial arbitrator to apportion costs associated with the maintenance of a private right-of-way pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 845. The trial court appointed an arbitrator who held a hearing and rendered a decision that apportioned liability for the maintenance costs among the co-owners equally, which included defendant property owner. The trial court, without a trial de novo, confirmed the arbitrator's award. Upon review, the court reversed. The court found the confirmation arbitration procedure under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1286.2, 1286.6, did not apply to arbitration under Cal. Civ. Code § 845 because those sections only applied to voluntary arbitration pursuant to an agreement. The court held that under Cal. Civ. Code § 845 defendant was afforded the right to a trial de novo because fewer than all co-owners of the private right-of-way accepted the arbitrator's award apportioning maintenance costs. The court found that § 845 entitled defendant to a trial before a judge and that it did not afford the right to a jury where the request for contribution was an equitable action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that denied defendant property owner a trial de novo in a suit to recover maintenance costs for a private right-of-way filed by plaintiff contributing co-owners. The court held that defendant was entitled to a hearing pursuant to a statute because fewer than all of the co-owners of the right-of-way agreed to the apportionment of maintenance costs determined by an appointed arbitrator.

 

709 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Prevatt v. Pennwalt Corp.


No. B006570.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six.


192 Cal. App. 3d 438; 237 Cal. Rptr. 488; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1783; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P11,499



June 4, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn. June 24, 1987; Appellant's petition for review DENIED October 1, 1987 and In light of Abbott Ford v. Superior Court (1987) 43 Cal. 3d 858, Reporter of Decisions is directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).
Kaufman, J., is of the opinion the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, Nos. SM36246, SM36816, SM37230, Bruce W. Dodds, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant corporation appealed consolidated judgments of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which found in favor of plaintiff employees in an action for personal injuries arising from a caustic chemical explosion.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employees were injured in a caustic chemical explosion involving defendant corporation's industrial chemicals. Defendant claimed "sliding scale recovery agreements" entered into with the other defendants were unconstitutional and denied it equal protection and due process, that the trial court failed to follow procedural requirements established by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 877.6 for good faith settlement, and the trial court failed to apply the good faith criteria in approving the settlements. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court regarding five of the six settlements, holding the legislative scheme for sliding scale recovery agreements did not interfere with equal protection considerations. The parties that entered into the agreements were required to promptly inform the court and any jury. The court reversed one of the settlements because there was no notice to defendant and its existence was unknown but moments before its approval. The court remanded that settlement agreement back to the trial court for consideration in conformity with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 877.6.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of plaintiff employees because the sliding scale recovery agreements were not unconstitutional and did not deny defendant corporation equal protection or due process of the law. The court remanded one agreement that was unknown only moments before the hearing because it violated the requirement that all such agreements were made known promptly to the court.

 

710 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Demetrius H.


No. H002001.


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


192 Cal. App. 3d 337; 237 Cal. Rptr. 380; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1773



June 1, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellant's petition for review DENIED September 24, 1987 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Oficial Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 90670, John Pasco, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant minor appealed from the order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which declared him a ward of the juvenile court and committed him to a rehabilitation facility for committing a battery on school grounds under Cal. Penal Code § 243.2, and claimed that the trial court incorrectly used the clear proof standard under Cal. Penal Code § 26 to determine his mental capacity, rather than beyond a reasonable doubt.


OVERVIEW: Defendant minor was declared a ward of the juvenile court and committed to a rehabilitation facility for 120 days as a result of being charged under Cal. Penal Code § 594(b)(2) for malicious damage to property, under Cal. Penal Code § 12020(a) for carrying a concealed dirk or dagger, and under Cal. Penal Code § 243.2 for committing a battery on school grounds. Only the battery count was sustained. Defendant argued that the standard used at trial to determine his mental capacity should have been beyond a reasonable doubt. The court disagreed, affirmed the trial court's decision, and held that capacity of a minor to commit a crime within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 26 was not an element of the crime and was not within the limited circumstances in which the reasonable doubt requirement applied to facts not identified as elements. The due process clause of the federal constitution did not require abandonment of the clear proof standard. The court held that the testimony at trial, the evidence of defendant's age, and the written and verbal instructions he received amply supported the trial court's finding that defendant knew it was wrong to fight at the time of the offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, which declared defendant minor a ward of the juvenile court, because defendant's criminal capacity was not a separate element of the crimes charged and the policy arguments provided no independent basis for judicially annulling the clear proof standard set forth by the legislature. Thus, the trial court correctly applied the clear proof standard in its determination of defendant's mental capacity.

 

711 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Times Mirror Co. v. City of L.A.


No. B023000


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


192 Cal. App. 3d 170; 237 Cal. Rptr. 346; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1761; 14 Media L. Rep. 1289



May 28, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 2, 1987. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C511941, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff newspapers appealed the summary judgment entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in favor of defendant city in plaintiffs' action that challenged the validity, on constitutional grounds, of a business tax imposed by defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff newspapers challenged, on constitutional grounds, the validity of a business tax collected by defendant city. Defendant was awarded summary judgment, and plaintiffs appealed. The court found that there was no constitutional problem with the tax and affirmed the judgment. The court noted that although the government could not unduly burden free speech through taxation or other regulatory measures, it was beyond dispute that the federal constitution did not exempt newspapers from generally applicable regulations and taxes. The court further stated that a compelling state interest was not needed to collect such a tax as long as newspapers were treated no differently than others who were similarly situated. The tax collected by defendant was applicable to those engaged in wholesale or retail business activities and, therefore, was a generally applicable tax that presented U.S. Const. amend. I problems. Furthermore, the various classifications and rates within the tax were neither arbitrary nor unreasonable. Finally, the court held that the tax provided clear guidelines for its administration, thus, it could not be considered an impermissible delegation of legislative duties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment awarded to defendant city because the business tax, which was challenged by plaintiff newspapers on First Amendment grounds, had broad application, and the various rates and classifications contained within it were neither arbitrary nor unreasonable. The court also stated that the tax was not an impermissible delegation of legislative authority.

Ross v. City of Rolling Hills Estates


No. B022314


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


192 Cal. App. 3d 370; 238 Cal. Rptr. 561; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1776



May 19, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 29, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SWC 83320, Abraham Gorenfeld, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondents to recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant homeowners sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their application for a writ of mandate compelling respondents, city, city council, and planning commission, to approve their building permit.


OVERVIEW: Appellant homeowners sought a writ of mandate to compel respondents, city, city council (council), and planning commission (commission), to approve their building permit. The trial court denied their petition. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. It found that respondent city's view protection ordinance was not too vague because a reasonably certain standard in light of the need for view protection could be determined. The court also found that the record did not reflect that respondent city relied on criteria contained in future zoning regulations to deny appellants' proposal. The court held that by affirming respondent commission's decision, respondent council adopted the commission's findings, which were sufficient to apprise the parties of the basis for the council's action. The court concluded that substantial evidence in the record supported respondents' findings of significant view impairment and of failure to mitigate the impact on existing views.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court denying appellant homeowners' petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondents, city, planning commission, and city council, to approve appellant's building permit. It found that respondent city's view protection ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague and that respondents' decision to deny appellants' building permit was supported by substantial evidence.

 

713 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



County of El Dorado v. Schneider


No. C000070


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


191 Cal. App. 3d 1263; 237 Cal. Rptr. 51; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1719



May 13, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rule 976.1 of the California Rules of Court, all portions of this opinion shall be published except parts I through VIII of the Discussion.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for rehearing was denied June 8, 1987, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 29, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of El Dorado County, No. 42934, William E. Byrne and Charles F. Fogerty, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant father appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of El Dorado County (California), declaring defendant to be the father in an action instituted by plaintiffs, the county, the mother, and the child. The trial court determined that Cal. Evid. Code § 892 authorized it to determine the paternity issue, and it awarded child support and arrearages.


OVERVIEW: Defendant father refused to take a blood test ordered by the trial court in an action brought by plaintiffs, the county, the mother, and the child, to establish paternity. The trial court applied Cal. Evid. Code § 892 to determine that he was the father. On appeal, the court held that the trial court's application of § 892 to determine paternity did not violate defendant's constitutional rights. Defendant did not prove that his rights under U.S. Const. amend. I were violated. Defendant was not wrongly denied a jury trial because U.S. Const. amend VI did not provide a right to a trial by jury in a paternity action which was civil in nature, and neither did U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Likewise, defendant did not have a right to a jury trial under U.S. Const. amend. VII because that right had not been incorporated into the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and was not binding on the states. The paternity determination did not violate due process because the trial court's sanction met a two prong test: first, it was "just;" and second, ordering the blood tests was related to the particular "claim" in the discovery order, namely, evidence to prove defendant was the father.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the entire judgment for plaintiffs, the county, the mother, and the child. The court held that defendant father's federal and state constitutional rights were not violated when the court imposed the statutory sanction declaring him to be the father after he refused a court ordered blood test.

 

714 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



William Dal Porto & Sons, Inc. v. Agricultural Labor Relations Bd.


No. C000206


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


191 Cal. App. 3d 1195; 237 Cal. Rptr. 206; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1714; 123 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P57,134



May 12, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied June 8, 1987, and the petitions of respondent and real party in interest for review by the Supreme Court were denied July 29, 1987.



DISPOSITION: Let a decree issue setting aside and remanding to the Board the make-whole portion of the order under review for further proceedings consistent with the views set forth in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer challenged a remedial order issued by respondent California Agricultural Labor Relations Board (board), arguing that the board's make-whole remedy under Cal. Lab. Code § 1160.3, could not have been imposed in the absence of a finding that petitioner and real party in interest union would have entered into a collective bargaining agreement but for petitioner's bad faith bargaining.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court remanded the case to respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board (board) in order for the board to make findings as to whether petitioner employer and real party in interest union would have consummated a collective bargaining agreement in the absence of petitioner's unlawful refusal to bargain. The court found that consummation of a collective bargaining agreement was implied in Cal. Lab. Code § 1160.3 as a prerequisite for application of the make-whole remedy. The test which the board was to apply was whether, but for petitioner's unlawful refusal to bargain, the parties would have concluded a collective bargaining agreement. On remand, the board was to apply a rebuttable presumption, affecting the burden of proof that such an agreement would have been consummated had the parties bargained in good faith. Good faith bargaining was defined as such bargaining that led to either a contract or a bona fide impasse (a deadlock). A legally cognizable impasse could not exist if the deadlock in negotiations was due to the failure of one of the parties to bargain in good faith.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case to respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board to allow it to make a finding as to whether petitioner employer and real party in interest union would have consummated a collective bargaining agreement providing for higher pay for employees in the absence of petitioner's unlawful refusal to bargain. The court upheld the board on all other challenges made by petitioner.

 

715 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Prudential Ins. Co. v. City and County of San Francisco


No. A030845


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


191 Cal. App. 3d 1142; 236 Cal. Rptr. 869; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1711



May 12, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 10, 1987, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 29, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 810306, Richard Best, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The superior court's grant of summary judgment, insofar as it (1) awards Prudential $ 9,996. 12 as a refund for taxes paid, and (2) awards Prudential attorney fees pursuant to section 5152, is affirmed. That part of the judgment awarding Prudential $ 49,503. 88 as a refund for taxes paid is reversed, and the court is directed to remand the matter to the Assessment Appeals Board for a determination of the hotel's value, as explained herein. Respondent is awarded costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a city and county, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent taxpayer and awarded respondent a refund for taxes paid and attorney fees based on an overvaluation of respondent's property.


OVERVIEW: Respondent taxpayer and appellants, a city and county, disputed the amount of real property taxes owed on a hotel purchased by respondent. Appellants assessed the value of the hotel based on the purchase price. After the Assessment Appeal Board (AAB) denied his appeal, respondent filed a complaint for refund of real property taxes paid asserting that appellants had overvalued the hotel by failing to account for the below-market interest rate loan which respondent assumed from the bank as required by Cal. Code Regs. tit. 18, § 4. The trial court granted summary judgment and awarded respondent a refund for taxes paid and attorney fees. On appeal, the court affirmed in part holding that the debt at a below-market interest rate was to be discounted to its cash equivalent value to determine the value for purposes of property tax based on tit. 18, § 4 and the legal definitions of fair market value. The court held that attorney fees were properly awarded under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 5152. The court reversed the refund award and directed the trial court to remand the case to the AAB for resolution of factual questions related to the discounting and determination of the hotel's value.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of respondent taxpayer because the below-market interest rate loan was to be discounted to its cash equivalent by appellants, a city and county, in assessing property value. The court reversed the tax refund award and instructed the trial court to remand the matter to the Assessment Appeals Board to determine the value in light of the discounting.

 

716 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Michael H. v. Gerald D.


Nos. B015384, B018241


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


191 Cal. App. 3d 995; 236 Cal. Rptr. 810; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1699



May 7, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied June 2, 1987, and the petitions of all appellants for review by the Supreme Court were denied July 30, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CF 022753, Stephen M. Lachs, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Summary judgment is affirmed. The case is remanded to the trial court so that it may determine, and make appropriate orders with regard to, the attorneys' fees and costs, if any, to which Victoria D. is entitled. The parties are to bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff putative father and cross-complainant, minor child, sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment against plaintiff, in an action brought by plaintiff against defendants, legal father, mother, and minor child (cross-complainant), to establish that he was cross-complainant's biological father, and to establish a father/child relationship with her.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff putative father filed a reverse paternity action against defendants, legal father, mother, and minor child (cross-complainant), to establish that he was the biological father of cross-complainant, and establish a parental relationship with her. Defendant legal father filed a motion for summary judgment contending that there was no triable issue as to the application of the conclusive presumption of Cal. Evid. Code § 621(a). The trial court granted the motion, and on appeal, the court affirmed the grant of summary judgment, but remanded for determination as to whether cross-complainant was entitled to attorney fees and costs. Applying a balancing test, the court concluded that plaintiff and cross-complainant were not denied due process because their interests were outweighed by the state's interest in upholding the integrity of the family. The court rejected cross-complainant's equal protection claim, as § 621 did not bar a suit by a child to establish paternity. Finally, the court held that defendant legal father was not estopped from asserting the conclusive presumption of § 621, and that the law did not recognize both a legal and de facto father of the same child.


OUTCOME: The grant of summary judgment was affirmed because there was no triable issue as to the application of the conclusive presumption that the child of a married woman cohabiting with her husband, who is not sterile or impotent, was the child of the marriage, and plaintiff putative father and cross-complainant, minor child, were not denied due process because the state's interest in upholding the integrity of the family outweighed their interests.

People v. Harris


No. B016657


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


191 Cal. App. 3d 819; 236 Cal. Rptr. 680; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1683



May 1, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Writ denied by, Stay vacated by People v. Superior Court (Harris), 217 Cal. App. 3d 1332, 266 Cal. Rptr. 563, 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 115 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., 1990)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A018568, Richard F. Charvat, Judge.
People v. Harris, 36 Cal. 3d 36, 201 Cal. Rptr. 782, 679 P.2d 433, 1984 Cal. LEXIS 174 (1984)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sentenced him to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole for his conviction on burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 459, two counts of robbery, § 211, and two counts of murder, § 187, with special circumstances, former §§ 190.2(c)(3) and (c)(5), after he was prohibited from testifying pursuant to § 1018.


OVERVIEW: In seeking review of the judgment that sentenced him to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole for his conviction on burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 459, two counts of robbery, § 211, and two counts of murder, § 187, with special circumstances, former §§ 190.2(c)(3) and (c)(5), defendant claimed he had been denied his constitutional right to testify on his own behalf, and that the trial judge should not have granted a motion under § 1018 that prohibited him from testifying. The court reversed the conviction, holding that the trial court should not have relied on § 1018 because defendant was not attempting to enter a plea, but was merely asserting his right to be heard in his own behalf. Even if his oral declarations constituted a complete confession, once defendant was advised of his legal rights, it was his decision, and not that of his attorney or the court, as to whether he testified.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that sentenced defendant to life imprisonment for his conviction on charges of burglary, robbery and murder with special circumstances because he had been denied his constitutional right to testify on his own behalf at his trial.

In re Baby Girl M.


No. D003076.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One.


191 Cal. App. 3d 786; 236 Cal. Rptr. 660; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1681



April 30, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn. May 21, 1987; Review denied July 30, 1987 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. A24905, Judith McConnell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant father appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which terminated his parental rights with respect to his five-year-old daughter.


OVERVIEW: When defendant father learned of the birth of his daughter, he contacted plaintiff department of social services and asked that his daughter be placed with the family then providing day care for his sons. The mother rejected defendant's placement request and formally relinquished the daughter for adoption. A petition to terminate defendant's parental rights pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 7017 was filed. The trial court found that an award of custody would be detrimental to the child and the award to a nonparent was required to serve the best interests of the child. On appeal, the court affirmed holding that the detriment standard of Cal. Civ. Code § 4600 precluded the trial court from merely comparing defendant with the prospective adoptive parents and choosing the placement which the court believed was best because where there was demonstrable harm to the child if placed with the parent, that placement was detrimental within the meaning of § 4600 and an alternative placement with nonparents was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the termination of defendant father's parental rights holding that the trial court was precluded from merely comparing defendant with the prospective adoptive parents and choosing the placement which the court believed was best.

 

719 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hernandez v. Municipal Court


No. B022421.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four.


203 Cal. App. 3d 949; 237 Cal. Rptr. 590; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1630



April 28, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted July 23, 1987 (See 49 Cal. 3d 713)


PRIOR HISTORY: This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of April 28, 1987, previously reported at 191 Cal. App. 3d 593.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner accused sought a writ of mandate regarding an order by respondent Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which denied petitioner's vicinage motion that attacked the jury draw in the criminal action against him pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. VI.


OVERVIEW: A criminal complaint was filed against petitioner accused. On the last day on which the case could have been brought to trial before becoming subject to mandatory dismissal, the case was transferred and respondent court denied petitioner's vicinage motion that challenged the jury draw pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. VI. Respondent held that the vicinage provision merely required that jurors be drawn from the same judicial district in which petitioner was tried. On appeal, the court reversed and issued a peremptory writ of mandate that directed respondent to vacate its order. The court held that petitioner was entitled to a jury drawn from a panel which included jurors that resided in the geographic area where the alleged crime occurred pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. VI's guarantee of a representative jury. Thus, the transfer of petitioner's case to a municipal branch court where the jury draw did not include residents of the area of the crime violated petitioner's U.S. Const. amend. VI right. The court stated that administrative inconvenience or even hardship had to, on balance, give way to petitioner's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of petitioner accused's vicinage motion and issued a peremptory writ of mandate vacating the order. The court held that because petitioner was entitled to a jury drawn from a jury panel that included jurors who resided in the geographic area where the alleged crime occurred, the transfer of the case to a court where the jury draw did not include such residents violated petitioner's Sixth Amendment right.

 

720 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Clothesrigger, Inc. v. GTE Corp.


No. D004758


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


191 Cal. App. 3d 605; 236 Cal. Rptr. 605; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1631



April 28, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 23, 1987. Lucas, C. J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 515519, Mack P. Lovett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying modification of class definition and deeming moot the motion to amend the complaint is reversed. The matter is remanded to the superior court for further proceedings in accord with this opinion. Appellant to have costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff customer appealed an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied his motion to modify the class definition to certify a nationwide plaintiff class and deemed moot his motion to amend the complaint, in plaintiff's action against defendant corporations alleging that defendants charged subscribers to their long distance service for unanswered long distance calls without disclosure.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff customer filed a class action against defendant corporations for fraud, misrepresentation, and unfair business practices, alleging that defendants charged customers of their long distance telephone service for certain unanswered long distance calls without disclosure. Plaintiff filed a motion to modify the definition of the certified class to include all customers nationwide, not just California residents, and a motion to amend the complaint. The trial court denied the motions. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the trial court made erroneous legal assumptions and used improper criteria for the class certification. The court found that in order for California to have exercised its jurisdiction over the claims of nonresident plaintiffs, California must have had significant contact to the claims asserted by each member of the class to ensure that the choice of law was not arbitrary. The court found that the trial court had not determined whether California law had applied to the claims of the proposed nationwide class members who were not residents of California. The court remanded for the trial court to determine plaintiff's motions under the appropriate analysis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order denying modification of class definition and deeming moot the motion to amend complaint in plaintiff customer's action against defendant corporations for charging customers for certain unanswered long distance telephone calls. The court held that the trial court's denial of plaintiff's motion for modification of the class was based upon improper criteria and erroneous legal assumptions.

 

721 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Guthrie


No. G003074


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


191 Cal. App. 3d 654; 236 Cal. Rptr. 583; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1668



April 28, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 27, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. D174568, James L. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The order is reversed and the matter is remanded to the trial court for a determination whether the family home award to which the parties stipulated was intended as additional child support. If the court finds the award was so intended, it may proceed to hear and determine the merits of James's order to show cause re modification.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex husband sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied appellant's motion to modify the parties' divorce settlement with respect to the sale of the family residence pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.7.


OVERVIEW: Upon dissolution of their marriage, appellant ex husband and respondent ex wife executed a settlement agreement that was incorporated in the judgment of dissolution of marriage. The agreement provided for use of the family home by respondent subject to certain contingencies. Upon an alleged change in circumstances affecting the economic status of the parties and their children, appellant applied for a motion to modify the judgment pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.7. The trial court denied appellant's motion. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded because the trial court's jurisdiction to modify a home award based on an agreement of the parties entered into before the enactment of § 4800.7 depended upon whether the award was intended as additional child support. If so, then the award was modifiable; if not, then § 4800.7 was no authority for the impingement of contractual rights. On remand, the trial court was instructed to determine whether the home award was intended as additional child support.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of appellant ex-husband's motion for modification and remanded, holding that the trial court's jurisdiction to modify a home award based on the parties' agreement entered prior to enactment of the relevant statute depended on whether the award was intended as additional child support. If so, the award was modifiable; if not, the relevant statute was no authority for the impingement of contractual rights.

 

722 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Aronson v. Superior Court


Nos. H001837, H001848


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


191 Cal. App. 3d 294; 236 Cal. Rptr. 347; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1603



April 22, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 12, 1987, and the petition of real party in interest for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 29, 1987.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of mandate issue to the Superior Court of Santa Clara County directing it to vacate its orders overruling petitioners' demurrers and instead to sustain said demurrers on the ground the statute of limitations, Code of Civil Procedure section 340. 5, bars the action. The trial court may grant leave to amend upon a proper showing.

 

723 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Anderson


Nos. F006844, F006865-F006875, F006877-F006880, F006884


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


191 Cal. App. 3d 207; 236 Cal. Rptr. 329; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1595



April 20, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Justice Court for the Delano-McFarland Judicial District of Kern County, Nos. DM9224-DM9229, DM9232-DM9234, DM9236, DM9237, DM9239-DM9241, DM9243, DM9293, DM9355, L. Bryce Chase, James Aaron and Curtis M. Spott, * Judges.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The actions are remanded to the justice courts with direction to allow each case to proceed to trial as infractions according to the views expressed in this opinion and without affording the defendants the right to a jury trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed from a judgment of the Appellate Department of the Kern County Superior Court (California), which reversed the trial court's order dismissing the charges against respondents, cotton farmers, for violation of Cal. Food & Agric. § 5784 and holding that the charges should be reinstated, but that respondents were entitled to a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Respondent cotton farmers were charged with violation of Cal. Food & Agric. § 5784 and Cal. Admin. Code, tit. 3, § 3595 (mandatory plowdown of cotton stalks). The trial court dismissed the charges because of an unconstitutional statutory denial of respondents' right to jury trials. Upon the state's appeal, the appellate division reinstated the charges, but held that respondents were entitled to jury trials pursuant to U.S. Const. art. III, § 2, cl. 3 and Cal. Const. art. I, § 16. On certified question, the court affirmed the order reinstating the charges against respondents, but remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to proceed to trial without a jury. In so holding, the court found that Cal. Penal Code § 654 prohibited additional fines and assessments for multiacre violations of § 5784, and thus the maximum that each respondent could be penalized was $ 850 ($ 500 fine plus $ 350 assessment). The court ruled that an $ 850 penalty constituted a petty offense and did not warrant a jury trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court agreed that the charges against respondent cotton farmers should be reinstated, but held that, because each respondent could only be penalized $ 850 (a $ 500 fine plus a $ 350 assessment), respondents were not entitled to a jury trial. The court opined that, for an individual citizen, a penalty would have to exceed $ 1000 in order to warrant a jury trial.

Title Insurance & Trust Co. v. County of Riverside


No. E002223.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two.


191 Cal. App. 3d 58; 236 Cal. Rptr. 207; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1580



April 15, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified April 20, 1987; Petition of County of Riverside et al. for review GRANTED July 16, 1987 (S001002) The matter is transferred to the Court of Appeal, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two, for reconsideration in light of Sav-on Drugs, Inc. v. County of Orange (1987) 190 Cal. App. 3d 1611 (See 196 Cal. App.3d 1353)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 144292, Gerald F. Schulte, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant counties sought review of a ruling of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which denied defendants the right to make ad valorem tax reassessments of real property owned by an acquired corporate subsidiary of plaintiff corporation as a result of a corporate merger under the provisions of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 64(c).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation acquired a company with subsidiaries, thereby triggering a reassessment for ad valorem tax purposes by defendant counties of plaintiff's subsidiaries' real property under the provisions of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 64(c). Plaintiff challenged the application of § 64(c) change of ownership provisions to the subsidiaries' real property and sought refunds of ad valorem taxes paid. Defendants appealed the decision in favor of plaintiff. The court affirmed, holding that the acquisition of one corporation by another corporation did not result in a change of ownership under the provisions of § 64(c) so as to require a reassessment of that real property pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XIII A. The court, analyzing the language of § 64(c), and declining to extend its provisions by implication, noted that § 64(c) omitted any reference to property of the subsidiaries owned by the acquired corporation. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of plaintiff's special request for refunds of taxes paid, holding that plaintiff was required to obtain refunds by following statutory administrative procedures.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of plaintiff corporation. The court found that, because no change of ownership occurred as a result of plaintiff acquiring another corporation with subsidiaries owning real property, no ad valorem taxes had been owed.

People v. Martinez


No. B020555.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division 4.


191 Cal. App. 3d 11; 236 Cal. Rptr. 174; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1575



April 14, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Respondents' petition for review DENIED June 25, 1987 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A776887, Miriam A. Vogel, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of possessing cocaine in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was a passenger in a car that was pulled over for a parking violation. The police officers noticed the driver attempt to conceal something in the car and ordered appellant and the driver out of the car. Appellant produced identification when the officer requested it. When a computer search revealed a possible warrant for appellant, the officer returned to get further identification from appellant, who was handcuffed. The officer reached into appellant's pocket, took out his wallet, went through it, and found a package of cocaine. The trial court denied appellant's motion to suppress pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, and appellant was convicted of possessing cocaine in violation of Cal. Health & Safety § 11350(a). On appeal, the court reversed and held that the cocaine was inadmissible because the identification search of appellant's wallet violated the U. S. Const. amend. VI. The court found that the initial detention was proper because of the parking violation and an outstanding warrant, and that it was proper to order appellant out of the car because of the driver's furtive gesture, but there was no basis to search appellant's wallet.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant's conviction for possessing cocaine because the identification search of appellant's wallet violated the Sixth Amendment and the cocaine evidence was, therefore, inadmissible.

Sav-On Drugs v. County of Orange


No. G003296


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


190 Cal. App. 3d 1611; 236 Cal. Rptr. 100; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1305



April 10, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 29, 1987, and on May 7, 1987, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 16, 1987. Kaufman, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County, No. 423348, Robert R. Fitzgerald, Judge. Affirmed.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. Respondents shall have costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporations sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which affirmed an administrative order of the Assessment Appeals Board of Orange County, denying appellants' challenge of a tax reassessment by respondent county under Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2 as a result of appellants' mergers with other companies.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporations, real property owners, merged with other companies. Respondent county subsequently reassessed appellants' real property for ad valorem tax purposes. Appellants filed action against respondent for refunds of ad valorem taxes paid on the grounds that the mergers did not constitute a change in ownership authorizing reassessment by respondent, and thus violated Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2. The trial court affirmed the Assessment Appeals Board of Orange County's decision rejecting appellants' claim. Appellant sought review and the court affirmed, holding that under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 64(c) appellants' mergers constituted changes of ownership justifying respondent's reassessment of the real property. The court rejected appellants' argument that their mergers fell within the "affiliated group" exemption to the change of ownership rules under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 64(b), finding that this interpretation would allow corporations to easily avoid the "ultimate control" principle of § 64(c). The court rejected appellants' claims that § 64(c) was unconstitutional and found that the electorate's passage of Cal. Const. art. XIII A was not derogated by § 63(c).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court upholding the decision of the Assessment Appeals Board of Orange County in favor of respondent county. The court held that appellant corporations' mergers with non-affiliated companies were considered changes of ownership allowing reassessment of real property for ad valorem tax purposes.

County of Marin v. Pezok


No. A029027


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


190 Cal. App. 3d 1441; 236 Cal. Rptr. 56; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1552



April 8, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 8, 1987, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 23, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 103505, Noel D. Martin, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged a judgment from the Superior Court, Marin County (California), in an action to recover child support and reimbursement of aid to families of dependent children funds used to support the child of respondent parents under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11350. The trial court had ruled that appellant was constitutionally precluded from obtaining support or reimbursement from respondents pursuant to § 11350.


OVERVIEW: A minor child had been declared a ward of the juvenile court pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, and committed to a group home. Appellant county sought child support and reimbursement of aid to families of dependent children funds used to support the child from respondent parents, which respondents agreed to pay. An order to show cause and declaration for contempt was subsequently issued asserting that respondents were in arrears. After a hearing on the order to show cause, the trial court determined that appellant was constitutionally precluded from obtaining support or reimbursement from respondents pursuant to § 11350. The order to show cause was discharged and the complaint dismissed. The court affirmed. The confinement of the minor was for the protection of society. The common law duty to support minor children did not authorize the state to recover the costs of confinement imposed for the protection of society and the minor and his rehabilitation. It would have been a denial of equal protection for the government to charge the cost of operation of a state function, conducted for the benefit of the public, to a particular class of persons.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that appellant county was constitutionally precluded from obtaining support or reimbursement from respondent parents, because government charges to a particular class of persons of the cost of operation of a state function, conducted for the benefit of the public, would have been a denial of equal protection.

Patterson v. County of Tehama

Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


190 Cal. App. 3d 1298; 235 Cal. Rptr. 867; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1545



April 3, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review denied June 25, 1987 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.

* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.

OVERVIEW: An ordinance impacting governmental control of land use was proposed and adopted by referendum. Plaintiffs sought declaratory and injunctive relief, as well as a writ of mandate compelling defendant county officials to perform duties imposed by state law without regard to the provisions of the ordinance. Only portions of the ordinance were in conflict with state laws and the remainder was valid. Defendant was sanctioned for failure to file a respondent's brief. Plaintiffs' request for attorneys fees was denied, and all other requests for sanctions were denied.

San Marcos Water Dist. v. San Marcos Unified Sch. Dist.


No. D001951


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


190 Cal. App. 3d 1083; 235 Cal. Rptr. 827; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1660



March 31, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 29, 1987, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 1, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N24145, Fiorenzo V. Lopardo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In a similar way, Government Code section 54995 is perhaps best viewed as a special claims statute directed at challenges to local agency user charges. The reasons why private litigants are required to comply with this type of statute apply equally to public agencies raising such challenges. As the Supreme Court explained in City of L.A., California decisions have not tended to exempt local governmental entities from these requirements. Consistent with this precedent, we hold that the School District's claim for a refund is barred by Government Code section 54995.

Each side shall bear its own costs for the appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district brought a successful challenge to a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), but the California Supreme Court remanded the matter to the court to determine whether appellant was entitled to a refund of "capacity fees" it paid for use of respondent water district's sewer system during certain years.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court remanded the case to the court after having held that a "capacity fee" assessed by respondent water district against users of its sewer system, including appellant school district, constituted a special assessment from which governmental entities were impliedly exempt. The court was directed to consider whether appellant was entitled to a refund of the capacity fees it paid to respondent in prior years. The court held that appellant's claim for a refund was barred by Cal. Gov't Code § 54995. First, the court noted that the capacity fees appeared facially barred by the 120-day limitations period in § 54995, because appellant failed to take any legal steps to dispute the fees and filed its action for a refund nearly four years after the last fee payment. Second, appellant asserted that its refund claim was not time-barred under the common law rule that statutes of limitation did not apply against the state unless the legislature expressly made them applicable. The court held, however, that the common law rule relied on by appellant was inapplicable to school districts.


OUTCOME: On remand from the supreme court, court held that appellant school district's claim for a refund of capacity fees charged by respondent water district was statutorily barred. The court further held that a common law rule that statutes of limitation did not apply against the state was not applicable to appellant.

 

730 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Vacu-Dry Co. v. County of Sonoma


No. A035858


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


190 Cal. App. 3d 947; 235 Cal. Rptr. 712; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1647



March 30, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 146715, William L. Bettinelli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), which sustained respondent county's demurrer and dismissed appellant's action. Appellant had sought a refund of property taxes paid, and argued that respondent's assessment procedures violated Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer brought an action against respondent county for a refund of real property taxes paid in the first year of its ownership because the purchase price was much less than respondent's current assessment. Respondent successfully demurred and the action was dismissed. Appellant argued that respondent's assessment procedures violated Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2. The court held that nothing in Cal. Const. art. XIII A expressly changed or prohibited the challenged aspect of the assessment procedure. Accordingly, the legislature did not violate this article when it incorporated the rate and assessed value restrictions of the constitutional amendment into the existing assessment procedure by enacting Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 50. Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 50 provided that appellant's property would be reappraised as of the date of its purchase and entered with the new value on the roll for the next succeeding lien date. Therefore, the supplemental assessment legislation merely created a new timing mechanism for the valuation and collection of taxes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and held that respondent county's assessment procedures were not unconstitutional. The court also held that the supplemental assessment legislation, under which respondent assessed appellant taxpayer's property, was merely a new timing mechanism for tax valuation and collection.

People v. Almodovar


No. B011757


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


190 Cal. App. 3d 732; 235 Cal. Rptr. 616; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1538



March 25, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified April 7, 1987. A petition for a rehearing was denied April 13, 1987. Spencer, P. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied June 25, 1987. Mosk, J., Broussard, J., and Kaufman, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A394853, Aurelio Munoz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed and the case remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that sentenced defendant to probation after she was convicted of pandering. The prosecution sought resentencing according to law.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of pandering which, under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.065, carried a mandatory prison sentence. The trial court sentenced defendant to a term of probation, and concluded that imposition of a prison sentence in defendant's case would constitute cruel or unusual punishment as to her. On appeal, the court reversed and held that imposition of a prison sentence for pandering was not cruel or unusual punishment on its face, because the penalty was not so disproportionate to the crime for which it was imposed that it shocked the conscience and offended fundamental notions of human dignity. The court further held that the penalty was not cruel or unusual as to defendant in light of her personal characteristics and the nature of the crime. The court held that it was not unfair to resentence defendant to prison because defendant had been the cause of the delay between the commission of the crime and her entry into prison.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court that sentenced defendant to probation and remanded the case for resentencing, holding that the imposition of a prison term was not cruel or unusual punishment for the offense of pandering, and that it would not be unjust to resentence the defendant.

Estate of Messner


No. D003900


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


190 Cal. App. 3d 818; 235 Cal. Rptr. 495; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1543



March 25, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied June 25, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 519647, Milton Milkes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The $ 45,019.24 judgment in favor of plaintiff Peter Rank, Director of the Department of Health Services against William R. Thurston as executor of the estate of Hazel B. Messner is modified to $ 12,639.52 and as modified, the judgment is affirmed. The plaintiff is to bear all costs for this appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant executor of decedent's estate challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that rendered judgment in favor of respondent Director of the Department of Health Services for reimbursement of payment of benefits made on behalf of the decedent.


OVERVIEW: Following the decedent's death, respondent Director of the Department of Health Services timely filed a creditor's claim with appellant executor of decedent's estate for amounts paid to various health care providers for treatment of the decedent under the Medi-Cal program from January 1, 1977, to the date of decedent's death. The claim alleged that reimbursement was authorized pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14009.5 for services rendered after the decedent attained age 65. Appellant rejected the claim. The trial court granted respondent's motion for summary judgment. On review, the court held that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14009.5 was to be applied prospectively only and that respondent was not entitled to reimbursement for services rendered prior to enactment of the statute. The court held that respondent's right to reimbursement arose with the adoption of the statute and the payment of benefits after the effective date of the statute, not when respondent applied for reimbursement. The court modified the judgment to reflect only those payments made subsequent to enactment of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent Director of the Department of Health Services for reimbursement of Medi-Cal payments on behalf of the decedent subsequent to the enactment of the statute permitting such reimbursement. The court modified the judgment and denied reimbursement to respondent for expenses incurred prior to enactment of the statute.

Nicolini v. County of Tuolumne


No. F005455


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


190 Cal. App. 3d 619; 235 Cal. Rptr. 559; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1528



March 23, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Tuolumne County, No. 23353, Jay A. Pfotenhauer, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee filed a petition for an original writ of administrative mandate pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5, seeking reversal of respondent county sheriff's and board of supervisors' decision to terminate appellant from his job as a deputy sheriff for dishonesty and conduct unbecoming a police officer under Tuolumne County, Cal., Ordinance 2.56.269(f), (o).


OVERVIEW: By way of a petition for a writ of administrative mandate, appellant employee sought to reverse respondents', a county sheriff and board of supervisors', decision to terminate appellant from his deputy sheriff's job. After a pharmacist called respondent sheriff claiming that appellant had just filled a questionable prescription and appeared to be "on" something, appellant admitted injecting himself with drugs during work hours and falsifying a prescription. Appellant claimed he injected himself with a painkilling prescription drug to combat a migraine headache. Respondents fired appellant for dishonesty and for discrediting the county by conduct unbecoming an officer. Upon review, the court of appeals held that appellant's behavior violated Tuolumne County, Cal., Ordinance 2.56.269(f) and (o). The ordinance was not void for vagueness because it gave a person of ordinary intelligence notice of the prescribed conduct. Appellant's theory that he was actually fired because of absenteeism due to a disability, chronic migraines, was not supported by the evidence. An almost three-year delay in commencing the disciplinary action did not violate due process. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court denied appellant employee's petition for a writ of administrative mandate. Respondent county sheriff and board of supervisors' decision to fire appellant based on conduct prohibited by county ordinance was supported by the evidence and did not violate appellant's due process rights, even though the disciplinary action was commenced almost three years after the infractions took place.

Fresno Police Officers Ass'n v. Cal.


Nos. A030412, A031651


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five

VI and XIV, and that granted judgment in favor of respondents state and state controller.

735 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Green v. Franklin


No. B002436.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two.


190 Cal. App. 3d 93; 235 Cal. Rptr. 312; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1478



March 12, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Respondent's petition for review DENIED July 16, 1987 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C247224, Robert C. Nye, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant physician appealed from a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied defendant's request to reduce the award of noneconomic damages and to order periodic payment of certain future damages to plaintiff patient in a medical malpractice case.


OVERVIEW: Defendant physician was plaintiff patient's anesthesiologist during a surgical procedure that resulted in plaintiff sustaining permanent brain damage and being rendered a quadriplegic. Plaintiff filed a medical malpractice action and obtained a damage award. The issues raised by the appeal focused on the damage award and the constitutionality and applicability of various portions of the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act of 1975 (MICRA). Defendant requested the court to apply MICRA to reduce the award of noneconomic damages to $ 250,000 and to order periodic payment of future damages in excess of $ 50,000. The court denied defendant's request, and defendant appealed. The court found plaintiff's constitutional claims failed under the doctrine of stare decisis. MICRA had been constitutionally upheld by the state supreme court. The court also found that defendant had not waived his right to invoke either Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2 or Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 667.7. Therefore, the court found that it was appropriate for the award of noneconomic damages to be reduced and for defendant to be able to make the periodic payments. The decision was reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The decision awarding plaintiff damages was reversed and remanded. The court found the legislative intent was for a medical malpractice defendant's noneconomic damages to be limited and for defendant to be able to make periodic payments. Under the doctrine of stare decisis, precedent holding the limitation of noneconomic damages to be constitutional was binding on the court.

 

736 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Nelson v. Bd. of Supervisors


No. D004711


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


190 Cal. App. 3d 25; 235 Cal. Rptr. 305; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1484



March 10, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 552669, Mack P. Lovett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is reversed. The superior court is directed to enter an order overruling the County's demurrer. Appellants to have costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeless residents appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which dismissed their lawsuit for mandate, injunction, and declaratory relief after sustaining without leave to amend the demurrer of defendant county supervisors. The lawsuit challenged the validity of county regulations terminating general relief payments to any recipient not obtaining a "valid address" within 60 days.


OVERVIEW: A county regulation terminated general relief payments to recipients not obtaining a "valid address" within 60 days. Challenging the regulation as statutorily and constitutionally invalid, plaintiff homeless residents filed a lawsuit for mandate, injunction, and declaratory relief. The trial court sustained the demurrer of defendant county supervisors, and dismissed. The court reversed. The court held that defendants' action in not providing general relief to homeless but eligible residents violated its statutory duties under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 17000 to aid its indigent resident population. The court found, for example, that one homeless plaintiff had been a resident of the county for most of the past 41 years. The court said that to be valid under the general relief statutes, a county's "valid address" regulations had to be consistent, not in conflict with the statutes and reasonably necessary to effectuate the statutory purpose. Although a "valid address" could have been an objective criterion useful in proving residence, it was not necessary to a determination of "true residence." The court did not need to address plaintiffs' constitutional claims.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of dismissal and directed it to enter an order overruling defendants' demurrer, because the court held that defendants' action in not providing general relief to plaintiffs, homeless but eligible residents, violated its statutory duties to aid its indigent resident population.
