People v. Buschbom


No. D002870.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One.


189 Cal. App. 3d 1615; 234 Cal. Rptr. 914; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1471



March 9, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review denied June 18, 1987 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. CR67760 and CR67757, J. Perry Langford, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him on one count each of conspiracy to commit murder, Cal. Penal Code, §§ 182(1) and 187, and attempted murder, Cal. Penal Code §§ 187 and 664, and found true the allegations that defendant used a firearm and inflicted great bodily injury.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed convictions of conspiracy to commit murder and attempted murder. The court affirmed the convictions but struck certain sentencing enhancements, finding that error in admitting evidence of an unrelated conspiracy did not require reversal because it was not reasonably probable that a more favorable result would have been reached. The court found no reversible jury instruction error. The incomplete instructions on conspiracy to commit first-degree murder did not require reversal because defendant could not demonstrate that he was guilty of a lesser offense, nor did the express malice instruction pose a danger of misleading the jury. Further, the trial court was not required to instruct the jury that defendant's wife was an accomplice because that was a fact question, and defendant waived other error by failing to object. The court reversed the sentence enhancements pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667 because prior convictions only established those facts which were necessarily adjudicated. The court also reversed the great bodily harm enhancement because it did not apply to the crime of murder and, thus, did not apply to conspiracy to commit the crime.


OUTCOME: The court struck the sentencing enhancement and the great bodily harm enhancement but affirmed the rest of the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of conspiracy to commit murder and attempted murder and enhancing his sentence for use of a firearm. The court found that no jury instruction error was reversible.

 

738 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Martinez v. County of Tulare


No. F006475


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


190 Cal. App. 3d 1430; 235 Cal. Rptr. 851; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1551



March 5, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 6, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 119395A, Nathaniel O. Bradley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of an order of the Tulare County Superior Court (California), that denied her petition for a writ of mandate regarding her dismissal from employment for violation of a personnel rule.


OVERVIEW: Respondent employer terminated appellant employee on the grounds that her welfare fraud conviction violated a personnel rule prohibiting conduct that discredited respondent. Appellant's petition for a writ of mandate was denied, and she sought review. The court held appellant waived her objection to the trial court's failure to file a written decision because she did not request a written statement orally or in writing. The court found that the personnel rule under which appellant was terminated was not unconstitutionally vague because the prohibitions included in the rule were ascertainable to a person of common intelligence; appellant knew or should have known that the commission of welfare fraud would discredit respondent. The court determined that substantial evidence supported the finding that appellant violated the personnel rule, and concluded that appellant's termination was not an excessive punishment. As there was a rational connection between appellant's conduct and her employment, appellant clearly discredited and defrauded respondent, and there was no abuse of discretion in terminating appellant. The order denying appellant's writ of mandate was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Order denying appellant employee's petition for a writ of mandate was affirmed because the personnel rule under which appellant was terminated was not unconstitutionally vague, substantial evidence supported the finding that appellant violated the rule, and there was no abuse of discretion in penalizing appellant by terminating her employment.

 

739 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Lim v. Board of Police Comm'rs


No. B021779.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four.


190 Cal. App. 3d 1036; 235 Cal. Rptr. 770; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1656



March 3, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review denied May 28, 1987 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 598683, Jack M. Newman, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant theater owner challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate to respondent, the Board of Police Commissioners of the City of Los Angeles, to set aside a revocation, under Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 103.109, of appellant's permit to conduct a motion picture show.


OVERVIEW: Appellant theater owner challenged a judgment denying writ of mandate by which appellant sought to set aside the revocation of his permit to conduct a motion picture show. Appellant contended that the revocation of respondent Board of Police Commissioners of the City of Los Angeles violated his state and federal free speech guarantees. The court disagreed, concluding that an U.S. Const. amend. I analysis was inapplicable to appellant's challenge to Los Angeles, Cal.., Municipal Code § 103.109(e)(2). Section 103.109(e)(2) was not directed at any First Amendment activity but went to unlawful public sexual conduct. Cal. Const. art. I, § 2's additional free speech guarantee did not afford further protection to appellant because the state's interest in regulating theaters was lawfully furthered by the prohibition of illegal sexual conduct. Substantial evidence, including that a guard was employed for cosmetic purposes, supported the trial court's finding that appellant failed to take vigorous action to supervise his premises. Section 103.109(e)(2) did not require evidence that appellant had personal knowledge of each incident of unlawful conduct. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying a writ of mandate to compel respondent Board of Police Commissioners of the City of Los Angeles to set aside revocation of appellant theater owner's motion picture show permit. The court found that the challenged ordinance lawfully furthered the state's interest in regulating theaters and that substantial evidence supported the finding that appellant failed to vigorously supervise his premises.

 

740 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Duncan


No. B022283


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


189 Cal. App. 3d 1348; 234 Cal. Rptr. 877; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1455



March 3, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 30, 1987, and Petitioner's Application for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied June 25, 1987.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner requested a writ of habeas corpus as to whether Cal. Penal Code § 311.3(a) was violative of his constitutional rights of free expression and privacy after petitioner was found guilty of the child pornography statute, Cal. Penal Code § 311.3(a) by the lower court (California).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged with two counts of violating Cal. Penal Code § 311.3 (a), the child pornography statute. Petitioner entered a plea of not guilty. He filed a motion to suppress pornographic pictures of children which had been seized from his home. That motion was denied. Petitioner was found guilty on both counts and sentenced to two consecutive one-year terms. On appeal, the judgment was affirmed and his petition for a writ of habeas corpus was denied. The court denied petitioner's petition for writ of habeas corpus because Cal. Penal Code § 311.3(a) was not overbroad or invalid under U.S. Const. amend. I and did not interfere with petitioner's constitutional right to privacy as there was a compelling interest in the protection of children from sexual exploitation. The court found no merit to petitioner's claim regarding ineffective assistance of counsel because counsel did have access to certain evidence in the development of an entrapment defense. The court found no merit to petitioner's claim of cruel and unusual punishment because petitioner's consecutive sentences were not disproportionate to other penalties prescribed in the area of child pornography.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's request for a writ of habeas corpus because the child pornography statute was not overbroad or invalid under the First Amendment. Further, the court held that the imposition of consecutive sentences was not disproportionate to other penalties for child pornography. The court held that petitioner's claim for ineffective assistance of counsel was without merit because his entrapment defense was developed.

 

741 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Estate of Cirone


No. H001577


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


189 Cal. App. 3d 1280; 234 Cal. Rptr. 749; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1440



February 27, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 18, 1987, and Respondents' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied June 3, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 105234, John A. Flaherty, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The court's December 3, 1985, order is reversed. Each party to bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state controller sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which ordered the state to pay attorneys' fees in the matter of a contested inheritance tax, from the funds of the Office of the State Controller and directed that the payment be made from the 1985-1986 budget of the state of California or from other appropriated funds.


OVERVIEW: An inheritance referee reported that decedent's estate owed inheritance taxes. Respondent executors objected to the taxes because the inheritance tax was repealed by a local proposition. The appellate court held that the imposition of inheritance taxes was precluded as to deceased. Upon remand, respondents were awarded attorneys' fees under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5. After appellant informed respondents that attorneys' fees were limited, they sought to collect the attorneys' fees from the State Board of Control, which requested monies to pay the award from the then current budge act. That collection effort failed when the appropriation was deleted. The lower court ordered that the attorneys' fees be paid from the 1985-1986 budget. The court reversed the lower court's order because it violated the separation of powers doctrine insofar as it directed the controller to pay an amount in excess of those restrictions. Furthermore, no party pointed to appropriations other than line-item 9810-001-001 of the 1985 Budget Act which were available for payment of respondents' fee award. Consequently, the lower court's order was overbroad.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order to pay a sum in the matter of contested inheritance tax, from the funds of the Office of the State Controller from the 1985-1986 budget of the state of California or from other appropriated funds because payment of those funds violated the constitutional separation of powers doctrine.

 

742 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Multiplex Ins. Agency v. Cal. Life Ins. Co.


No. A030526


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


189 Cal. App. 3d 925; 235 Cal. Rptr. 12; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1420



February 24, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 20, 1987, and Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 20, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 572698-5, Ken M. Kawaichi, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in favor of respondent in the amount of $ 9,377.71 is affirmed. The award of punitive damages is reversed with the right of a new trial on the basis of tort liability as set out in Seaman's and discussed herein. Each side shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurance corporation challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which entered a judgment in favor of respondent insurance agency, awarding respondent general and punitive damages. The trial court's denial of appellant's motion for a new trial notwithstanding the verdict and for judgment not withstanding the verdict prompted its appeal.


OVERVIEW: Respondent insurance agency filed a complaint for breach of fiduciary duty, tortious breach of implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing and breach of contract against appellant insurance company. The complaint was based upon appellant's refusal to pay respondent certain commissions purportedly due under the contract appointing respondent as a nonexclusive general agent for the sale of appellant's insurance policies. The trial court entered a judgment in respondent's favor, awarding general and punitive damages. Appellant then unsuccessfully filed a motion for a new trial and for judgment notwithstanding the verdict. After appellant's motions were denied, it filed a timely notice of appeal. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's award of general damages in favor of respondent and reversed the punitive damages award with the right of new trial on the basis of tort liability. The court held that under the given instructions, the jury could not have determined that appellant was liable in tort for breach of covenant of good faith and fair dealing because of special relationship, because as a matter of law no special relationship existed between the parties.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision awarding respondent insurance agency punitive damages with the right of new trial on the basis of tort liability, holding that as a matter of law no special relationship existed between respondent and appellant insurance company. The court affirmed the trial court's decision to award respondent general damages.

 

743 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Young


No. B012755


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


189 Cal. App. 3d 891; 234 Cal. Rptr. 819; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1418



February 23, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 13, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A089833, Jacqueline L. Weiss, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187 and attempted murder in the first degree in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 664, 187 entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California). Appellant was found sane at the time of the offenses and argued, among other things, that the trial court erred in refusing to admit appellant's pre-offense psychiatric records.


OVERVIEW: Appellant drove onto a sidewalk crowded with people, killing one and injuring others. He was charged with one count of murder and 48 counts of attempted murder. Appellant gave police samples of his blood, breath, and urine, which revealed no alcohol or drugs in his system. Appellant confessed that he knew what he did was wrong. Prior to the crimes, appellant was diagnosed with schizophrenia and had taken a prescribed combination anti-psychotic and antidepressant drug. The jury found appellant sane at the time of the crimes and convicted him of first degree murder and attempted first degree murder. The court affirmed and held that the trial court was correct in denying appellant's motion to appoint a particular psychiatrist because the defense had no right to the appointment of any particular expert. The court also held that the trial court properly conducted the trial in accordance with the limitations of Cal. Penal Code §§ 25, 28, and 29 in that the psychiatrists were not allowed to opine on appellant's ability to premeditate or harbor malice, but they were allowed to present testimony describing appellant's mental illness and its effect on his conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction of first degree murder and attempted murder because, among other things, the trial court did not err in refusing to admit appellant's pre-offense psychiatric records.

 

744 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Brooks


Nos. D003662, D004996


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


189 Cal. App. 3d 866; 234 Cal. Rptr. 573; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1415



February 20, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 72948, Barbara T. Gamer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County, (California), which convicted him of unlawfully taking a vehicle. Appellant had entered a negotiated plea to the offense and sought to withdraw the plea of guilty.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of his conviction for unlawfully taking a vehicle. Appellant had entered a negotiated plea to the offense. Appellant challenged the denial of his motions to withdraw his guilty plea and to dismiss the information pursuant to the Interstate Agreement on Detainers (IAD) Cal. Penal Code § 1389. Appellant sought habeas corpus relief and contended he was denied the effective assistance of counsel when he entered his guilty plea and when he unsuccessfully moved to withdraw his plea and to dismiss the charges. The court concluded that appellant had not waived his IAD challenge. The court held that a formal detainer must be filed before an inmate such as appellant could invoke the provisions of the IAD. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and denied the petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, because a formal detainer had to be filed before appellant could invoke the provisions of the agreement on detainers. The court denied the petition for a writ of habeas corpus.

 

745 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Carmel Valley Fire Protection Dist. v. State of California


Nos. B006078, B011941, B011942


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


190 Cal. App. 3d 521; 234 Cal. Rptr. 795; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1266



February 19, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified March 10, 1987. A petition for a rehearing was denied March 17, 1987, and appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied May 14, 1987. Eagleson, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C437471, Norman L. Epstein, Judge; No. C514623 and No. C515319, Jack T. Ryburn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As modified, the judgment is affirmed. Respondents to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged the judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which ordered appellant to reimburse respondent county for state-mandated costs in three consolidated appeals.


OVERVIEW: Respondent county purchased protective clothing and equipment for firefighters within its employ as required by Cal. Code Regs. tit. 8, §§ 3401- 3409 (1978). Respondent argued that it was entitled to reimbursement from appellant state for these expenditures because they constituted a state-mandated "new program" or "higher level of service" under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 2207 and 2231 and Cal. Const. art. XIII B, § 6. Respondent filed a test claim with the California Sate Board of Control (board) for these costs and the board determined that there was a state mandate and that respondent should have been reimbursed. Appellant did not seek judicial review of the decision and respondent filed a petition for writ of mandate and complaint for declaratory judgment. The trial court issued a writ of mandate and ordered appellant to pay the costs. On appeal, three cases were consolidated. The court affirmed with modifications and held that appellant had waived its right to challenge the board's findings and also was collaterally estopped from doing so. The court also held that the expenditures were pursuant to a new program within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XIII B, § 6.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments, ordering appellant state to reimburse respondent county for state-mandated costs because appellant was collaterally estopped from challenging findings of the California State Board of Control and because the reimbursement was for a new program within the meaning of the California Constitution. The court modified the judgments primarily to command the comptroller to draw warrants if necessary.

 

746 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



City of Salinas v. Ryan Outdoor Adver.


No. A012721


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


189 Cal. App. 3d 416; 234 Cal. Rptr. 619; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1378



February 13, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Ordered Withdrawn May 12, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 71306, Nat A. Agliano, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment as to sign A-15 for the reason that Ryan is entitled to compensation for removal of the sign. The judgment of the trial court enjoining Ryan from maintaining the signs listed in exhibit A to the complaint and ordering their removal is affirmed. The City's Ordinance No. 1103 as amended in Ordinance Nos. 1488, 1020, and 1838 are partially preempted by the Outdoor Advertising Act and are to that extent invalid. Ryan to bear costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant outdoor advertiser challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which enjoined appellant from maintaining signs that failed to conform with respondent city's ordinance and denied damages for their removal. The U.S. Supreme Court remanded for further consideration in light of its decision in Metromedia, Inc. v. City of San Diego, 543 U.S. 490, 69 L.Ed.2d 800, 101 S.Ct. 2882 (1981).


OVERVIEW: Appellant outdoor advertiser challenged the constitutionality of respondent city's ordinance, which regulated offsite signs in general commercial zoning districts and in unclassified zones. The trial court concluded that the ordinance was valid, that appellant's signs were public nuisances as a matter of law; and that appellant was not entitled to damages for the removal of its offsite advertising signs. On remand from the U.S. Supreme Court, the court held that appellant was entitled to damages for a sign that was in place prior to the federal Highway Beautification Act, 23 U.S.C.S. § 131, but affirmed the remainder of the trial court's order, finding that the ordinance was constitutional. The terms "commercial" and "noncommercial" did not need to be codified because they were sufficiently defined by the U.S. Supreme Court. Also, the ordinance constitutionally favored property owners because a private individual has greater protection under U.S. Const. amend. I to free speech on his own property, and discrimination between offsite and onsite billboards without reference to the contents of the message was valid where the shape of the sign was the concern addressed by the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, which denied appellant outdoor advertiser's request for damages associated with the required removal of an offsite sign that was in place prior to the federal Highway Beautification Act, but affirmed the remainder of the court's order that ordered appellant to remove all of its other signs for noncompliance with respondent city's ordinance, on the basis that the ordinance was constitutional.

 

747 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Taylor


No. A030984


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


189 Cal. App. 3d 435; 234 Cal. Rptr. 486; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1379



February 13, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II-B, II-C and III.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied May 14, 1987. Mosk, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 717274, James Timothy Ford, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of a postjudgment order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which determined her interest in respondent husband's judicial retirement benefits and denied her requests for modification of spousal support and attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: During the initial dissolution of marriage proceedings, the trial court concluded that appellant wife had a community property interest in a certain portion of respondent husband's judicial retirement benefits, but reserved jurisdiction over valuing appellant's share until such time as respondent was eligible to retire. When respondent became eligible to retire, appellant filed a motion seeking her proportionate share of respondent's retirement benefits. The trial court ordered that appellant receive monthly payments at a percentage of the gross amount during the joint lives of appellant and respondent, to terminate and revert to respondent on appellant's death. Appellant challenged the validity of the terminable interest rule, as applied to the Judges' Retirement Act. On appeal, the court held that the trial court's order was correct when entered, but nevertheless reversed the order and remanded the cause. The court directed the trial court, on rehearing, to apply the recently enacted Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.8 and to make whatever order was necessary to assure that the parties received his or her full community property share in the retirement plan.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the portion of the trial court order that determined appellant wife's interest in respondent husband's benefits, and remanded the cause for reconsideration. The court ordered the trial court to then make whatever orders were necessary or appropriate to assure that each party received his or her full community property share in the retirement plan, in accordance with a recent amendment to the Family Law Act.

 

748 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Long


No. H000965


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


189 Cal. App. 3d 77; 234 Cal. Rptr. 271; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1356



February 5, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied April 23, 1987. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 98910, Marilyn Pestarino Zecher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed, but the case is remanded to the superior court to amend its order suspending the imposition of sentence to allow for the appropriate good-time/work-time credits.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which convicted him of possession of methamphetamine.


OVERVIEW: During an investigatory stop, police officer searched through defendant's wallet looking for identification and found bags of methamphetamine. The court affirmed the conviction and found that the officer had reasonable suspicion to stop defendant because he was in a bar with an under age girl who had apparently arrived with defendant, and he showed signs of drug intoxication, an indication that he may have been in possession of drugs. Furthermore, the court found that the officer was justified in demanding identification during the stop because the intrusion on defendant's right to privacy was minimal compared to a police officers need to obtain the identification of one suspected of committing a crime. The court also found that the officer was justified in searching through defendant's wallet because defendant was apparently intoxicated, insisted he had no identification, had a wallet clearly visible in his pants pocket, and tried to shield the wallet from the officer indicating that had a concealed weapon or contraband. Finally, the court found that defendant should have received good-time/work-time credits against his imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of methamphetamine because the arresting officer was justified in stopping defendant, asking for identification, and looking through defendant's wallet. The court remanded the case for redetermination of his presentence good-time/work-time credits.

 

749 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Zieff v. Weinstein


No. A032974


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


191 Cal. App. 3d 243; 236 Cal. Rptr. 536; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1599



February 3, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 25, 1987, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 23, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 122506, Beverly B. Savitt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured party challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which sustained defendant host's demurrer without leave to amend because defendants were statutorily immune from liability under Cal. Civ. Code § 1714(c), and Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25602(b). Plaintiff brought an action against defendants for selling liquor to an obviously intoxicated minor who injured thereafter injured plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff injured party, a passenger in a minor's car, was injured when minor caused the automobile to crashed. Plaintiff brought an action against defendant hosts, who had sold alcohol to minor at a private party earlier in the evening when he was obviously intoxicated. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrer without leave to amend, holding that defendants were immune from liability under Cal. Civ. Code § 1714(c), and Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25602(b). The court deemed the minute order sustaining the demurrer to incorporate an order of dismissal and considered the appeal. The court held that the legislature enacted § 1714(c), and § 25602(b), to overrule a line of case law that had extended liability against alcoholic beverage providers for injuries arising from alcohol consumption. The court held that the exception to this immunity, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25602.1, could not be invoked because defendants were not licensed vendors. The court held that the legislature created broad immunity against liability occasioned by the furnishing of alcoholic beverages, which extended to an unlicensed host who sells alcoholic beverages to an obviously intoxicated minor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which had sustained defendant hosts' demurrer of plaintiff injured party's action against defendants for selling liquor to an obviously intoxicated minor who thereafter injured plaintiff in an automobile accident. Defendants were statutorily absolved of liability for injuries occasioned by their unlicensed vending of alcoholic beverages to the minor.

 

750 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Evangelatos v. Superior Court


No. B021968, No. B022000.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two.


198 Cal. App. 3d 320; 234 Cal. Rptr. 344; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1345; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P11,340



February 2, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petitions for review GRANTED April 16, 1987. (See 44 Cal. 3d 1188)
Withdrawn from the bound Cal. App. 3d volume.


PRIOR HISTORY: This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of February 2, 1987, previously reported at 188 Cal. App. 3d 1582.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff fabricator and defendants, wholesaler and retailer, filed petitions for alternative writ and writ of mandate in the California Supreme Court after the appellate court affirmed the trial court's ruling that the Fair Responsibility Act of 1986, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 1431.1-1431.5, was constitutional and was properly applied retroactively to plaintiff's suit for damages for personal injuries.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff fabricator was injured while fabricating fireworks at home and filed an action for damages against various defendants, but only defendants, wholesaler and retailer, remained at trial. The parties filed pretrial motions seeking a determination as to the applicability and interpretation of the Fair Responsibility Act (Act) of 1986, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 1431.1-1431.5, and the trial court held that the Act was lawful , that it applied to all cases not yet tried before its effective date, while no fault was attributable to the manufacturers, fault was attributable to all who contributed to plaintiff's injuries and not just plaintiff and defendants, and liability for future medical expenses and lost earnings were joint and several obligations. All parties sought writs of mandate which were denied by the appellate court. The supreme court returned further writ petitions to the appellate court, they were consolidated and the appellate court again upheld the trial court's rulings, discharged the alternative writ and denied both petitions for writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The appellate court discharged the alternative writ and denied both writs for mandate, and again affirmed the judgment of the trial court holding that the Fair Responsibility Act of 1986 was properly applied retrospectively to plaintiff fabricator's claims using the rational relation test, that allocation of liability considered all tortfeasors, and that economic damages encompassed future medical expenses and loss of future earning.

 

751 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Freeman


No. B016295.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four.


201 Cal. App. 3d 1081; 234 Cal. Rptr. 245; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1491



February 2, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted April 16, 1987 (S000319)
Trans. December 8, 1988 to Ct. App., 2d Dist., Div. 4, with directions to vacate decision and make an order directing trial ct. to vacate its judgment and sentence, in accordance with People v. Freeman (1988) 46 Cal. 3d 419.
Review granted, Depublished by People v. Freeman, 734 P.2d 562, 1987 Cal. LEXIS 297, 236 Cal. Rptr. 1 (Cal., 1987)
 Transferred by, Vacated by People v. Freeman, 764 P.2d 700, 1988 Cal. LEXIS 904, 253 Cal. Rptr. 572 (Cal., 1988)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A805936, James A. Albracht, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of February 2, 1987, previously reported at 190 Cal. App. 3d 180.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declined to sentence defendant to the minimum sentence of three years for pandering in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 266(i), pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §1203.065, because it would have constituted cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: The state sought review of the trial court's decision to refuse to sentence defendant to the minimum sentence of three years, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1203.06, for pandering in violation of Cal. Penal Code §266(i) because it would have constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The state contended that the pandering statute did not inherently impose cruel and unusual punishment. The court held that Cal. Penal Code §1203.065 was constitutional and did not inherently impose cruel and unusual punishment. However, the court held that there was nothing in California law which limited the trial court's discretion to determine that the sentence was disproportionate to a defendant's individual culpability. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court because the application of a minimum three-year prison sentence to the defendant in this case constituted cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling of the trial court to refuse to invoke a three year minimum sentence on defendant because there was nothing in California law which prohibited a trial court from using its discretion and determining that the sentence was disproportionate to defendant's culpability.

 

752 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Altaville Drug Store, Inc. v. Employment Development Dep't


Civ. No. 25385.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


188 Cal. App. 3d 1398; 234 Cal. Rptr. 78; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1330



January 28, 1987 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted April 16, 1987 (See 44 Cal.3d 231)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Calaveras County, No. 12936, Orrin K. Airola, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant agency sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Calaveras County (California), which issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing appellant to relieve respondent former employer's reserve account of charges for claimant former employee's unemployment benefits.


OVERVIEW: Claimant, a former employee, filed for unemployment benefits after she voluntarily resigned from her employment with respondent former employer in anticipation of imminent marriage and relocation to a new residence, making her commute to work impracticable. Appellant agency granted claimant's application for unemployment benefits pursuant to Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1256 and charged respondent's reserve account for the amount paid to claimant. The lower court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing appellant to relieve respondent's account of charges for claimant's benefits, holding that the word "spouse," which for purposes of § 1256 included "imminent spouse," had the same meaning for purposes of Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1032. The court reversed, construing the word "spouse" to mean a husband or wife, not someone who has not yet attained that status, based on the clear and unambiguous language of § 1032. The court found that the amendment to § 1256 chose to enlarge the meaning of the word "spouse" as used in that section without mention of § 1032. The court noted that such selective silence was significant in showing that a different intent existed as to each provision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, holding that the word "spouse" in one provision was not synonymous with the identical word as used in another provision of the unemployment insurance code. The court remanded the matter with directions to the lower court to deny the writ.

 

753 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Park 'n Fly of San Francisco v. City of S. San Francisco


No. A029879


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


188 Cal. App. 3d 1201; 234 Cal. Rptr. 23; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1315



January 26, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied April 15, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 269857, Allan J. Bollhoffer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs on appeal are awarded to respondent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commercial parking facility challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) denying its claims for declaratory and injunctive relief against respondent city, arguing that respondent's business license tax upon parking facilities was unconstitutional and that respondent had misclassified its business.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the declaratory and injunctive relief sought by appellant commercial parking facility against respondent city, which had passed an ordinance imposing a business license tax upon such parking facilities. Finding that appellant had failed to exhaust its administrative remedies in challenging the way respondent classified its business, the court then considered the constitutionality of the tax. It rejected appellant's claim that the tax should have been apportioned because a substantial part of its revenues was derived from driving its customers outside the city, finding that the taxable event was its provision of a parking facility inside the city. It found that the ordinance did not violate appellant's equal protection rights, noting that tax classifications carried a presumption of constitutionality and that respondent's justification for the ordinance, equalizing the tax burden among various municipal businesses, was rational. Finally, the court found that although appellant's customers were air travelers, the ordinance's effect on interstate commerce was so indirect and insubstantial that it could not be described as discriminatory.


OUTCOME: Finding that the business license tax imposed by respondent city against appellant commercial parking facility was constitutional and that it did not require apportionment, the court affirmed the trial court's denial of the declaratory and injunctive relief sought by appellant. It rejected appellant's claim that respondent had misclassified its business, finding that appellant had not exhausted its administrative remedies on that issue.

 

754 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Garinger


No. E002976


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


188 Cal. App. 3d 1149; 233 Cal. Rptr. 853; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1310



January 22, 1987 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition for habeas corpus is denied. The order to show cause heretofore issued is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought a writ of habeas corpus review to challenge his conviction of driving under the influence of alcohol and the trial court's denial of his motion to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate was charged with driving a vehicle under the influence of alcohol (DUI). When he was arrested, the police failed to advise him, as was required by statute, that if he took a breath test, the results could not be preserved for later reanalysis. The police also failed to advise petitioner that he could provide a blood or urine sample that could be retained. Petitioner moved to suppress the evidence of the breath tests for the failure of the police to give the statutory advice. After denial of his motion, petitioner pleaded not guilty, lost an appeal of his subsequent conviction, and sought habeas corpus review. Petitioner asserted that the police department had a deliberate policy of not giving the required statutory advisements. The court denied habeas corpus relief, finding that inasmuch as the failure to preserve breath samples did not violate constitutional rights, the failure to give the statutory advisements that breath tests were not preservable also did not violate petitioner's constitutional due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's request for habeas corpus relief. The court found that petitioner failed to establish that there was a deliberate policy by the police department to avoid giving the statutory advisements concerning alcohol breath and urine tests. The court found that the failure advise petitioner did not violate his rights to due process because failure to preserve a breath sample was not violative of the constitution.

Russ Bldg. Partnership v. City and County of San Francisco


No. A030997, No. A033493


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five.

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petitions for review GRANTED, April 30, 1987 and Pursuant to rule 29.2 (b) of the California Rules of Court, review is limited to and briefs on the merits are to be confined to the following issues, discussed in Part V of the opinion of the Court of Appeal: (1) whether extrinsic evidence is relevant for interpreting the building permit; and (2) whether the permit, properly interpreted, gave adequate notice to appellants of the development fee imposed after the permits were issued.

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 780795, 789365 and 790661, Morton R. Colvin, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developers challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which held certain development fees promulgated by respondent municipality to be constitutional and applicable to appellants.


OVERVIEW: Respondent municipality enacted San Francisco Ord. No. 244-81, which imposed a fee designed to compensate for the increased transit traffic resulting from construction of new office space in the downtown area. Appellant developers sought to have the ordinance declared invalid on its face, and challenged retroactive application of the ordinance. The trial court found that the ordinance was not a tax but a development fee, and its retroactive application was legal. The court held that the charge was a reasonably imposed fee to compensate for the additional burden on city services occasioned by the new construction and was not violative of appellants' due process or equal protection rights. The court upheld the trial court's judgment against one class of appellants who sought to declare the ordinance invalid. The court reversed the judgment as to the other class of appellants and found that retroactive application of the ordinance was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that respondent municipality's ordinance was constitutional, but the judgment holding that the ordinance applied retroactively was reversed.

Russ Bldg. P'ship v. City


Nos. A030997, A033493


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


199 Cal. App. 3d 1496; 246 Cal. Rptr. 21; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 2512



January 20, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to the direction of the Supreme Court (44 Cal.3d 839, 854-855 [244 Cal.Rptr. 682, 750 P.2d 324]), the Reporter of Decisions is ordered to partially publish this decision. The portions ordered published follow.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 780795, 789365 and 790661, Morton R. Colvin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In summary, we hold that the transit fee was a lawful development fee which is not governed by California Constitution articles XIII A or XIII B, and it does not interfere with plaintiffs' due process and equal protection rights. The fee is not a double tax and does not violate section 3.598 of the city's charter. Any error in calculating the fee was harmless, and the judgment in favor of the city and against Russ Building plaintiff is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, building owners, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which upheld the validity of a local transit fee imposed by defendants, city and county, as a lawful development fee not governed by Cal. Const. art. XIII A or XIII B.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, city and county, enacted an ordinance to provide public transit services for new downtown development. The ordinance required owners of certain buildings to pay a transit fee as a condition of issuance of a certificate of completion. In a consolidated appeal, plaintiffs, building owners, appealed from a judgment upholding the validity of the ordinance. They contended that the trial court erred in treating the ordinance as a development fee and in finding that the ordinance could not be successfully challenged under the constitution. The court affirmed, holding that the transit fee was not a special tax within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4 and therefore was not the type of revenue intended to be controlled by art. XIII B. It was imposed as a condition of development of real property, which was not a regulatory license, user fee, or charge. It was charged one time, and did not recur as does a property tax. Furthermore, the transit fee was designed specifically to fund maintenance and development, whereas a property tax provides general revenue to cover a wide range of municipal services.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendants, city and county, holding that defendant's impact fee was not an unconstitutional special tax but, rather, a lawful development fee on downtown building owners because it was a one-time fee used specifically for transportation improvements in the area charged and not as a general revenue measure.

 

757 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cox Cable San Diego v. City of San Diego


No. D003687


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


188 Cal. App. 3d 952; 233 Cal. Rptr. 735; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1293



January 16, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 536560, Mack P. Lovett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court entered upon the grant of summary judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, San Diego County (California), which granted plaintiff cable company's motion for summary judgment in its action seeking a declaration that the Cable Rate Deregulation Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 53066.1, preempted local cable rate regulation and seeking a declaration of its rights under a franchise agreement with defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff cable company signed a franchise agreement with defendant city that purported to regulate the rates plaintiff could charges. Subsequently, plaintiff filed an action claiming that the Cable Rate Deregulation Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 53066.1, preempted local rate regulation. Reviewing the language of § 53066.1, the court held that the statute preempted municipal regulations governing cable rates because the statute fully covered the issue and indicated its subject matter was of statewide concern. Additionally, the court held that federal law, specifically the Cable Communications Policy Act of 1984, sanctioned the state's preemption of cable rate regulation. The court then rejected defendant's claim that preemption by § 53066.01 amounted to an unconstitutional impairment of contracts; instead, the court held that plaintiff, as a political subdivision of the state, had no standing to raise the defense of impairment of contracts in opposition to a legislative act. Nonetheless, the court found that the statute did not unconstitutionally impair the right to contract. Thus, the court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of plaintiff cable company , in its action seeking a declaration of its rights under a franchise agreement with defendant city, because state law preempted local regulation of cable rates.

 

758 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Jeffers


No. D003182


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


188 Cal. App. 3d 840; 233 Cal. Rptr. 692; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1284



January 13, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied April 2, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 70195, Elizabeth N. Zumwalt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, San Diego County (California), which convicted defendant for pimping, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 266h, and pandering, in violation of § 266i (b).


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to being convicted of pimping and pandering, defendant argued that the trial court violated his right to counsel by denying his motion for a continuance to seek substitute counsel on the first day of trial. However, the court held that the trial court acted within its discretion in denying defendant's motion for a continuance because defendant failed to establish that he made a good faith, diligent effort to obtain retained counsel before the scheduled trial date. In so holding, the court noted that defendant's right to counsel did not include the right to be represented by a specific attorney from the firm appointed to defend him and that the counsel representing defendant stated that he was prepared for the trial. Reviewing the case, the court then held that any prosecutorial comment concerning defendant's failure to testify at trial was harmless error in light of the trial court's curative instruction and the overwhelming evidence in support of defendant's guilt. Applying a three-part test, the court then concluded that the trial court's denial of the opportunity for probation, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1203.065, did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentences for pimping and pandering because prosecutorial comments concerning defendant's failure to testify constituted harmless, if any, error, and the denial of probation to defendant pursuant to state law did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment.

Rutherford v. Cal.


No. D003131


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


188 Cal. App. 3d 1267; 233 Cal. Rptr. 781; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1320



January 9, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 6, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 470666, Franklin B. Orfield, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which held that Cal. Fish & Game Code § 1603 was constitutional, and that respondents, state and Department of Fish and Game properly enforced § 1603 against appellants when they added improvements to their land.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, landowners, added roads to their land, without first obtaining permission and permits from respondents, state and Department of Fish and Game. Appellants claimed that Cal. Fish & Game Code § 1603 was unconstitutionally vague. The court found that § 1603, which made it unlawful to substantially divert or obstruct the natural flow of any water without first notifying respondents, was clear and unambiguous. The court found that appellants' criminal misdemeanor conviction did not act as a collateral estoppel to the civil action. The court affirmed, holding that § 1603 was constitutional, and respondents' enforcement of the statute was not malicious, abusive, or done in bad faith.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the constitutionality and enforcement of the statute by respondents, state and Department of Fish and Game, against appellants, landowners, as to their improvements to the land made without proper notification to respondents.

 

760 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Ramirez


No. A020625


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


189 Cal. App. 3d 603; 236 Cal. Rptr. 404; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1394



January 9, 1987 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rules 976 and 976.1, California Rules of Court, this opinion is certified for partial publication. The parts to be published follow.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitions for a Rehearing were Denied February 6, 1987, and Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied April 2, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 83123, Conrad Lee Rushing, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment shall be modified to adjudge each appellant guilty of a single count of conspiracy, and of two counts of simple penetration by foreign object in violation of section 289, subdivision (a). As so modified, the judgment is affirmed. The sentence is vacated and the case remanded for resentencing in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Santa Clara County (California), convicting them of several criminal offenses including forcible rape in concert, Cal. Penal Code §§ 261(2) and 264.1; attempted murder, Cal. Penal Code §§ 664/187; penetration by foreign object in concert, Cal. Penal Code §§ 289(a) and 264.1; attempted robbery, Cal. Penal Code §§ 211 and 664; and sodomy in concert, Cal. Penal Code § 286(d).


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted among other things of forcible rape in concert, Cal. Penal Code §§ 261(2), 264.1; attempted murder, Cal. Penal Code §§ 664/187; penetration by foreign object in concert, Cal. Penal Code §§ 289(a), 264.1; and sodomy in concert, Cal. Penal Code § 286(d). Defendants appealed, arguing that the jury was not instructed that it was required to unanimously agree upon which overt act was committed in furtherance of a conspiracy and that they were erroneously punished for both the conspiracy and the substantive offenses which were its object. The court ruled that any error in failure to give a specific unanimity instruction was harmless because there was no realistic possibility of disagreement among the jurors as to the fact of the alleged overt acts. Further, the court held that punishment for both conspiracy and the underlying offense was impermissible when the conspiracy contemplated only the act performed in the offense. The court modified and affirmed the judgment, concluding that sentencing under Cal. Penal Code § 667.6 was impermissible without a jury finding that the offenses were perpetrated by the means which triggered application of § 667.6.


OUTCOME: Judgment convicting defendants was modified and, as so modified, affirmed where a specific jury instruction was not required if there was no possibility of disagreement among jurors, and defendants could not be convicted of a crime neither charged nor necessarily included in a charged offense.

Lucia Mar Unified School Dist. v. Honig


No. B019083.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six.


198 Cal. App. 3d 299; 233 Cal. Rptr. 531; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1270



January 7, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified January 29, 1987; Appellants' petition for review GRANTED, March 26, 1987 (See 44 Cal. 3d 830)
Withdrawn from the bound Cal. App. 3d volume.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 60152, Walter W. Charamza, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of January 7, 1987, previously reported at 188 Cal. App. 3d 678.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California) that denied its petition for a writ of mandate, declaratory relief, and restitution in a dispute with respondent state school superintendent about the funding of state special schools.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied appellant school district's petition for a writ of mandate, declaratory relief, and restitution in a dispute with respondent state school superintendent about the funding of state special schools. Appellant challenged Cal. Educ. Code § 59300 that required appellant and other local districts to pay 10 percent of the excess annual cost of educating pupils who attended a state operated school whose parent or guardian lived within the district. Appellant claimed that this statute mandated a new program or higher level of services and was constitutionally void. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and upheld the validity of the Cal. Educ. Code §59300 because it did not create a new program or service but only shifted 10 percent of the excess cost to the local school districts.


OUTCOME: Denial of appellant school district's petition for a writ of mandate, declaratory relief, and restitution in a dispute with respondent state school superintendent about the funding of state special schools was affirmed. The court upheld the validity of the statute that shifted 10 percent of the excess cost for operating the state schools to the local districts and rejected appellant's claim that the statute was unconstitutional.

 

762 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Adams


No. F005824


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


188 Cal. App. 3d 683; 233 Cal. Rptr. 534; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1271



January 7, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 172592, Donald B. Cantwell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, the appeal is dismissed. Husband to have his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former wife challenged the amended order of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California) that denied appellant the opportunity and right to reopen and modify her dissolution of marriage from respondent former husband under Cal. Civ. Code § 5124. Appellant sought to include respondent's military retirement as a divisible community property asset.


OVERVIEW: Appellant former wife and respondent former husband divided up the community property assets of the marriage by written stipulation, which were subsequently adopted by the trial court. After the hearing, the Federal Uniform Former Spouses' Protection Act, 10 U.S.C.S. § 1001 was enacted and retroactively allowed modification of community property judgments to include division of military retirement benefits. California also passed sunset legislation, Cal. Civ. Code § 5124, to allow retroactive modification. Thereafter, appellant filed a motion to modify the dissolution judgment to include respondent's military retirement. The trial court denied her motion and declared that Cal. Civ. Code § 5124 was unconstitutional. Appellant filed a notice of appeal. The court first held that the entry into the minutes of the trial court denial of appellant's § 5124 postjudgment motion was an appealable order. However, the court held that it was without jurisdiction to hear the appeal because her notice of appeal was filed over a month late. Pursuant to Cal. R. Ct. 2(a), appellant had 180 days after the date of entry of judgment to file her notice of appeal.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed appellant former wife's appeal of the trial court's denial of her motion to reopen and modify her dissolution of marriage from respondent former husband to include respondent's military retirement as a divisible community property asset. The court lacked jurisdiction because the time for filing notice of appeal was mandatory and because appellant's notice was filed subsequent to expiration of the statutory period.

People v. White


Crim. No. 14801


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


188 Cal. App. 3d 1128; 233 Cal. Rptr. 772; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1308



January 6, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 70594, James W. Luther, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The sentence imposed is vacated and the matter remanded to the trial court for resentencing in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which convicted and sentenced him for a multitude of crimes arising out of a sexual assault.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted on several charges arising out of a sexual assault. At sentencing, the trial court included enhancements for use of a deadly weapon, and for habitual offender status. Appellant challenged the sentence, asserting that the habitual offender enhancement was erroneously applied and that the trial court improperly sentenced him to consecutive life sentences for two instances of the same act. The appellate court determined that despite a history of violent offenses against women, appellant was not a habitual offender as defined by Cal. Penal Code § 667.7, and the court struck a consecutive life sentence imposed by the trial court. The court further found that the trial court had erred in instructing the jury on the elements which made an enhancement applicable, but determined the error harmless beyond a reasonable doubt because the missing element was proven by uncontroverted evidence. Appellant's convictions were affirmed, but the sentence was vacated and remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: Appellant's convictions on charges resulting from a sexual assault were affirmed, but his sentence was vacated and remanded for resentencing because the trial court improperly sentenced him as a habitual offender.
