Penn v. City of San Diego


No. D003990


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


188 Cal. App. 3d 636; 233 Cal. Rptr. 514; 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1268; 1987-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P67,493



January 6, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 496656, Benjamin W. Hamrick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a summary judgment granted in favor of respondents by the Superior Court of San Diego County, California in case involving appellant's claim that an ordinance was unconstitutional and a violation of antitrust law, under the Cartwright Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §16700 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant owned a legal gambling establishment where people could play card games. A municipal ordinance fixed an hourly rate to be charged to each player in the establishment for participating in the card games. Appellant claimed the charge was an unconstitutional exercise of respondent municipality's police power because it did not have a reasonable relationship to the objectives of the ordinance. Appellant also claimed the ordinance was an antitrust violation because it was an unlawful restraint on trade. Appellant sought declaratory and injunctive relief from the court, which granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, the municipality and its police chief. In affirming the lower court, the appellate court held the hourly rate was reasonably related to the municipality's purpose of discouraging gambling centers. The court also held the municipality was not a "person" for purposes of the state's antitrust law and could not be sued under the law.


OUTCOME: The lower court's judgment was affirmed because the ordinance was reasonably related to the legitimate government purpose of discouraging gambling centers, and the municipality could not be sued under state's antitrust act.

 

765 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Loska v. Superior Court


No. B019388


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


188 Cal. App. 3d 569; 233 Cal. Rptr. 213; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2405



December 31, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 29, 1987, and Petitioner's Application for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied March 17, 1987.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Appellate Department of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County is affirmed and the cause is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein. The stay issued herein April 16, 1986, is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner seller sought a writ of review to challenge a decision of respondent Appellate Department of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held the ticket-selling ordinance that petitioner was charged with violating to be constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner seller was charged with violating Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Ord. 42.03, which prohibited selling tickets in public places to events to take place in a place of public assemblage. Petitioner challenged the constitutionality of the ordinance, which was upheld by the lower court. He then sought a writ of review, claiming the ordinance was an anti-scalping ordinance that deprived him of property without due process. The People, real party in interest, asserted that it was not an anti-ticket scalping ordinance because it did not totally prevent sale or resale of tickets or regulate resale prices, just sales at certain locations. The court found the ordinance to be overly broad, but that it was rationally related to protecting the public against fraud and counterfeiters. The court affirmed the lower court's finding that the ordinance was constitutional, but gave it a narrow construction to preserve its validity. The court determined that the ordinance applied only to offers to sell or resell made by those in the business or practice of selling tickets where both the solicitation and sale took place on public property.


OUTCOME: The lower court's determination that an anti-ticket scalping ordinance petitioner seller was charged with violating was constitutional was affirmed because, narrowly construed, the ordinance was reasonably related to the People's interest in protecting the public against fraud. The matter was remanded for further proceedings consistent with the court's determination.

 

766 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Blew v. Horner


No. A028549


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


187 Cal. App. 3d 1380; 232 Cal. Rptr. 660; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2348; 51 Cal. Comp. Cases 615



December 17, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied March 5, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 123312, William B. Boone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed an order from the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), where defendant employer's motion for summary judgment was granted. The trial court found plaintiff was defendant's employee as a matter of law under Cal. Lab. Code § 2750.5.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff brought a personal injury action against defendant employer. The trial court granted defendant's motion for summary judgment on the grounds that when plaintiff was injured, he was defendant's employee as a matter of law pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 2750.5 and that the dual capacity doctrine was inapplicable. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. First, the court held that plaintiff was not an independent contractor because a prerequisite of being an independent contractor was being properly licensed. The court stated that although plaintiff was hired through third party, a finding that third party was defendant's employee due to his failure to be properly licensed, meant plaintiff was also defendant's employee. Thus, plaintiff was limited to the workers' compensation. Lastly, the court held that defendant was not liable under the dual capacity doctrine because plaintiff failed to demonstrate that defendant manufactured a product for sale to the public and the risk thereby created was the same as the one to which plaintiff was exposed. In addition, the court held that the doctrine was not applicable to defendant's concurrent role as an owner or occupier of land.


OUTCOME: The appeals court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in defendant employer's favor because plaintiff was defendant's employee as a matter of law. The court held that plaintiff was not an independent contractor because third party which hired plaintiff was not properly licensed.

 

767 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. O'Connor


No. D003855


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


188 Cal. App. 3d 645; 233 Cal. Rptr. 529; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2411



December 12, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 2, 1987. Anderson, J., * participated therein. Broussard, J. was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 73886, Richard D. Huffman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an appeal from the decision of the Superior Court, San Diego County (California), which convicted her of pandering pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 266i(c); she received the lower term of three years in prison after the trial court stated that it would have granted probation except for the mandate of Cal. Penal Code § 1203.065.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. Defendant appealed from a judgment convicting her of pandering pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 266i(c). She received the lower term of three years in prison after the trial court stated that it would have granted probation except for the mandate of Cal. Penal Code § 1203.065. Defendant argued that the legislature could not validly deny probation to all persons convicted of violating Cal. Penal Code § 266i, nor impose a mandatory three-year minimum sentence for a nonaggravated, nonviolent pandering offense. These punishments, she contended, were cruel and unusual. The court held that although defendant argued that pandering was not a serious crime, the opinion of the legislature was to the contrary. The panderer commonly preyed on very young women. The court concluded that it was legislatively reasonable to make prostitution a misdemeanor, but to impose a more serious penalty for one who would force, promise, threaten, or encourage another to be a prostitute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court which convicted defendant of pandering, and she received the lower term of three years in prison after the trial court stated it would have granted probation except for the mandate of the statute, because the state's punishment for the crime was not the most severe in the nation and its three-year minimum sentence was not cruel or unusual.

 

768 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Nathaniel Z.


No. G003426


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


187 Cal. App. 3d 1132; 232 Cal. Rptr. 378; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2327



December 11, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 26, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-121347, James R. Franks II, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Appellants to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) ordering appellants to reimburse appellee county for the costs of their son's home supervision plan and probation supervision.


OVERVIEW: Appellee county successfully petitioned the trial court to order appellant parents to reimburse appellee for costs associated with their son's home supervision plan and probation. The court of appeals reversed. The juvenile was committed under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602. The basis of commitment under that section was criminal conduct. One of the purposes of the confinement and treatment in commitments included the protection of society from the confined person. Thus, the state's purpose and benefits provided were for society generally and appellee could not recover its costs. Appellants were obligated to pay for only those state functions not conducted for the public benefit.


OUTCOME: The order requiring appellant parents to reimburse appellee county for the costs of their son's home supervision plan and probation supervision was reversed because appellee was not authorized to seek reimbursement for costs where the purpose of confinement and supervision included the protection of society from the individual.

 

769 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cowan v. Myers


Civ. No. 22987


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


187 Cal. App. 3d 968; 232 Cal. Rptr. 299; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2315



December 9, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 7, 1987, and the Petition of Plaintiffs and Appellants for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied April 2, 1987. Mosk, J., Broussard, J., and Kaufman, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 308404, William A. White, Judge. +


+ Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the writ of mandate vacated save and except as to that portion restraining enforcement of regulations implementing the Welfare and Institutions Code which sets the standard for approval of Medi-Cal services and provides billings as "medically necessary to protect life or prevent significant disability." Costs shall be recovered by the appellant State agencies.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state agency sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) issuing a writ of mandate and finding certain provisions of Medi-Cal Benefits Program, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14000 et seq., contrary to the federal Medicaid Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396 et seq. The plaintiffs cross-appealed contending the trial court erred in failing to restrain a post-service system of authorization.


OVERVIEW: Defendant state agency sought review of an order from the trial court finding certain provisions of Medi-Cal Benefits Program (Medi-Cal), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14000 et seq., contrary to the federal Medicaid Act (Act), 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396 et seq. The trial court issued a writ of mandate ordering defendant to refrain from enforcing the provisions. The plaintiffs two claimants and a taxpayer cross-appealed. The court reversed the judgment in part and vacated the writ in part. The amended provisions of Medi-Cal, which limited health services to those medically necessary to prevent significant illness, to alleviate severe pain, to protect life, or to prevent significant disability, were consistent with the Act. Medi-Cal's definition of medical necessity in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14059.5 was a proper limitation on services within the discretion provided by the federal law because it did not single out any particular condition and it was reasonable on its face. Moreover, the system of prior authorization was not violative of the Act because it was a permissible utilization control device contemplated by the Act, approved by the responsible agency and upheld by the court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in part and vacated the writ in part. The court concluded that the provisions of Medi-Cal, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14000 et seq., were not inconsistent with the federal Medicaid Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396 et seq., because the restrictions were reasonable and within the state's discretion. The court also held the system of authorization was not violative of the Medicaid Act, and therefore, the cross-appeal was moot.

City of Sacramento v. California State Legislature


Civ. No. 25620


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


187 Cal. App. 3d 393; 231 Cal. Rptr. 686; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2261



November 25, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 11, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 331607, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of dismissal) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which issued a judgment dismissing respondent legislature from an action in which appellant sought to compel respondent to appropriate money.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city filed an action against respondent legislature in an attempt to obtain a reimbursement, pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XIII (B), § 6, of costs it expended in compliance with a state-mandated unemployment insurance program. Appellant sought a writ of mandate commanding respondent to appropriate funds for the reimbursement of all costs associated with appellant's compliance from the inception of its participation in the program. Respondent demurred to the complaint on the ground that it is not a proper party to the action. The trial court sustained the demurrer without leave to amend. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's order of dismissal of respondent, holding that the judiciary had no power, under the doctrine of separation of powers, to grant appellant relief. The court found that separation of powers did allow for some incidental overlap of function, but a judicially compelled enactment of legislation was the very exercise of legislative power itself, and not an incidental overlap.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order of dismissal as to respondent legislature, holding that the judiciary had no power, under the doctrine of separation of powers, to grant appellant city relief in its action to compel reimbursement from respondent.

 

771 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Eckstrom


No. A030621


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


187 Cal. App. 3d 323; 231 Cal. Rptr. 664; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2255



November 25, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 16, 1986, and Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 4, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Lake County, No. Cr-1755, Roger G. Gilbert, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Lake County Superior Court (California), that convicted him of possession of marijuana for sale, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of marijuana for sale, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359. Defendant appealed, arguing his motions for mistrial, acquittal and suppression should have been granted, and asserting the search of his property violated U.S. Const. amend. IV. The court held that defendant's motion for mistrial was properly denied because the objectionable testimony did not prejudice defendant's defense. Defendant's motion for acquittal was also properly denied because all elements of the crime were established by substantial circumstantial evidence and reasonable inferences. The court found that defendant failed to preserve his objection to the search of his property because he did not object to the search at trial. Regardless, defendant did not have a reasonable expectation of privacy in crops in a field, and the aerial search of his fields was constitutional; police observations occurred within public airspace in a nonintrusive manner. Finally, defendant's motion to suppress evidence was properly denied as the police substantially complied with the evidence destruction requirements of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11479. Defendant's conviction was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Order convicting defendant of possession of marijuana for sale was affirmed; defendant's mistrial motion was properly denied as defendant was not prejudiced by objectionable testimony, and defendant's acquittal motion was properly denied as substantial evidence proved the elements of the crime. Defendant's objection to the search of his land was waived, and evidence was properly admitted as destruction requirements were substantially followed.

People v. Spann


Crim. No. 14627


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


187 Cal. App. 3d 400; 232 Cal. Rptr. 31; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2262



November 25, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Butte County, No. 86272, Loyd H. Mulkey, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which convicted him of possession of an unauthorized narcotic substance or drug in jail, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4573.6.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for possession of an unauthorized substance or drug in jail, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4573.6. That conviction was based solely on evidence of Valium in defendant's bloodstream that could not be attributed to a pre-incarceration period. The court reversed the conviction, holding that a person was not in control or possession of a substance circulating through his digestive and blood system, and that case law forbade convictions based upon the inferential and circumstantial link of use to possession.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for possession of an unauthorized narcotic substance or drug while in jail. Evidence that defendant had used Valium while in jail could not serve to establish circumstantial proof that defendant had also possessed that Valium, within the meaning of the statute creating the offense, while in jail.

 

773 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Luparello


No. D003512


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


187 Cal. App. 3d 410; 231 Cal. Rptr. 832; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2263



November 25, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Appellant Luparello for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 11, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C47683, Mark A. Soden, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged their respective judgments convicting them of conspiracy to commit an assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, and murder, in the Superior Court of Orange County (California), on the basis of prosecutorial misconduct, ineffective assistance, jury misinstruction, improperly admitted hearsay evidence, and insufficient evidence to support the convictions.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were convicted of conspiracy to commit an assault likely to produce great bodily injury, and murder. Appellants challenged their convictions on the grounds of prosecutorial misconduct, ineffective assistance, jury misinstruction, improperly admitted hearsay evidence, and insufficient evidence. The court affirmed. The court held that any prejudice flowing from the prosecutor's questions was minimized by the witness's testimony, which completely eliminated any suggestions of wrongdoing. Additionally, the admission of gang member evidence which was improperly placed before the jury did not support a reasonable probability that the jury would have reached a different verdict. The court held that in reviewing the allegations of misconduct, while it found error, it also found that no significant prejudice to appellants resulted. The court also held that appellants failed to establish their burden of showing that they received ineffective assistance of counsel. Additionally, substantial evidence supported the application of conspiracy and aiding and abetting theories, and the murder conviction, predicated on the principals' conduct was also substantially supported.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellants' convictions because any prejudice flowing from prosecutor's questions was minimized by witness's testimony, admission of gang member evidence which was improperly placed before jury did not support a reasonable probability that jury would have reached a different verdict, and in reviewing allegations of misconduct, while the court found error, it also found that no significant prejudice to appellants resulted.

In re Marriage of Griffis


No. F005805


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


187 Cal. App. 3d 156; 231 Cal. Rptr. 510; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2243



November 21, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 22, 1986, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 5, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 114428, William Silveira, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed only with respect to the property division and is affirmed in all other respects. The case is also remanded for further proceedings in accordance with the views expressed herein. 3 This includes the taking of any additional evidence necessary to resolve material factual questions not previously addressed and which will aid the court in making an equitable division of the marital property. Wife shall have her costs on appeal.



3 In view of our disposition of this matter, husband's request to take judicial notice of the declaration of Nancy Owens-Cierly is denied as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, wife, sought review of her divorce judgment from the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which awarded reimbursement to respondent, husband, for all contributions made from the parties' joint bank account to community property which he traced to his separate property. Appellant claimed that the trial court's retroactive application of Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2 deprived her of vested property rights without due process of law.


OVERVIEW: During their marriage, appellant wife and respondent husband established a joint bank account that was almost exclusively funded from respondent's separate property funds. Respondent used that account to make payments on his separate property, including a home and several motor vehicles. Appellant requested reimbursement of those contributions from the joint account but the trial court, applying Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2, traced respondent's separate property through the joint account to subsequently purchased or improved property and confirmed the subsequently purchased property was his separate property. Therefore, the trial court ordered respondent reimbursed for his separate contributions to improved community property, and denied appellant's request for reimbursement. On appeal, the court held that application of Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2 to property acquired prior to January 1, 1984, deprived appellant of vested property rights without due process of law because her property rights vested before the enactment of the statute and could not be altered, absent a significantly important state interest. Therefore, the court reversed and remanded the case for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court held that application of Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2 to property acquired before January 1, 1984 deprived appellant wife of vested property rights without due process of law. Therefore, the court reversed the judgment with respect to the property division and remanded it to the trial court for further proceedings in order to make an equitable division of the marital property.

In re Marriage of Carpenter


No. A028500


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


188 Cal. App. 3d 604; 231 Cal. Rptr. 783; 233 Cal. Rptr. 510; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2408



November 21, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 4, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 116759, Rex H. Sater, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to provide that wife's entitlement to her community interest in husband's military retirement benefits is limited to her interest in benefits payable on or after February 1, 1983. As modified, the judgment is affirmed. Wife to recover her costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), which modified a marital settlement agreement pursuant to respondent wife's request for a division of appellant's military retirement benefits under Cal. Civ. Code § 5124.


OVERVIEW: A marital settlement agreement between appellant husband and respondent wife, which included a provision that appellant's military retirement benefits were his separate property, was incorporated by reference into a final judgment of dissolution. The trial court subsequently granted respondent's request to divide those retirement benefits pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 5124, which authorized modification of a community property judgment to include a division of military retirement benefits payable on or after a certain date. On appeal, the reviewing court rejected appellant's contention that the application of § 5124 unconstitutionally impaired his contract rights under the United States and California constitutions. Although the court found that under certain circumstances, modification pursuant to § 5124 might result in an unconstitutional distortion of the parties' bargain, here the parties had stipulated to a substantially equal division of their community property. There was no evidence that modification would result in a substantial unequal division of the remainder of the marital property. The court affirmed the judgment as modified to comport with applicable dates in § 5124.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment to provide that respondent wife's entitlement to her community interest in appellant husband's military retirement benefits was limited to her interest in benefits payable on or after the date specified in the statute authorizing modification. As modified, the judgment was affirmed. The court found that the statute, as applied to this case, did not unconstitutionally impair appellant's contract rights.

In re Marriage of Siller


Civ. No. 24317


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


187 Cal. App. 3d 36; 231 Cal. Rptr. 757; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2232



November 20, 1986 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to Rule 976.1 of the California Rules of Court, the Reporter of Decisions is directed to publish all portions of this opinion except part II.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 8, 1986, and Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 5, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 1699, Sheldon H. Grossfeld and James I. Morris, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The orders appealed from are affirmed. Respondent Gail S. Siller is awarded her costs on appeal, the same to be paid one-third by respondent and appellant Charles W. Siller and two-thirds by claimants and appellants Siller Bros., Inc., and Siller Bros., a partnership.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a corporation and a partnership, sought review from an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which directed them to pay $ 100,000 attorney fees pendente lite to respondent wife's counsel pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 4370.


OVERVIEW: The trial court directed appellants, a corporation and a partnership, to pay $ 100,000 attorney fees pendente lite to respondent wife's counsel pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 4370. On appeal, the court stated that § 4370 provided that a trial court could order any party, except a governmental entity, to pay such amount as was reasonably necessary for the cost of maintaining or defending the proceeding and for attorney fees. Thus, appellants were not beyond § 4370's reach. Further, the court held that the trial court had jurisdiction to make an award of fees. The trial court acquired subject matter jurisdiction by respondent's complaint in joinder alleging appellants held and claimed properly in which she had a community property interest. The court concluded that application of § 4370 was reasonably related to a proper legislative goal, was fundamentally fair, and did not violate the constitutional rights of appellants. Substantive due process was satisfied by respondent's showing she had prevailed on the matters for which the fees were awarded. The application of § 4370 to appellants was neither unreasonable nor oppressive. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which directed appellants to pay $ 100,000 attorney fees pendente lite to respondent wife's counsel. The trial court had subject matter jurisdiction to make the award, appellants' constitutional rights were not violated, and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in making the award.

Bohemian Club v. Fair Employment & Hous. Com


No. A024988


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


187 Cal. App. 3d 1; 231 Cal. Rptr. 769; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2230; 52 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 755; 42 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P36,729



November 20, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Plaintiff and Appellant for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 26, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 119026, Kenneth M. Eymann, Judge.



DISPOSITION: That part of the judgment denying the Club attorney's fees is affirmed. In all other respects the judgment is reversed with directions to the trial court to reinstate the Commission's findings. The parties shall bear their own costs. 13



13 This judgment is no way precludes the Club from attempting to demonstrate that male gender is a BFOQ for particular employment positions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The parties sought review from a decision of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), which granted plaintiff club's petition for a writ of mandamus and dismissed the underlying action brought by intervenors, Department of Fair Employment and Housing and others, against plaintiff for alleged violations of the Fair Employment and Housing Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 12920 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Intervenor Department of Fair Employment and Housing filed an accusation against plaintiff club for alleged violations of the Fair Employment and Housing Act (FEHA), Cal. Gov't Code § 12920 et seq. The accusation alleged that plaintiff, as an employer covered by the FEHA, had, in violation of the FEHA, pursued a discriminatory hiring policy which systematically excluded women from all temporary and permanent jobs at certain locations. The trial court granted plaintiff's petition for a writ of mandamus and dismissed the action against plaintiff. On appeal, the court stated that the FEHA's inclusion of private clubs in no way hindered the overriding objective of eliminating employment discrimination in Title VII of the 1964 Civil Rights Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e. The evidence overwhelmingly established that club members preferred male employees; however, customer preference could not form the basis of a bona fide occupational qualification defense. The court held the fact that plaintiff was a private club did not exempt it from high legal authority which unequivocally declared that employment decisions could not be predicated upon stereotyped characterizations of the sexes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that part of the judgment denying attorney fees to plaintiff club and in all other aspects reversed the judgment with directions to the trial court to reinstate the findings of defendant Fair Employment and Housing Commission. Plaintiff's employment decisions could not be predicated upon stereotyped characterizations of the sexes, and club members' preference for male employees could not form the basis of qualification defense.

 

779 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Byrd


No. B004307.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


186 Cal. App. 3d 1262; 231 Cal. Rptr. 407; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2167



November 6, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Feb. 5, 1987 Respondent's petition for review Denied. Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art.VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A530941, Loren Miller, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which issued an order striking defendant's prior serious felony conviction under Cal. Penal Code § 667, after defendant pled guilty to robbery, and to using a dangerous weapon.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that a prior conviction for federal bank robbery qualified as a "serious felony" within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 667. The court found that defendant's agreement to plead guilty to robbery and concede to the validity of the enhancement allegation "in exchange" for assurances by the trial court that it would not impose sentence in excess of six years was plea bargaining even though the trial court did not expressly state in so many words that defendant was required to plead guilty in order to receive a maximum of six years in state prison. The court held that although the court possessed the authority to strike the enhancement, it abused its discretion because it made no finding of circumstances which could support striking the enhancement other than that it took the circumstances into consideration in selecting the upper term. The court reversed and remanded the order of dismissal with directions to vacate the sentence, permit defendant to withdraw his plea of guilty, enter a plea of not guilty, and proceed to trial or disposition of the matter.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the order of dismissal with directions to vacate the sentence for robbery, permit defendant to withdraw his plea of guilty, enter a plea of not guilty, and proceed to trial or disposition of the matter because trial court engaged in plea bargaining with defendant, and it abused its discretion in striking the enhancement because it made no findings of circumstances to support the striking.

 

780 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Hathaway v. Baldwin Park Cmty. Hosp.


No. B015313


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


186 Cal. App. 3d 1247; 231 Cal. Rptr. 334; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2165



November 6, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C249467, Raymond Cardenas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the Hathaways' petition for extraordinary attorneys' fees is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their petition for extraordinary attorneys' fees in a medical malpractice action in which plaintiffs prevailed over respondent hospital. Plaintiffs requested that the trial court permit them to compensate their attorney at higher rate than the amount prescribed in Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6146.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs prevailed in a medical malpractice action against defendant hospital. Plaintiffs brought a petition for extraordinary attorneys' fees, which requested that they be allowed to compensate their attorney at a rate of one-third of the amount that they recovered rather than the limited amount recoverable under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6146, of the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act. The trial court denied the petition. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the legislature considered giving the trial court the power to set reasonable attorneys' fees and rejected doing so. The court held that under the language of § 6146, the trial court did not have the power to award extraordinary attorneys' fees under § 6146. The court held that appellants failed to preserve their constitutional arguments by not specifically stating which constitutional provisions were violated by the application of § 6146 to their case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which denied plaintiffs' petition for extraordinary attorneys' fees after they had prevailed in their medical malpractice action against respondent hospital. The trial court lacked the authority to grant extraordinary attorneys' fees under the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act. Plaintiffs failed to properly assert the alleged constitutional infirmity.

 

781 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Stalnaker v. Boeing Co.


No. E002339


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


186 Cal. App. 3d 1291; 231 Cal. Rptr. 323; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2169



November 6, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. SCV-219867, John W. Kennedy, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff survivors challenged a decision of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County, (California) which granted summary judgment to defendant aircraft company and its subsidiary. Plaintiffs contended the trial court erred in ruling that Cal. Lab. Code §§ 3600 and 3601 precluded judicial relief, and in ruling that they could not maintain a constitutional tort action.


OVERVIEW: In plaintiff survivors' tort action against defendant aircraft company and its subsidiary, the trial court ruled in defendants' favor and granted summary judgment. Plaintiffs challenged this decision, contending that the trial court erred in ruling that Cal. Lab. Code §§ 3600 and 3601 precluded judicial relief, and in ruling that they could not maintain a constitutional tort action. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, finding that the trial court did not err in granting summary judgment to defendants. The court noted that Cal. Lab. Code §§ 3600 and 3601 were not applicable because there was no intentional misconduct on defendants' part to cause injury and that the modern view respecting actionable intentional misconduct by the employer was that it must be alleged and proved that the employer acted deliberately with the specific intent to injure the employee. The court held that plaintiffs' remedy for their deprivation was not to be found under some nonexistent "constitutional tort" action but under the workers' compensation law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, finding that the trial court did not err in granting summary judgment to defendant aircraft company and its subsidiary. The court found that plaintiff survivors' remedy could only be found under the workers' compensation law.

 

782 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



McKee v. Bell-Carter Olive Co.


No. F004677


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


186 Cal. App. 3d 1230; 231 Cal. Rptr. 304; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2164



November 5, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 2, 1986, and Respondent's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied February 25, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 97464, Jay R. Ballantyne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Plaintiff shall recover his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff olive grower challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which granted summary judgment for defendant olive processor because plaintiff did not exhaust administrative remedies under Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 55401 et seq. Plaintiff's claims against defendant included breach of contract, fraud, conversion, and breach of the implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff olive grower brought an action against defendant olive processor for breach of contract, fraud, conversion, and breach of the implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing. The trial court granted summary judgment for defendant, determining that it lacked jurisdiction because plaintiff failed to exhaust the administrative remedies of Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 55401 et seq. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the administrative remedy was cumulative to the judicial remedy, not exclusive. The court reasoned that Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 55437, was virtually identical to Cal. Civ. Code §§ 1752 and 1942.5(h), both of which provided that their remedies were in addition to judicial remedies. The court held that because a common law remedy preexisted Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 55437, the statutory remedy was cumulative, and plaintiff could elect either the statutory or common law remedy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment rendered by the trial court in favor of defendant olive processor in plaintiff's action for breach of contract, fraud, conversion, and breach of the implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing. Because plaintiff olive grower's judicial remedy was cumulative to the administrative remedy, he was not required to exhaust administrative remedies before bringing his judicial action.

 

783 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Stevens


Nos. A030244, A033139


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


189 Cal. App. 3d 1020; 236 Cal. Rptr. 296; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2424



November 5, 1986 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rule 976.1 of the California Rules of Court, the portion of the opinion certified for publication follows.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 26189, William R. Channell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, and the petition for habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which convicted him of attempted receipt of stolen property. Defendant also sought a writ of habeas corpus based on the alleged ineffectiveness of his trial counsel.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant was convicted of one count of attempted receipt of stolen property. On appeal, defendant contended the trial court erred in giving an instruction on the statutory presumption of guilty knowledge embodied in Cal. Penal Code § 496. The court found there was sufficient evidence from which a jury could infer defendant was a secondhand dealer within the meaning of § 496. The court concluded that the trial court properly let the jury determine whether the presumption of guilty knowledge applicable to secondhand dealers in § 496 was applicable because there was no indication of legislative intent to adopt the statutory definition of "secondhand dealers" set out in Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 21626 into the statutory presumption set out by § 496. The court rejected defendant's contention that § 496 was unconstitutionally vague because the language applying the statutory presumption to those "whose principal business is dealing in" secondhand merchandise satisfied the constitutional standard of clarity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed criminal defendant's conviction and denied his petition for habeas corpus because the trial court did not err in giving an instruction on the statutory presumption of guilty knowledge applicable to persons whose principal business was dealing in or collecting used or secondhand merchandise.

 

784 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Barnes v. Superior Court


No. A034672


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


186 Cal. App. 3d 969; 231 Cal. Rptr. 158; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2207



October 30, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied January 22, 1987.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of mandate is denied and the alternative writ is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, inmate at a state hospital, sought a writ of mandate to require respondent court, the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) to afford him a jury trial on the issue of whether he should be placed in a local mental health program for one year.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, inmate at a state hospital, was charged with various criminal offenses and found not guilty by reason of insanity. Petitioner was committed to a state hospital. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate after respondent court refused to grant petitioner's demand to have a jury determine whether he was suitable for a one-year placement in a local mental health program. The court denied petitioner's request for a writ of mandate and found that the language and the requirements of Cal. Penal Code § 1026.2(e), did not require that a jury make the initial placement decision. The court held that because of the procedural safeguards found in Cal. Penal Code § 1026.2(a), (d), (j), due process did not require that an applicant pursuant to § 1026.2(e) be given a jury trial at the initial placement proceeding. Finally, the court held that § 1026.2(e) did not violate equal protection by requiring a court make the initial placement decision.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's, inmate at a state hospital, writ of mandate to require respondent court to afford him a jury trial because a state statute did not require the initial determination of defendant's suitability for local placement be made by a jury. Furthermore, the court found that a court trial of the placement question did not violate defendant's procedural due process or equal protection rights.

 

785 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Gebert v. Patterson


No. A033408


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


186 Cal. App. 3d 868; 231 Cal. Rptr. 150; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2198



October 28, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco, No. 845218, Raymond D. Williamson, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying the petition for writ of mandate is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court with directions to dismiss the petition as moot. Appellant is awarded costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, indigent proponent of a ballot argument, challenged the decision of the Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied appellant's petition for a peremptory writ of mandate. Appellant contended that a local regulation which required the proponent of a ballot argument to pay a fee before it was published in the voter's handbook violated U.S. Const. amend XIV; Cal. Const. art. I, § 7.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, indigent proponent of a ballot argument, submitted to respondent, registrar of voters, a ballot argument in favor of an act and a declaration of indigency. Respondent returned appellant's argument for failure to prepay the estimated printing costs. Appellant petitioned the trial court for a peremptory writ of mandate to require respondent to accept his ballot argument for inclusion in the voter information pamphlet and to waive the publication fee required by the San Francisco Administrative Code. Appellant's petition was denied and he sought review. The court held that by permitting individuals to submit ballot arguments for publication in the voter's information handbook the city and county created a forum for the exercise of political expression. The court held that San Francisco, Cal. Admin. Code § 5.74, violated state and federal guarantees of equal protection by qualifying an individual's participation in the electoral process solely upon his ability to pay the administrative (i.e., publication) costs. The court reversed the denial of appellant's petition and remanded the cause to the trial court with directions to dismiss the petition as moot.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded to the trial court with instruction to dismiss the petition as moot. The court held the denial of petitioner's, indigent proponent of a ballot argument, request for a writ of mandate was improper where the city ordinance requiring payment of a fee before a ballot argument was published violated the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and impeded free expression guaranteed by the First Amendment.

 

786 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Beaty v. Imperial Irrigation Dist.


No. D003325


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


186 Cal. App. 3d 897; 231 Cal. Rptr. 128; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2201



October 28, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 24, 1986, and Respondents' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied January 14, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Imperial County, No. 3386, Henry Wien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in part and reversed in part. The appellants to receive their costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant land owners challenged an order of the Superior Court of Imperial County (California), which denied relocation assistance benefits under the Relocation Assistance Act, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 7260(c)(g), 7262, after appellant's land was flooded by respondents, Imperial Irrigation District and the Coachella Valley Water District. Appellants brought an inverse condemnation action against respondents.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owners brought an inverse condemnation action against respondents, Imperial Irrigation District and the Coachella Valley Water District, after respondents flooded their land. Appellants vacated their land, and then unsuccessfully requested relocation assistance benefits from respondents under the Relocation Assistance Act, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 7260(c)(g), 7262. Appellants sought judicial intervention to compel respondents to provide the assistance, but the trial court denied their request holding that the statute was inapplicable to inverse condemnation actions. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment in part, and reversed in part. The court held that appellant's request was premature. The court noted that parties who successfully bring inverse condemnation actions are entitled to relocation assistance benefits under the statute if they are actually displaced by the inverse condemnation. In the present case, however, appellant's condemnation claim had not been fully and finally adjudicated. Therefore respondent's negative determination of benefits was untimely. The court awarded appellants costs on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part, because landowners who are actually displaced as a result of inverse condemnation, and who successfully litigate or settle their condemnation claims, are entitled to relocation assistance under the statute. Appellant landowner's claims, however, had not been fully and finally adjudicated and therefore a determination of benefits was premature. Appellants were granted costs on appeal.

Redondo Beach Police Supervisory etc. Ass'n v. Board of Administration


No. B015584.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


186 Cal. App. 3d 724; 231 Cal. Rptr. 65; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2146



October 22, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review den. Dec. 31, 1986 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 511445, Irving A. Shimer, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant board of administration sought review of a judgement from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted plaintiff police association's preemptory writ of mandate in an action to add a benefit provided by Cal. Gov't Code § 20862.8 to a contract. Plaintiff police association sought review of a denial of attorney fees under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendant board of administration contracted with the city to provide retirement benefits. The city made an agreement with plaintiff police association to amend its Public Employee's Retirement System (PERS) contract and add a benefit provided by Cal. Gov't Code § 20862.8. Defendant refused the amendment because it had been made on behalf of plaintiff police association and not on behalf of all local policemen. Plaintiff was granted a preemptory writ of mandate requiring the amendment's acceptance. Defendant sought review of the writ, and plaintiff sought review of a denial of attorney fees under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5. The court affirmed the writ because § 20862.8 of the Public Employees' Retirement Law, Cal. Gov't Code § 20000 et seq., provided for benefits that were available to less than all members of a PERS classification when such classification could be fragmented into smaller bargaining units pursuant to the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3500 et seq. The court reversed the denial of attorney fees and remanded for an assessment of the litigation under the private attorney general theory.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment in part granting plaintiff police association's preemptory writ of mandate in an action against defendant board of administration because the amended Public Employee's Retirement System contract had been authorized, even though the benefit was not afforded to all local policemen, and reversed and remanded in part for a determination of attorney fees under the private attorney general theory.

 

788 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



King v. McMahon


No. A029065


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


186 Cal. App. 3d 648; 230 Cal. Rptr. 911; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2140



October 21, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 13, 1986, and respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 15, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 786370, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, the judgment is reversed. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, California Department of Social Services, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) which enjoined the denial of state foster care benefits to foster children living with relatives, while granting benefits to foster children living with non-relatives.


OVERVIEW: Respondent foster children and their related foster parents brought suit after be denied financial benefits by the state and obtained summary judgment that enjoined denial of benefits to foster children living with relatives, while granting benefits to foster children living with non-relatives. The court reversed and held that the different treatment was reasonable because it was rationally calculated to achieve the goal of providing the maximum amount of foster care with available funds. It determined that the rationale that justified the different treatment was that more children were in need of foster care than were receiving it; that persons having a family tie to a child would provide assistance without financial incentive to do so; that persons having no family tie would not provide foster care unless financially induced to do so; and that if the savings resulting from the denial of benefits to related foster parents were instead used to increase the benefits to unrelated foster parents, more foster care would be provided to all children in need thereof than if the limited public funds available were used to provide benefits to related as well as unrelated foster parents.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that enjoined denying benefits to foster children living with relatives while granting benefits to foster children living with non-relatives because it was rationally related to the goal of providing the maximum foster care with available public funds since relatives will provide support without financial incentive.

 

789 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (himmelsbach)


No. H001779


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


186 Cal. App. 3d 524; 230 Cal. Rptr. 890; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2129



October 16, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Real Party in Interest for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied January 29, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 101984, Paul R. Teilh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the court to (1) vacate its disposition in its entirety, (2) to permit the real party to withdraw his pleas of guilty upon proper notice and motion, and (3) otherwise proceed in a manner not inconsistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought review of an order of respondent Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which stayed the execution of the sentences for criminal convictions of real party in interest, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 654, and dismissed a charge, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel the trial court to vacate its disposition of the charges.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest, defendant, pleaded guilty to criminal charges based upon respondent trial court's indication that he would receive probation with a two-year county jail condition. Petitioner state sought a writ of mandate and contended that respondent based its disposition on an unfounded determination that any prison sentence would constitute cruel and unusual punishment, that it engaged in impermissible plea bargaining, and that it circumvented the probation bar of Cal. Penal Code § 12311 by its improper application of Cal. Penal Code § 654. The court granted the petition and held that the disposition was not based on facts and that respondent had exceeded its jurisdiction when it engaged in plea bargaining in contravention of existing law. The court held that respondent had no authority under § 654 to stay execution of the sentence imposed upon the most seriously punishable offense, but that Cal. Penal Code § 1385 specifically provided for dismissals in the interests of justice and required a statement of reasons. Section 654 could not be used to grant probation for a disqualified offense because § 12311 placed limitations on the exercise of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court granted a peremptory writ of mandate in favor of petitioner state and directed respondent trial court to vacate its disposition and to permit the real party in interest to withdraw his pleas of guilty. The court held that respondent had exceeded its jurisdiction when it dismissed a criminal charge and stayed the execution of sentences of criminal convictions.

Am. Isuzu Motors v. New Motor Vehicle Bd.


No. B013980


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


186 Cal. App. 3d 464; 230 Cal. Rptr. 769; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2124



October 16, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C517297, Norman L. Epstein, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court denying the petition for writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant manufacturer sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied its petition for writ of mandate to set aside a decision by respondent, California New Motor Vehicle Board. The superior court upheld the decision of the board, sustaining respondent dealer's protest to appellant's notice of termination of its franchise.


OVERVIEW: After receiving a termination notice from appellant manufacturer, respondent dealer filed a protest with respondent, California New Motor Vehicle Board, which sustained the protest. Appellant's petition for writ of mandate to set aside respondent board's decision was denied. The court affirmed, holding that respondent board, comprised of dealer and public members, was not unconstitutionally biased because only public members participated in dealer-manufacturer protests. The court found no evidence that the hearing officer had any financial stake to present an appearance of bias. The court found that substantial evidence supported respondent board's findings that the sole grounds for the proposed termination was respondent dealer's purported failure to provide an exclusive showroom, that the franchise agreement did not require an exclusive showroom, and that good cause was not established to terminate the franchise. The court held that respondent board was not required to consider grounds that were not included in the notice, because doing so would have infringed on respondent dealer's procedural due process right.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying the petition filed by appellant manufacturer to set aside the decision of respondent California New Motor Vehicle Board. The court found that substantial evidence supported respondent board's findings, sustaining respondent dealer's protest to a termination notice, and that respondent board was not unconstitutionally biased.

People v. Long


No. H000965.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH Dist.


186 Cal. App. 3d 216; 230 Cal. Rptr. 483; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2100



October 2, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted November 3, 1986 (See 189 Cal. App. 3d 77)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 98910, Marilyn Pestarino Zecher, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for possession of methamphetamine in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11377 by the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for possession of methamphetamine in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11377 on the ground that the drug evidence should have been suppressed. The court affirmed the conviction and held that defendant was lawfully detained at the time the drugs were discovered because the officer suspected that defendant had violated Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25665 by accompanying an underage girl into a bar, and suspected that he was under the influence of methamphetamine and in possession of methamphetamine. The court determined that under the specific circumstances of defendant's detention, it was constitutional for the officer to direct defendant to produce written identification and that, when defendant insisted he had no identification, it was proper for the officer to direct defendant to produce identification because the officer observed a wallet-sized bulge in his pocket. The court held that the officer did not violate defendant's rights under U. S. Const. amend. IV when he took the wallet and examined it, because defendant's movements created a reasonable suspicion that he was attempting to destroy or conceal evidence, or that he had a weapon.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of methamphetamine and remanded because defendant was lawfully detained when the drug evidence was obtained. Under the specific circumstances of defendant's detention, it was not violative of defendant's Fourth Amendment rights for a police officer to request identification or to examine his wallet.

People v. Chandler


No. B014687


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


186 Cal. App. 3d 200; 230 Cal. Rptr. 492; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2098



October 2, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied January 14, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A630536, Rosemary M. Dunbar, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 459, and found prior convictions of burglary and attempted robbery, Cal. Penal Code §§ 664/211.


OVERVIEW: Neighbors discovered appellant as he broke into a house. After throwing clothes onto the front porch, appellant came out of the house at a neighbor's threatening behest. At appellant's trial for burglary, Cal. Penal. Code § 459, he admitted to prior convictions for burglary and attempted robbery, Cal. Penal Code §§ 664/211. Appellant was convicted of burglary, after accepting appellant's jury waiver on the issue of the prior convictions, and his sentence was enhanced. Appellant challenged the finding of the prior offenses and the sentence enhancement. On appeal the court affirmed, holding that appellant was free to attack respondent state's case following the waiver of his jury trial so that it was constitutionally sufficient. The court also held that admissibility of evidence of prior convictions was not contingent on a foundational showing that they were constitutionally valid. The court further held that enhancement could be the same for dissimilar offenses. Finally, the court held that any ineffective assistance in allowing the impeachment by prior offenses was harmless error due to the overwhelming evidence of guilt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's robbery conviction and sentence enhancement because the jury trial waiver was constitutional as appellant could still attack respondent state's case, prior convictions could be admitted even if they were unconstitutional, enhancement could be the same for dissimilar offenses, and overwhelming evidence of guilt rendered any ineffectiveness of counsel harmless.

 

793 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Emberton v. County of San Diego


No. D003701


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


186 Cal. App. 3d 268; 230 Cal. Rptr. 572; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2106



September 23, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied December 17, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 519900, Mack P. Lovett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which dismissed his action for failure to return summons within one year pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 5147.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed appellant taxpayer's action for failure to comply with Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 5147. Appellant sought review, arguing that Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 5147 was unconstitutional because its requirement of summons and proof of service returned within one year bore no rational relationship to a legitimate state purpose where respondent county had answered the complaint. The court affirmed and held that appellant had failed to demonstrate that Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 5147 was unreasonable as applied to him, or that he had been denied an opportunity for a hearing in order to establish a denial of due process. Furthermore, the court held that because the one-year limitation was applicable to all litigants, § 5147 did not violate the equal protection clause. Finally, the court held that respondent was not estopped from applying § 5147 to this case because the request to transfer was employed properly and was filed early in the statutory period. Thus, the motion could not have been said to have "lulled" appellant into failing to comply with his own filing requirements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which dismissed appellant taxpayer's action, because the statutory requirement that summons and proof of service were to be returned within one year was not unconstitutional, and appellant failed to show that the statute was unreasonable applied to him, or violated due process or equal protection because it applied to all litigants equally.
