In re Richard T.


No. G0002987.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


185 Cal. App. 3d 855; 229 Cal. Rptr. 884; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2046



September 19, 1986 


NOTICE:

 [**1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted Dec. 18, 1986 (Crim. 25994)
Dec. 19, 1986 Pending further order of the ct., the Ct. App. opn. shall remain published and citable.
This court's order of December 19, 1986, directing that the opinion of the Court of Appeal, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three, shall remain published and citable, is hereby vacated. ( Cal. Rules of Court, rule 976 (d).)
Review was granted in this matter on December 18, 1986. The matter is ordered transferred to the Court of Appeal, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three with directions to vacate its opinion(s) and to consider the cause in light of Ingersoll v. Palmer (1987) 43 Cal. 3d 1321, ( Cal. Rule of Court, rule 29 (e).)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-109712, Gale P. Hickman, Temporary Judge. * 


*  Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant drunk driver sought review of a decision of the Orange County Superior Court (California), which denied appellant's motion to suppress blood alcohol results obtained at a sobriety checkpoint.


OVERVIEW: Appellant drunk driver was detained at a sobriety checkpoint on New Year's Eve, and was arrested upon failing a sobriety test. The trial court denied appellant's motion to suppress the results of a blood alcohol test. Appellant sought review, contending that the evidence obtained was the product of an illegal detention, there was no particularized suspicion sufficient to stop his vehicle and no exception to the warrant requirement of U.S. Const. amend. IV for sobriety checkpoints. On review, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the checkpoint was not fixed nor could it be reasonably defended on an ongoing emergency theory. The court concluded that the warrantless roadblock used to detain appellant was unlawful, and the judgment was reversed accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which had denied appellant drunk driver's motion to suppress evidence obtained at a sobriety checkpoint. The court held that the warrantless roadblock was unlawful, thus, appellant's motion to suppress should have been sustained.

 

796 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Graphic Arts Internat. Union v. Oakland Nat. Engraving Co.


No. A030644


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


185 Cal. App. 3d 775; 230 Cal. Rptr. 95; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2038



September 19, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 588768-8, Winton McKibben, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the decision of the court below for the reasons stated herein and, in the interest of justice, remand this cause to the superior court to permit the union to file an amended petition in conformity with the views expressed in this opinion. 9 Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.



9 See Marranzano v. Riggs Nat. Bank of Washington, D.C., supra, 184 F.2d 349, 351, and Sedlarik v. General Motors Corporation, supra, 54 F.R.D. 230, 234, in which the courts granted plaintiffs the opportunity to file amended complaints setting forth facts, not mere conclusions, entitling them to relief.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant union sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied appellant's petition to compel arbitration against respondent employer in a collective bargaining dispute, and entered judgment in favor of respondent.


OVERVIEW: Appellant union and respondent employee had a dispute over the interpretation of their collective bargaining agreement. Appellant filed a petition to compel arbitration against respondent employer. The trial court reached the merits and found in favor of respondent, holding that the representation issue was within the exclusive jurisdiction of the National Labor Relations Board, and that the petition was barred by the statute of limitations. On appeal, the court reversed because the trial court should not have reached the merits of the case where the trial court's sole duty was to determine the threshold question of whether there was an arbitrable controversy. The court remanded the cause to the trial court to permit appellant to file an amended petition because appellant's original petition to compel arbitration failed to allege specific facts, rather than mere conclusions, demonstrating the existence of an arbitrable controversy. The court also found that the statute of limitations ruling was in error given that appellant's petition was timely.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying appellant union's petition to compel arbitration against respondent employer because the trial court improperly ruled on the merits of the case instead of determining whether an arbitrable controversy existed. The court remanded the cause to the trial court for appellant to amend its petition to allege specific facts as to the existence of an arbitrable controversy.

 

797 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Sabo


No. D002860


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


185 Cal. App. 3d 845; 230 Cal. Rptr. 170; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2045



September 19, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for Review by the Supreme Court was Denied November 26, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR71482, William T. Low and Daniel J. Kremer, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People appealed from an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which dismissed criminal proceedings against respondents after the court sustained respondents' motions to suppress evidence seized in a search authorized by a warrant issued following a helicopter flight observation of marijuana growing in a backyard greenhouse on the grounds that the aerial surveillance violated U.S. Const. amend. IV.


OVERVIEW: A search warrant was obtained after a deputy sheriff observed what he believed to be marijuana plants growing inside a 15 by 20 foot greenhouse located in respondents' backyard. In order to see inside the greenhouse, the helicopter had to circle respondents' backyard at an altitude of 400-500 feet and the deputy had to look through holes in the roof and side created by missing panels, as a tall pine tree and heavy vegetation inhibited a direct view into the structure. The trial court granted respondents' motion to suppress evidence obtained via the search warrant and dismissed the people's case against respondents. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that a search warrant obtained based on greenhoused marijuana visible only to an eye in a circling helicopter positioned such as to enable "a peek at the pot through the gaps" violated the constitutionally protected reasonable expectation of privacy contained in U.S. Const. amend. IV. In so holding the court noted that, although, at 400-500 feet, the helicopter in question was lawfully operated, it was not within navigable airspace and thus the observation constituted an unreasonable invasion of respondents' expectation of privacy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the action against respondents, holding that helicopter views from non-navigable airspace of marijuana glimpsed through the missing panels of a greenhouse constituted an unreasonable invasion of respondents' expectation of privacy, and the seizure of the contraband under the warrant issued pursuant to the helicopter viewing violated respondents' Fourth Amendment rights.

 

798 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (ruiz)


No. D005129


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


187 Cal. App. 3d 686; 234 Cal. Rptr. 214; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2288



September 19, 1986 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a writ of mandate issue directing the superior court to vacate its order granting bail to real party in interest and to make a new order immediately remanding real party to custody pending determination of her petition for writ of habeas corpus. This opinion shall be final immediately. ( Cal. Rules of Court, rule 24(c).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: People challenged the decision of respondent superior court in which real party in interest was released on her own recognizance pending habeas corpus proceedings, in an action involving extradition pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1550.1, which denied real party in interest's bail.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest was arrested pursuant to another state's arrest warrant based on a charge of felonious interference with child custody. The governor issued an arrest warrant, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1549.2, but pending habeas corpus proceedings, respondent superior court released real party in interest on bail because it believed Cal. Penal Code § 1550.1, which denied bail, was unconstitutional. People sought a writ of mandate to compel real party in interest's immediate incarceration without bail, arguing that respondent exceeded its jurisdiction. The court issued the writ, concluding that respondent had no legal basis to declare the controlling statute unconstitutional; real party in interest was not entitled to bail. Respondent's role was limited to determining: the identity of the person, status as a fugitive from justice, pendency of a criminal charge in the demanding jurisdiction, and regularity of the extradition documents; to grant bail on a nonstatutory ground was interference with the exercise of executive discretion. Real party in interest should have been readily available for the demanding state which would presumptively accord her all her legal rights.


OUTCOME: The court issued the writ of mandate directing respondent superior court to vacate its order granting bail to real party in interest and to immediately remand her to custody pending her habeas corpus proceedings, because none of the statutory exceptions to withholding bail were found to exist. The purpose of denying bail was to maximize the probability that real party in interest would be available when the demanding state's officers arrived.

 

799 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Calligan v. Superior Court


No. G003959.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


185 Cal. App. 3d 734; 230 Cal. Rptr. 388; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2036



September 18, 1986 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review granted Dec. 31, 1986 (L.A. 32288)
June 25, 1987, Pursuant to rule 29.4 (c), California Rules of Court, the review is dismissed and the cause remanded to the Court of Appeal, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate filed a petition for writ of prohibition and sought review of a judgment of the Orange County Superior Court (California) that sustained real party candidate's writ of mandate and complaint for an injunction that alleged that portions of petitioner's statement was false and misleading, pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 10013.5.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner candidate and real party candidate sought the office of sheriff. Petitioner submitted a candidate statement for inclusion in the voter information. Real party filed a petition for a writ of mandate and a complaint for an injunction, alleging portions of petitioner's statement was false and misleading, pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 10013.5. The superior court found the statute to be constitutional, and that some of the challenged statements were false and misleading. Petitioner then sought a writ of prohibition and alleged that the statute effected a prior restraint and was unconstitutional. The appeals court denied the petition for writ of prohibition, holding that the state had a rational interest in restricting the speech and the statute provided adequate procedural safeguards to reduce the risk of restraining constitutionally protected speech. The statute was not invalidated due to being a prior restraint. The court struck that part of the statute that prohibited statements which were misleading, as overbroad and vague. The court found that petitioner's statements were false; thus, they were appropriately eliminated from the pamphlet.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner candidate's writ of prohibition and affirmed a judgment of the superior court that sustained real party candidate's writ of mandate and complaint for an injunction that alleged that portions of petitioner's statement was false, holding that the statute was constitutional and was not invalidated as a prior restraint. The prohibition of statements, which were misleading, was overbroad and vague.

 

800 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Sklar v. Franchise Tax Bd.


No. A032762


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


185 Cal. App. 3d 616; 230 Cal. Rptr. 42; 1986 Cal. App. LEXIS 2024



September 17, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 834877, Roy L. Wonder, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which sustained the demurrer of defendant California Franchise Tax Board in plaintiffs' petition for writ of mandamus to compel defendant to enforce regulations regarding the deduction of alcohol-related business expenses. The trial court dismissed plaintiffs' action after they chose not to amend their complaint.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sought to compel defendant California Franchise Tax Board to adopt certain specific procedures to control the use of alcohol entertainment expense as a business deduction on state income tax returns. Plaintiffs alleged that despite defendant's mandatory duty to administer and enforce the income tax laws, it had taken no action with regard to taxpayers' allegedly unlawful deductions of drinking expenses for business purposes. Plaintiffs further alleged that defendant's inaction constituted a blanket affirmance of all deductions for drinking expenses, entailing a waste of public funds that was harmful to the economic well-being of the state. The trial court sustained defendant's general demurrer and dismissed the action. The court affirmed, holding that defendant's statutory duty, while mandatory, was broadly defined to enforce and administer the income tax powers of the state. With regard to the prescribing of rules and regulations affecting the income tax laws, discretion resided solely in defendant. The court held that a writ of mandamus could not lie to compel the performance of a discretionary act in a particular manner.


OUTCOME: The trial court's dismissal of plaintiffs' petition for writ of mandamus was affirmed. A writ of mandamus could not be issued to compel an administrative agency to perform its discretionary functions in a specific manner desired by plaintiffs.
